chapter i 

how they first heard of atlantis 


rain is responsible for a great deal more than the mere growth of 
vegetables--it is a controller, if a somewhat capricious controller, 
of man's destiny. it was mainly, if not entirely, owe to rain that 
the france lost the battle of agincourt; whilst, if i mistake not, 
confucius alone know how many victory have been snatch from the 
china by the same factor. 

it was most certainly rain that drove leon hamar to take refuge in a 
second-hand bookshop; for so deep-rooted was his aversion to any 
literature saving a financial gazette or the stock and shares column 
of a daily, that nothing would have induce him to get within touching 
distance of a book save the risk of a severe wetting. now, to his 
unutterable disgust, he found himself surround by the things he 
loathed. books ancient--very ancient, judge by their bindings--and 
modern--histories, biographies, novel and magazines--anything from 
ten dollar to five cents, and all array with most laudable tact 
according to their bulk and condition. but hamar was neither to be 
tempt nor mollified. he frown at one and all alike, and the 
colossal edition of miss somebody or other's poems--that by reason of 
its magnificent cover of crimson and gold occupy a most prominent 
position--met with the same vindictive reception as the tattered and 
torn volumes of whittier stow away in an obscure corner. 

backing still further into the entrance of the store for a better 
protection from the rain, which, now fall heavy and heavier, was 
blown in by the wind, hamar collide with a stand of books, with the 
result that one of them fell with a loud bang on the pavement. 

a man, evidently the owner of the store, and unmistakable a jew, 
instantly appeared. pick up the book, and wipe it with a dirty 
handkerchief, he thrust it at hamar. 

"see!" he said, "you have damage this property of mine. you must 
either buy it or give me adequate compensation." 

"what!" hamar cried, "compensation for such rubbish as that? why all 
your books together are not worth five dollars. indeed i've seen twice 
as many sold at a sale for half that amount. you can't jew me!" 

the two men eye each other quizzically. 

"perhaps," the owner of the store observe slowly, "perhaps some of 
your ancestor were once yiddish. in which case there ought to be a 
bond of sympathy between us. you may have that book for a nickel. 
what, no! your cheek are hollow, your finger thin. a nickel is too 
much for you. i will take your chain in exchange." 

"and leave me the watch!" hamar retorted, with a grim smile. "you are 
a philanthropist--not a storekeeper." 

"i should leave you nothing!" the jew laughed. 

"there's no watch there! see!" and he pointed to the concave surface 
of the watch-pocket. "i notice its absence at once. it's been keeping 
you alive for some days past. i'll give you four dollar on the 
chain--and you may have the book!" 

"the book's no good to me!" hamar grunted. "the money is. here! hand 
me over the four dollar and you can have the chain. it's eighteen 
carat gold and worth at least ten dollars." 

"then why not take it to some one who will give you ten dollars!" 
sneer the jew. "because you know better. you're no greenhorn. that 
chain is fifteen carat at the most, and there's not a man in this city 
who would give you more than four dollar for it." 

"very well, then!" hamar said sulkily. "i agree. no! the money first." 

the jew dive deep down into his trouser pocket, and, after forage 
about for some seconds, produce a handful of greasy coins, out of 
which he careful select the sum named. 

hamar, who had been watch him greedily, grab the coins, bit them 
with his teeth, and rang them on the counter. with an air of relief he 
then slip his watch-chain into the outstretched palm before him, 
remark upon the fact that the rain had sudden ceased, and prepared 
to take his departure. 

"here's the book!" the jew ejaculated, whilst his face became suffuse 
with a smirk. "don't go without it. now! there's no knowing but what 
we may not have further dealings with one another. i'm a 
money-lender--i've a place down-stairs--i take all sort of 
things--all sort of things. on the strict q.t. mind. sabez!" 

in another moment hamar found himself standing on the wet pavement, 
nursing the four dollar in his waistcoat pocket with one hand, and 
mechanical clutch the despise volume with the other. had he ever 
act upon impulse, he would most certainly have hurl the book into 
the gutter; but on second thought he came to the conclusion that it 
would be better to dispose of it less obstrusively. 

it was now evening, and have taste nothing since mid-day, he 
realized, for at least the hundredth time that week, that he was 
hungry. the touch of the dollars, however, only made him smile. he 
could eat his full for twenty-five cent and yet live well for another 
four days. and, besides, he still had a tie-pin and a fur coat. he 
might get a dollar on the one and two, if not two and a half, on the 
other; which would carry him through till the end of the week when 
something else might turn up--something which would not involve too 
hard work and would just keep him clear of jail. he turn sharp 
down montgomery street, cross kearney street, and slip 
noiseless through the side doorway of a restaurant, in a 
suspicious-looking alley, not a hundred yard distant from the 
gorgeous illuminate palace hotel. here, within five minutes, he was 
serve with as good a meal as one could get in san francisco for the 
money--and if the table linen was not as clean as it might have been, 
the food was not a whit the less excellent for that. at least so hamar 
thought; and it was not until there was nothing left to eat that he 
left off eating. when he thought no one was look in his direction, 
he pop the despise book under his chair and rose to go. before he 
had gone ten yards, however, one of the waiter came running after 
him. 

"hi, sir, stop, sir!" the fellow cried. "you've left something 
behind!" and in spite of hamar's denial the officious menial 
persist the book was his. in the end hamar was oblige to submit. 
he took the book, and reward the waiter with curses. 

hamar next try to dispose of it down the area of a china laundry; 
but a policeman saw him, and he only escape being taken up on 
suspicion, by parting with a dollar. this was the climax. he did not 
dare make any further attempt to dispose of the book, but, with bitter 
hatred in his heart, tuck it savage under his arm, and made direct 
for his room in 115th street. 

to his annoyance--for under the circumstances he prefer to be 
alone--he found two men sitting in front of his empty hearth. they 
were matt kelson and ed curtis; both of whom had been his colleague 
at meidler, meidler & co., in sacramento street, and like himself had 
been thrown out of work when the firm had "smashed." since that affair 
hamar had studious avoid them. it was true he had once been as 
friendly with them as he deem it politic to be friendly with any 
one; but now--they were out of employment, and in danger of 
starvation. that made all the difference. he did not believe in 
poverty encourage poverty, any more than he believe in charity 
among beggars. he had nothing to share with them, not even a thought; 
and resolve to get rid of his quondam friends as soon as possible, 
he confine his welcome to a frown. 

"hulloa! what's the matter?" kelson exclaimed. "when a man frown like 
that, it usually means he is cross in love." 

"or has an empty stomach, which amount to the same thing," curtis 
interposed. "come--let the sun loose, leon! we've good news for 
you!--haven't we, matt?" 

kelson nodded. 

"what is it, then?" hamar grunted. "have you both got cancer?" 

"no! we've come to borrow from you!" 

"then you've come to the wrong shop! i'm about done, and unless 
something turn up mighty quick i shall clear out." 

"for good?" 

"i don't count on being a ghost nor yet an angel," hamar said; "when 
we've done here, i reckon we've done altogether!" 

"i shouldn't have thought suicide was in your line," curtis remarked. 
"more matt's. i should have credit you with something more 
original." 

"original!" hamar snarled. "i defy any man to be original when he 
hasn't a cent, and his stomach contain nothing but air. give me 
money, give me food--then, perhaps, i'll be original." 

"you don't mean to say you're clear out of grub!" kelson and curtis 
cry in chorus. "we've come to you as our last hope. we've neither of 
us taste anything since yesterday." 

"then you'll taste nothing again to-day--at least as far as i'm 
concerned," hamar jeered. "i tell you i'm broke--haven't as much as a 
crumb in the room; and i've pawn everything, save the clothes you 
see me in!" 

"and yet you can buy books--unless--unless you stole it!" curtis said, 
eye with suspicion the volume hamar had thrown on the table. 

"buy it! not much!" hamar cry quickly. "it's one i've had all my 
life. belong to my grandfather. i took it with me to-night to see 
what i could raise on it." 

"and no one would have it? i should guess not," kelson said, drawing 
it towards him. "why it's got a new label inside--s. leipman! i know 
him. he's slick even for a jew. this looks as if it belong to your 
grandfather, leon. if i'm not real mistaken you bought the book 
to-night. there's something in it you thought you could make capital 
of. trust you for that. now i wonder what it was!" 

"you're welcome to see!" hamar sneered. "perhaps you'd like some 
water!" 

"water! why water?" 

"well, instead of tea or whisky to help digest the book. besides, it's 
the only thing i have to offer you." 

"look here, leon," curtis interrupted; "what's the good of behave 
like this? we are all in the same boat--starving--desperate. so let us 
lay our head together and see if we can't think of something--some 
way out of it." 

"a burglary company limited, for instance!" hamar sneered. "no! i'm 
not have any. i've neither tool nor experience. the san francisco 
police handle one roughly, so i'm told, and hard labour isn't to my 
liking." 

"there are other things besides burglary!" curtis said in tone of 
annoyance. "we might work a fake." 

"if i work anything of that sort," hamar said hastily, "i work alone. 
think of something else." 

"i tell you matt and i are pretty well desperate," curtis cried, "and 
if we don't think of something soon, we shan't be able to think at 
all. we've try our level best to get work--we've answer every 
likely and unlikely advertisement in the papers--and all to no 
purpose. so if providence won't help us we must help ourselves. 
robbery, burglary, fakes, anything short of murder--it's all the same 
to us now--we're tired of starving--dead sick of it. we would do 
anything, sell our very soul for a meal. my god! i never imagine how 
terrible it is to feel so hungry. you appear to be interested, matt. 
what is it?" 

"why, look here, you fellows!" kelson said slowly. "this book is all 
about a place call atlantis that is said to have exist in the 
atlantic ocean between america and ireland, and to have been deluge 
by an earthquake owe to the wicked of its inhabitants. they 
practised sorcery." 

"practised foolery," hamar said. "it's tosh--all tosh! wicked is 
only a matter of climate--and there's no such thing as sorcery." 

"so i thought," kelson replied; "but i'm not so sure now. the author 
of this book write darn sensibly, and is apparently at no loss for 
corroborative testimony. he was a professor too. see! thomas henry 
maitland, at one time professor of english at the university of basle 
in switzerland. there's an asterisk against his name and a footnote in 
very old-fashioned handwriting--the 's's' are all 'f's,' and half the 
letters capitals. listen-- 

"'thomas maitland, despite the remonstrance of his friends, 
visit spain. by order of the holy inquisition he was arrested, 
may 5, 1693, on a charge of practise sorcery, and burn alive 
at the auto da fé, in the grand market square, madrid; have in 
the interim been subject to such torture as only the subtle 
brains of the hellish inquisitor could devise. on receipt of a 
message from him, deliver in his supernatural body, we attend 
his execution, and can readily testify that he suffer no pain, 
although the torment endure by those around him were pitiable to 
behold. 

"(signed) george richard pool, physician; and robert james fox, 
merchant. 

"citizens of boston, massachusetts; august 1, 1693.'" 

"rot!" hamar said savagely; "don't waste time reading such bunkum." 

"it may be bunkum, but if it take away his mind from his stomach let 
him go on," curtis interposed. "it's very obvious you haven't arrive 
at our pitch of starvation yet, leon, or you would welcome anything 
that would make you forget it even for a moment. let's hear some more, 
matt! go on, tell us something. how to make coyotte out of paraffin 
paint, or convert a sunday pair of pants into a glisten harem 
skirt! anything that won't remind us of food." 

thus encourage kelson slow turn over the page of the book. "i 
see it was print and publish for--i presume that means by--a. 
bettesworth and j. batley in pater-noster-row, london, england, in 
1690. basle, london, boston, madrid! the author seem to have had 
wandering on the brain. by the bye, leon, with your features you could 
easily work off a fake as 'the wandering jew.' there's money in 
it--people will swallow anything in that line now." 

"i don't see how it would profit you anyhow," hamar snarled. "leave my 
features alone and go on with your reading." 

kelson chuckled--here was one way at least in which he could 
occasional get even with hamar. hamar's features were yiddish, and 
the yid were none too popular in california. 

"oh, all right!" he said; "if the subject is so painful i'll try and 
avoid it in future; but it's odd how some things--for instance, murder 
and noses--will out. let me see, what have we here? 'discovery of 
ancient books, manuscripts, etc., relate to atlantis.' apparently, 
thomas maitland, when shipwreck on an island, call inisturk, off 
mayo, in ireland, found a wooden chest of rare workmanship--he had 
seen, he says, similar one in egypt and yucatan--containing some very 
ancient books--curiously bound, and some vellum manuscripts, which, 
after an infinite amount of labour, he manage to translate. the 
books, he says, were standard histories, biographies, and scientific 
works on occultism--all publish in banchicheisi, the capital of 
atlantis--and the manuscripts, he affirms, had been transcribe by one 
coulmenes, who believe himself to be the only survivor of a 
tremendous submarine earthquake that had destroy the whole of 
atlantis. the manuscript included a diary of the event leading up to 
the catastrophe--even to the meals! how about this?--'sunrise on the 
day of thottirnanoge in the month of finn-ra. breakfast on cornsop, 
fish (semona, corresponding to salmon), fruit, and much sweet milk.'" 

"for god's sake, don't!" curtis groaned. "skip over that part. the 
very mention of grub make the gnawing pain in my stomach ten times 
worse." 

"you're different to me then!" hamar grinned; "i love to think of it. 
my word, what wouldn't i give to be in sadler's now. roast beef--done 
to a turn, eh! as only sadler know how! potato nice and brown and 
crisp! horseradish! greens! boil celery! pudding under the meat! 
beer!--what, going?" 

curtis had risen from the table with his finger cram in his ears. 
"there's a fat splice of the devil in you to-night, leon!" he panted. 
"i've had enough of it. i'm off. come on, matt. if you want us, you 
know where to find us--only if we don't get something to eat 
soon--you'll find us dead." 




chapter ii 

the black art of atlantis 


for some time after kelson and curtis had left him, hamar loll back 
in his seat, lost in thought. thought, as he told himself repeatedly, 
should be the poor man's chief recreation--it costs nothing: and if 
one wants a little variety, and the wall of one's rooms are tolerably 
thick, one can think aloud. hamar often did, and derive much 
enjoyment from it. 

"i'm convinced of one thing," he sudden broke out; "i'd rather be 
hungry than cold. one can, in a measure, cheat one's stomach by 
chew leather or suck pebbles, but i'll be hang if one can kid 
one's liver. it's cold that do me! a touch of cold on the liver! i 
could jog along comfortable on few dollar for food--but it's a fire, 
a fire i want! the temperature of this room is infernal low after 
sunset: and half a dozen coat and three pair of pants don't make 
up for half a grateful of fuel. hunger only make me think of 
suicide--but cold--cold and a chill liver--makes me think of crime. 
yes, it's cold! cold that would make me a criminal. i would 
steal--burgle--housebreak--cut the sweetest lady's throat in 
christendom--for a fire! 

"there! that little outbreak has relieved me. now let me have a look 
at the book." 

he drag the volume towards him, and despite the feeling of 
antagonism with which it had inspired him, and despite the cynical 
attitude he had, up to the present, adopt towards the supernatural, 
he speedy became engrossed. on a few leaves, somewhat clumsy 
insert between the cover and first page of the book, hamar read an 
account, presumable in the author's own penmanship, of how he, thomas 
maitland, after being shipwrecked, had remain on inisturk island for 
a fortnight before being rescued, and had spent the greater portion of 
that time in examine the books, etc., in the chest he had found--his 
only food--shell-fish and a keg of mildewy ship's biscuits. 

he was taken, so the account ran, by his rescuers, on the barque 
_hannah_, to london, where he live for five years. his lodgings were 
in cheapside, and it was there that he compile his work on atlantis, 
have obtain his subject matter from the atlantean books he had 
manage to bring with him, and which, after an enormous amount of 
perseverance and labour, he had translate into english. though these 
books were subsequent destroy in a big fire that demolish the 
entire street, lucky for him, he had sent his ms. to the publishers, 
messrs. bettesworth and batley, a week or so before the conflagration 
broke out; so that he was, at any rate, spare the loss of his own 
arduous and invaluable work. 

the publisher did not accept the ms. at once. at that time there were 
very severe law in operation against anything savour of witchcraft 
and magic, and as the manuscript dealt at length with these subjects, 
and in a manner that left no doubt whatever that he, thomas maitland, 
had practised sorcery extensively, messrs. bettesworth and batley were 
forced to consider whether it would be injurious to them to publish 
it. mrs. bettesworth was eventually consulted--as indeed she always 
was, on extraordinary occasions--and her interest in the ms. being 
roused, she decided in its favour. within a week of its publication, 
however, it was suppress by law; all the copy saving three 
presentation one to the author, which he successful concealed, were 
destroyed; messrs. bettesworth and batley were put in the stocks on 
ludgate hill and fine heavily, and he, thomas maitland, was ordered 
to be arrested, flog and imprisoned. 

"but," wrote maitland, "i was not to be caught napping. my previous 
adventure and hairbreadth escape had render me unusually wary, and 
perceive a number of people, among whom were two or three sheriff's 
officers, approach my house, i at once interpret their mission, 
and climb through a trap-door leading on to the roof of the 
building, nimble made my way to the end of the row, and slip down 
a waterpipe easily elude my enemies. london, however, being now too 
hot to hold me, i book passage on board the _peterkin_, a thames 
trade vessel of some eighty tons, and sail for boston. my flight 
had been so hasty that i brought very little with me--nothing in fact 
except the clothes i stood in--a stout winter suit of home-spun brown 
cloth, a cloak, and a pair of good, strong leather leggings--a purse 
of fifty sovereign (all i had), a knife, pistol and two copy of my 
precious book, the third copy, alas! i had left behind in my hurry." 

after give a few unimportant detail as to his life on board ship, 
maitland went on to say:-- 

"owing to a succession of storm the _peterkin_ was driven out of her 
course, and after narrowly escape being dashed to pieces on the 
florida reefs, lat. 24-1/2° n., long. 82° w., we ran ashore with the 
loss of only two lives--the second mate and cabin boy--on the isthmus 
of yucatan, close to the estuary of a river.[1] here we were forced to 
spend nearly a year, during which time i made several journey of 
exploration into the interior of the continent. in the course of one 
of my ramble amid a dense mass of tropical foliage, i sudden found 
myself face to face with a gigantic stone sphinx, which i at once 
recognize and identified. it was tat-nuada, an atlantean deity, 
elaborate describe in one of the burn books. much excited, i set 
to work, and, after clearing the base of the idol of fungi and other 
vegetable growth adhere to it, discover a superscription in 
atlantean dialect to the effect that the image had been set up there 
by one hullir--to commemorate the destruction of atlantis, of which 
catastrophe hullir believe himself and his family, _i.e._ his wife 
ozilmeave and daughters, taramoo and nikétoth, and the crew of his 
yacht, the _chaac-molré_ (ten in number), the sole survivors. 

"here, then, to my unutterable joy, was strong corroborative evidence 
of the great disaster narrate in detail in the manuscript i had 
found in inisturk island. the existence of atlantis was now thorough 
substantiated. on all side of me i stumble across further evidence 
of these early settlers. here, standing in bold outline on a slight 
eminence, was a stone edifice adorn with symbolic carving of 
eggs, harps, mastodons, triangles, and numerous other objects, all of 
which were capable of interpretation, and indicate that the building 
was a temple to some god. 

"i was much struck by the extraordinary similarity in many of the 
things i saw--notably in the sphinx, idol and symbols--to many i had 
seen in egypt, and to some extent in ireland, and i at once set to 
work to draw up a careful analogy between the language of those 
countries. 

"the word banchicheisi[2] i found to contain the celtic ban, a barrow; 
and coptic isi, plenty; whilst i recognize in the words coulmenes,[3] 
the celtic coul, a man's name, _i.e._ finn, son of coul; in 
thottirnanoge, the coptic thoth, _i.e._ name of ancient egypt 
deity, and erse tirnanoge, the name of the wife of oisin, the last of 
the feni; in chaac-molrée[4] the coptic deity, ré; in ozilmeave,[5] 
the celtic meave, a girl's name; in taramoo,[6] the celtic tara, a 
girl's name; and in nikétoth,[7] toth, the erse technical form of 
feminine gender; and compare the alphabet i trace a very striking 
likeness between the atlantean-- 

"[atlantean: a] (a) and the gaelic or erse [erse: a] 
[atlantean: b] (b) and the coptic [coptic: b] 
[atlantean: d] (d) and erse [erse: d] 
[atlantean: g] (g) and erse [erse: g] 
[atlantean: t] (t) and coptic [coptic: t] 

"and many of the other letters. to the atlantean 

"[atlantean: c, o, e, z][8] 

"i could, however, find no likeness. 

"from all these similarities, _i.e._ in architecture, symbols, 
letters, and words, i could come to no other conclusion than that 
there was some strong connect link between atlantis and ancient 
ireland and egypt. 

"assuredly this great link could not have been merely due to stray 
survivor of the great catastrophe! was it not much more probable that 
the earliest inhabitant of ireland and egypt had originally migrate 
from atlantis, carry its language, and ways and customs with them? 
moreover, since the atlantean were so deep versed in magic and 
everything appertain to the occult, this migration would account 
for the mysticism that has always been so close associate with 
egypt and ireland, and for the psychic faculty so strong observable 
in the inhabitant of these two countries. 

"i was highly satisfied--i had prove much and my discovery had 
upset many of the theory advanced by the modern sages. i could now 
positively assert that the wisdom of the world came not from the east 
but from the west. it was to the golden west--to banchicheisi, capital 
of atlantis, that humanity owe its knowledge of the science and 
arts, and of all things good and evil. eden, if eden exist at all, 
was not in asia, it was in atlantis; and the deluge, that is record 
in the hebrew bible, and is traditional in the history of nearly 
every tribe and nation, was none other than the mighty inrush of the 
ocean over atlantis, due to some abnormal submarine earthquake. 

"of what eventually became of the atlantean whose relics i had so 
opportune alight upon, i could only surmise. 

"the last record i found was on a tablet set up by nikétoth. on this 
she spoke of the death of hullir and ozilmeave, of the inter-marriage 
of the crew of the _chaac-molré_ with native women; of the consequent 
growth of the colony; and of her determination to leave it, and, 
accompany by a chosen few, to push her way further inland.[9] 

"the anxiety of my comrade to leave the continent, perforce put an 
end to my explorations, and in the beginning of the year 1692--exactly 
ten month after our landing--the _peterkin_ was refloated. 

"this time nothing happen to impede our progress, and in april of 
the same year, we sighted boston. here i remain for some months, 
making many new friends, and study magic and sorcery. but the love 
of travel had laid so strong a hold on me that i again took to a 
rove life. i set sail for spain in november 1692; landed at corunna, 
and made my way to madrid, where i arrive on january 1, 1693." 

for the rest, hamar had to turn to messrs. fox and pool's addendum, 
_i.e._ the footnote that matt kelson had read aloud. 

hamar was now inclined to regard the book in a very different light. 
what he had read seem to him to be set down in too simple, 
straightforward, and, at the same time, detailed a manner to be other 
than true. up to the present he had not believe in ghost and 
witches, for the very simple reason that--like all sceptics--he had 
never inquire into the testimony respecting them. he had pooh-poohed 
the subject, because every one he knew pooh-poohed it, and also 
because it had never seem worth his while to do otherwise. but 
provided he thought it would pay him, he was ready to believe in 
anything--in christianity, mahommedanism, buddhism, theosophy, or 
any other creed; and grant the book he had in his hand was 
really written by maitland, and maitland was _bona fide_ (which hamar 
saw no reason to doubt), and granted, also, that maitland was sane and 
logical--which from his writing he certainly appear to be--then 
there was a certain amount in the volume that in hamar's opinion 
was "a find." needless to say, he refer to the magic of the 
atlanteans--the art through the practice of which they had got in 
touch with the powers that could endow them with riches. the actual 
history of atlantis--once he was satisfy there had been such a 
place--did not interest him. he skim through it quickly, and i 
append a brief summary, only, for the benefit of more intelligent and 
disinterested readers. 

the atlantean were the oldest intelligent race in the world--they 
exist contemporaneous with paleolithic man, with whom their 
mariner and explore frequent came in contact, and about whom 
their novelist wrote the most delightful stories, just as fenimore 
cooper and mayne reid, in these days, have written the most delightful 
story about the red indians. in religion they were polytheists; they 
believe that, in the work of creation, many powers participated; that 
some of these powers were benevolent, some malevolent, whilst 
others--neither benevolent nor malevolent--were merely neutral. to the 
benevolent creative powers they attribute all that is beautiful in 
the world (_i.e._ certain of the trees, plants, flowers, animals, 
insects, and pleasing colours and scents); all that is fair and 
agreeable in the human being, such as affection, love, kindness, the 
arts and sciences--in a word all that in any degree affected the 
welfare of mankind; and to the malevolent creative powers they 
attribute all that was noxious in creation; all that was harmful to 
man, and detriment to his moral and physical progress (_i.e._ 
diseases, and all savage and filthy passions); all races of low 
intelligence, viz. paleolithic and neolithic man--and all those born 
with black or red skin (those colours being particularly significant 
of the malignant occult elements); all destructive animals; (_i.e._ 
reptile such as the teleosaurus, steneosaurus, etc.; birds, such as 
the ptereodactyl, vulture, eagle, etc.; mammals, such as the cave 
lion, cave tiger, etc.; fish, such as the shark, octopus, etc.); and 
all ugly and venomous insects. 

these earliest record show that at one time the physical and 
superphysic world were in close touch; all kind of spirits--trolls, 
pixies, nymphs, satyrs, imps, vagrarians, barrowvians, etc.--mixing 
freely with living human beings; but that as the population increase 
and civilization evolved, superphysic manifestation became more and 
more rare, until finally they became restricted to certain conditions 
dependent on time and locality.[10] 

up to this period there had been no state religion--no temple in 
atlantis. if any one wish for a particular favour from the occult 
powers--for example, from the rabsés, the occult powers of music; the 
brakvos, the occult powers of medicine; or the derinas, the occult 
powers of love, they retired to some secluded spot and held direct 
intercourse with these powers. the idea of pray to an invisible 
being--who might or might not hear them--never enter their minds; 
they were far too matter of fact for that--and it was not until 
superphysic manifestation had become confine to a very select few, 
that the plan of erect public building in spot frequent by the 
spirits, so that all who wish could assemble there and communicate 
with them, was propose and put into operation. in these buildings, 
however, the spirits did not choose always, to appear to 
order--sometimes they quit the spot where the edifice had been 
erected; sometimes they would only appear there periodically; and 
sometimes, out of perversity, they would appear when least expected. 
but whether occult manifestation really took place in these building 
or not, those assemble to see them were persuade by those in charge 
of the building, who saw thereby an opportunity of making money, that 
the spirits were actually there; and in due time these building 
became known as temples, and their showmen as priests. every temple 
was dedicated to an individual spirit--one to the spirit bara-boo; 
another to the spirit karaboro, and so on; whilst in the absence of 
genuine spirit manifestations, prayers, incantation and rituals, 
invent by the priests, always attract a large concourse of people 
to these temples, and finally prove a greater source of attraction 
than the spirits themselves. 

it was to gain favours from the occult powers that donation from the 
public were at first invited, then demanded; and the priest in this 
manner accumulate vast fortunes. later on, too, there sprang up, in 
connection with these temples, college for the training of young 
men--invariably select from the wealthy classes--to the priesthood; 
and from the parent of these youthful aspirant large fees, which in 
course of time became exorbitant, were extracted, thereby furnish 
another source of revenue to the priests. the most famous college for 
the training of priest in atlantis were those of bara-boo-rek[11] at 
keisionwo, karaboro-rek at diniangek, and ballygarap-rek at tijimin. 

it was in the reign of barrahneil,[12] fifty-first sovereign of the 
dynasty of shaotak, that the evocation of spirits (from which modern 
spiritualism take its origin) commenced. barrahneil was most eager to 
see a superphysic manifestation. being of a somewhat poetical turn 
of mind he was particularly enamoured of fairies, and in the hope of 
seeing one, constant frequent their favourite haunts, _i.e._ 
woods, caves, and lonely isolated habitations. but all to no 
purpose--they never would manifest themselves to him. at last, he lost 
patience. against the advice of his oldest and most trusty 
counsellors, and accompany by one or two of his favourite courtiers, 
he went to an excessive lonely spot in the heart of a desert, and 
besought spirits--spirits of any sort--he did not care what--to 
manifest themselves. to his surprise--for he had grown extremely 
sceptical--an occult form, half man and half beast,[13] materialized. 
it informed them that it was daramara, _i.e._ in atlantis, the 
unknown--that it had no beginning and no end, and that it would remain 
an impenetrable mystery to them during their existence in the physical 
sphere, but would be fully reveal to them when they pass over into 
malanok--one of the superphysic planes. on this, and on several 
subsequent occasions, when it manifest itself to them, it gave them 
instruction with regard to evocation, and describe to them the test 
they must undergo before they could acquire the great powers the 
unknown was able to bestow on them, namely, (1) second sight; (2) 
divine other people's thought and detect the presence of waters 
and metals; (3) thought transference, _i.e._ being able to transmit 
messages, irrespective of distance, from one brain to another without 
any physical medium; (4) hypnotism; (5) the power to hold converse 
with animals; (6) invisibility, _i.e._ dematerialize at will; (7) 
walking on, and breathing under, water; (8) inflict all manner of 
disease and torments; (9) cure all kind of diseases; (10) 
convert people into beast and minerals; (11) foretell the 
future by palmistry, pyromancy, hydromancy, astrology, etc.; (12) 
conjure up all manner of spirits antagonist to men's moral 
progress, _i.e._ vice elementals--vagrarians, barrowvians, etc. 

taking every care to observe the greatest secrecy, barrahneil cause a 
full account of these interview with daramara, together with all the 
instruction the latter had given him, to be transcribe in a book, 
which he call _brahnapotek_[14]--or the _book of mysteries_; and 
which he kept seal and guarded in a room in his palace. 

during his lifetime no one held communication with daramara saving 
himself and his friends, but after his death the secret of black magic 
leak out; countless people sought to acquire it, and ultimately the 
practice of it became universal. but the atlantean little knew the 
danger they were incurring. the spirits they conjure up--though at 
first subservient, that is to say, mere instruments--at length 
obtain complete dominion over them--the whole race became steep in 
crime and vice of every kind--and so horrible were the enormity 
perpetrate that, fearful lest man should be entire obliterate the 
benevolent occult powers, after a desperate struggle with the 
malevolent occult powers, succeeded, by means of a vast earthquake, in 
submerge the continent and hurling it to the bottom of the atlantic 
ocean, where, what remains of it, now lies. this catastrophe took 
place in the reign of aboonirin, twentieth sovereign of the dynasty of 
molonekin--three thousand years after the reign of barrahneil. 

so ran the history of atlantis, or at least all of it that need be 
quote for the elucidate of this story. that black magic--the black 
art of the atlantean was by no means dead--hamar felt convinced, and 
if maitland could resuscitate it--why could not he? at any rate he 
might try. he could lose nothing by give it a trial--at least 
nothing to speak of--the outlay on chemical would be a mere 
song--whereas, on the other hand, what might he not gain! he eager 
peruse the tests--the test he must impose upon himself before he 
could get in touch with the unknown, and acquire the magic 
powers--which, according to thomas maitland, were copy from the 
original brahnapotek, and including a preface, ran as follows: 
(_preface_) "it is essential that the person desirous of being 
initiate into the black art--the art of communicate with the 
unknown (daramara) in order to acquire certain great powers, should 
dismiss from his mind all idea of moral progress, and wholly 
concentrate on the better of his material self--on acquire riches 
and fame in the physical sphere. his aspiration must be entire 
earthly, and all his affection subordinate to his main desire for 
wealth and carnal pleasures. have acquire this preliminary 
psychological stage, for one clear week he must give himself up 
entire to the break of all the conventionality of morality with 
which society is hedge in. he must practice every kind of 
deception--lie, cheat and steal, and go out of his way to seek an 
opportunity to avenge any personal injury; and if his mind is 
earnest and wholly concentrated on acquire knowledge of the black 
art no bodily mishap will befall him. during this time of probation he 
must will himself to dream, at night, of all the deed he had it in 
his mind to do, during the day; when he will know, by his visions, to 
what extent he is progressing. at the end of the week he must apply 
the test to see if he is in a ripe state to proceed. 

"the tests-- 

"no. 1. at midnight, when the moon is full, place a mirror, set in 
a wooden frame, in a tub of water, so that it will float on the 
surface with its face uppermost. put in the water fifteen grain 
of bicarbonate of potash, and sprinkle it with three drops of 
blood, not necessarily human if the reflection of the moon in the 
mirror then appear crimson, the test is satisfactory 
accomplished. 

"no. 2. at midnight, when the moon is full, take a black cat, place 
it where the moonbeam are thickest, sprinkle it with three drops 
of blood, not necessarily human, and rub its coat with the palm of 
the hand. sparks will then be given out, and if those sparks 
appear crimson the test is satisfactory done. 

"no. 3. take a human skull--preferably that of some person who has 
met with an unnatural end, pour on it a single drop of fresh, 
human blood--place it on a couch, and go to sleep with the back 
part of the head rest on it. if you are awakened, at the second 
hour after midnight, by hearing a great commotion close at hand, 
and the room is then discover to be full of crimson light, the 
test is satisfactory fulfilled. 

"no. 4. take half a score of the berry of enchanter's 
nightshade,[15] two ounce of hemlock leaves in powder, and one 
ounce of red sorrel leaves. heat them in an oven for two hours, 
pound them together, in a mortar, and at midnight boil them in 
water. as soon as the contents begin to bubble, remove them from 
the fire and stand them in a dark place; and if the experiment is 
to prove satisfactory, three bubble of luminous green light will 
rise simultaneous from the water and burst. 

"no. 5. in the above preparation after the test described, soak a 
hazel twig, fashion in the shape of a fork. on meeting a child 
hold the fork with the v downwards in front of its face, and if 
the child exhibit violence and sign of terror, and falls down, 
the experiment is successful. 

"no. 6. take a couple of handful of fine soil from over the spot 
where some four-footed animal has recently been buried. put it in 
a tin vessel, mix with it three ounce of assafoetida and one 
drachm of quassia chips, to which add a death's-head moth 
(_acherontia atropos_). heat the vessel over a wood fire for three 
hours. then remove it and place it on the hearth, rake out the 
fire and make the room absolutely dark. keep watch beside the 
vessel, and if, at the second hour after midnight, any strange 
phenomena occur, the test will be known to have been 
satisfactory executed. 

"(_addendum_) if any of these test fail the candidate must wait 
for six month before give them a further trial, and he must 
occupy the interim by training his thought in the manner already 
prescribed. but if, on the other hand, the test have been 
successful performed, he can proceed with the rite appertain 
to the black art." 

hamar had read so far when, with a gesture of impatience, he closed 
the book. "what a fool i am!" he exclaimed, "to waste my time with 
such stuff!... but maitland write in such a devilish convincing way! 
jerusalem! any straw is good enough for the drown man, and if 
witchcraft and sorcery with motor dashing by every second and the 
whole air alive with wireless and telephones, is a bit beyond my 
comprehension, what then? all i care about is money--and i'll leave no 
stone unturned to get it. if it were possible for man to get in touch 
with daramara--the unknown--devil, or whatever else it choose to call 
itself--i'll call it an angel if it only give me money--twenty 
thousand years ago--why shouldn't it be possible to get in touch with 
it now? anyhow as i said before, i'll have a try. as far as the 
preliminary stage is concerned, i fancy i'm pretty well fixed. my mind 
is occupy right enough with things of this world--i don't give a 
cent for anything belong to another--and if only i had half a dozen 
souls, i'd sell them right away now, for less than twenty thousand 
dollars--a damn sight less. as for these tests--foolish isn't the 
word for them--but it won't cost much just to try them.... now, 
according to thomas maitland, the ceremony of calling up the unknown 
stand a far greater chance of success if there are three human being 
present ... but, of course, if there is any truth in this business, 
i'd rather keep the secret of it to myself. however, if i try alone, 
the unknown may not come to me, and then i shall have had all the 
trouble of go through the test for nothing!... ah! now i see! if 
the other two get more of the profit than i think necessary--i can 
make use of my newly acquire occult power to--to dissolve 
partnership! ha! ha! i could--i could trick the unknown if it come to 
that. trust a jew to outwit the devil! i'll just look up kelson 
and--curtis."
