chapter iii 

learning to sin 


messrs. kelson and curtis did not live in pacific avenue where the 
pope hold sway, nor yet in california street where the crock are 
wont to entertain their millionaire friends. where they lived, there 
were no massive granite steps flank with equally massive 
pillars--such as herald the approach to the nob hill palaces; no rare 
glass bow-windows look out on to flower bedeck lawns; no vast 
betile hall, with rotunda in the centre; no highly polish oak 
staircases; no fresco ceilings; no tufted, cerulean blue silk 
draperies; and no sweet perfumery--only the smell, if one may so 
sudden sink to a third-class expression--only the smell of rank 
tobacco and equally rank lager beer. no, messrs. kelson and curtis 
reside within a stone's throw of the five cent baths in rut 
street--and that was the nearest they ever got to bathing. their suite 
of apartments consist of one room, about ten by eight feet, which 
serve as a dining-room, drawing-room, study, boudoir, kitchen, 
bedroom, and--from sheer force of habit, i was about to add bathroom; 
but as i have already hint cold water on half-empty stomach and 
chilly liver is uninviting; besides, soap costs something. their 
furniture was antique but not massive; nor could any of it be fairly 
reckon superfluous. all told, it consist of a bedstead (three 
six-foot plank on four sugar cubes; the bedclothes--a pair of 
discard overalls, a torn and much emaciate blanket, a woolly neck 
wrap, a yellow vest, and the garment they stood in); a small round 
and rather rickety deal table; and one chair. of the very limited 
number of culinary utensils, the frying-pan was by far the most 
important. its handle serve as a poker, and its pan, as well as for 
frying, roasting and boiling, did duty for a teapot and a slop-basin. 
they had no crockery. they had only one thing in abundance--namely, 
air; for the lower frame of the window have long lack glass in it, 
a couple of page of the _examiner_, fixed in it, flap dismal 
every time the wind came blow down 216th street. 

they had not live there always. in the palmy days of work, before the 
firm smashed, they had aspire to what might properly be call 
diggings; and, moreover, had "digged" in respectable surroundings. it 
was the usual thing--the thing that is happening always, every hour of 
the day, in all the great city of the world--starvation, through 
lack of employment. civilization still shut its eyes to everyday 
poverty. who knows? who cares? who is responsible? no one. is there a 
remedy? ah! that is a question that require time. time--always time! 
time for the politician, and time for the starve ones! half the 
world thinks, whilst half the world dies; and the cause of it all is 
time--too much, a damn sight too much--time! 

but kelson and curtis could not grumble. they had their room--bare, 
dirty and well-ventilated--for next to nothing. fifty cent a week! 
and they could furnish it as they pleased. fancy that! what a 
privilege! they were glad of it all the same--glad of it in preference 
to the streets; and probably, when asleep, they thought of it as home. 
but on leave hamar's, that evening, they had fully resolve to 
convert their little room into a cemetery. what else could they do? 
what can any one do who has no money and no prospect of get any, 
and who has reach the pitch of acute hunger? he has pass the stage 
of wanting work, because, if work were offer to him, he would not be 
in a fit state to do it--he would be too weak. too weak to work! what 
a phenomenon! yes--to all those who have never miss a day's meals. 
to others--no! they can understand--and understand only too well--the 
really poor who have long cease to eat, cannot work--they are beyond 
it. 

when curtis and kelson stagger down the stairs of the house where 
hamar lodged, they realize that unless something turn up pretty 
soon, it would be too late--they would be past the stage of care for 
anything--too feeble to do anything but lie on the ground and pray 
that death would come quickly. 

"home?" kelson inquired, as they emerge on to the pavement. 

"hell!" curtis answered, and kelson, taking it for grant that the 
terms were synonymous, at once head for their garret. 

"don't walk so confounded fast," curtis gasped; "this pain in my 
side is like a hundred stitch roll in one. it fairly doubles me 
up. ease down a bit, for heaven's sake!" 

kelson obeyed, and presently came to a dead halt before a 
dingy-looking restaurant. both men lean against the window and gaze 
wolfish at the food. a warm, foetid rush of air from under the 
grating at their feet tickle their nostril and mock their hunger 
with a mockery past endurance. arrange on the window-sill was a 
miscellaneous collection of very smeary plate and dishes, contain 
an even more miscellaneous collection of food. a half-consumed ham, 
with more than a mere suspicion of dirt on its yellowish-white fat; 
some concoction in a bowl that might have been brawn made from some 
peculiarly liverish pig, or--from one of the many homeless mongrel 
that roam the street at night; a pile of noxious-looking mussels, 
side by side with a glisten mass of particularly yellow whelks; a 
round of what purport to be beef--very fat and very underdone; some 
black shiny sausages, and a score or so of lurid red polonies. a 
similar assortment was to be seen on the counter behind which loll 
an anæmic girl, in a dirty cotton blouse, and a much soil sky-blue 
skirt. 

a month ago such an exhibition would have been an offence in the 
fastidious eyes of messrs. kelson and curtis; but now it was 
otherwise. their stomach would have refuse nothing short of garbage. 

"matt!" curtis's hand had left off clutch at his belt and were now 
hanging by his side; the finger twitch to and fro in a manner that 
fascinate kelson. "matt! is there any logic in our starving?" 

"none, excepting that we haven't a cent between us!" kelson rejoined. 

"i know that," curtis went on slowly, "but--i mean--why should we 
starve when all this grub is within two inch of us! it's 
unreasonable--it's intolerable." 

"doesn't the smell of it satisfy you?" kelson replied, attempt to 
force a smile, and failing dismally. 

"d--n the smell!" curtis cried. "it's the ham i want. i'd give my soul 
for a good munch at it. and just look at that tea, too! don't you see 
it steam over there? what wouldn't i give for just one cup! ten 
minutes more and it may be too late. the pain will come on again--and 
it will be very doubtful if i shall ever get home. i'm close on the 
stage when one begin to digest one's own stomach. curse it! i won't 
starve any longer! matt! she's in there all by herself!" 

"so i've been thinking," kelson murmured, glancing uneasy up and 
down the street. "still she's a girl, ed!" 

"that's just it!" curtis whispered; "it is because she is a girl. if 
she were a man, in our present condition we shouldn't stand a chance. 
come! it's this or die in the gutters. it's our one and only chance. 
let's go in--have a feed--take what we can and make a bolt for it. if 
she try to stop us we can settle her right enough." 

"without being too rough! there's no need to be too rough with her, 
ed." 

"i shouldn't stick at much!" curtis answered. "occasions like these 
don't admit of chivalry. come along! it's the ham i'm after." 

curtis shuffle forward as he spoke, and the next moment kelson and he 
were standing in front of the counter. 

the girl eye curtis very dubious and it is more than likely would 
have refuse to serve him had he been alone. but her expression 
change on look at kelson. kelson was one of those individual who 
seldom fail to meet with the approval of women--there was a something 
in him they liked. probably neither he nor they could have define 
that something; but there it was, and it came in extremely handy now. 

"what do you want?" she inquire shortly. 

"ham! give me some of that ham over there, miss, and a cup of tea! 
bread too!" curtis cry eagerly. "do you know what it is to have a 
twist on, miss? i have one on now--so please give us a full 
twenty-five cents' worth." 

kelson said nothing, but his eyes glistened, and the girl wonder as 
she pass him the polonies. 

both men ate as they had never eaten before, and as they would not have 
eaten now had they paid any attention to the advice of hunger experts. 
however, they survived, and when they could eat no more they lean 
back in their chair to enjoy the sensation of returning--albeit, 
slow returning--strength. 

curtis was the first to make a move. "matt," he murmured, "we've about 
sat our sit. we'd better be off. you go and say a few nice words to 
the girl and make pretence of paying. i'll secure the ham--there's 
still a good bit left--and anything else i can grab. the moment i do 
this, throw these chair on the ground so that the girl will fall over 
them when she make a dash for me, which she is certain to do. we will 
then head straight away for 216th street. don't look so scared or she 
will think there is something up. she has never taken her eyes off you 
since we sat down!" 

"she's rather a nice girl!" kelson said. "i wish i didn't look quite 
such a blackguard--and--i wish i hadn't to be quite such a blackguard. 
who'll pay for all this? will she?" 

"we shan't, anyway," curtis sneered. "come, this is no time to be 
sentimental. it was a question of life and death with us, and we've 
only done what any one else would do in our circumstances. the girl 
won't lose much! are you ready?" 

curtis rose, and kelson, who was accustomed to obey him, reluctantly 
follow suit. a look almost suggestive of fear came into the girl's 
eyes as they encounter those of curtis, and she shot a swift glance 
at an inner door. then kelson spoke, and as she turn her head 
towards him, her lip part in a sort of smile. 

"nice night, miss, isn't it?" kelson said, halting half-way between 
the counter and the chairs. "aren't you a bit lonely here all by 
yourself?" 

"sometimes," the girl laughed. "but my mother's in the room there," 
and she nod in the direction of the closed door. "and one can't be 
dull when she's about. she's that there active as a rule, there's no 
keeping her quiet--only just at present"--here she glance 
apprehensive at curtis--"she's recover from ague. get it every 
year about this time. your friend seem to have kind of taken a fancy 
to our ham!" 

kelson look at curtis and his heart thumped. curtis's right hand was 
get ready to spring at the ham, whilst his left was creep 
stealthy along the counter in the direction of a loaf of bread. 
kelson slow realize that an acute crisis in both their lives was at 
hand, and that it depend on him how it would end. he had never 
thought it possible to feel as mean as he felt now. besides, his 
natural sympathy with women tempt him to stand by the girl and 
prevent curtis from rob her. he was still deliberating, when he 
saw two long dark objects, with lightning rapidity, swoop down on the 
plate and dishes. there was a loud clatter, and the next moment the 
whole place seem alive with movement. 

a voice which in his confusion he did not recognize at once 
shouted--and seemingly from far away--"quick, you fool, quick! fling 
down the chair and grab those sausages!" whilst from close beside 
him--almost, he fancied, in his ears--came a wild shriek of "mother! 
mother! we are being robbed!" 

had the girl appeal to him to help her it is more than likely that 
kelson, who was even yet undecided what course to adopt, would have 
offer her his aid; but the instant she act on the defensive his 
mind was made up; a mad spirit of self-preservation swept over 
him--and dashing the chair on the ground at her feet, he seize the 
sausages, and flew after curtis. 

ten minutes later, curtis and kelson, their arms full of spoil, 
clamber up the staircase of their lodgings, and reel into their 
room. 

"look!" curtis gasped, sink into the chair. "look and see if we are 
followed!" 

"there's no one about!" kelson whispered, peer cautious out of 
the window. "not a soul! i don't believe after that first rush across 
rut street, any one notice us. to leave off running was far the 
best thing to do. you are a perfect genius, ed. i wonder if this sort 
of thing--er--thieving--is dormant in most of us? i say, old fellow, i 
wish i hadn't look at that book of hamar's. do you know, directly i 
took it up, an extraordinary sensation of cunning came over me; and i 
declare, when i put it down, i felt it would take very little to make 
me a criminal!" 

"we're both criminal now--in the eyes of the law--anyway!" curtis 
said. "and now we've got so far there's no alternative but to go on! 
it's easy for a hundred camel to pass through the eye of a needle 
than for a clerk to get work, that's a fact. the market are 
hopeless overstocked--no one wants us! no one help us! no one even 
think about us. the labour man get pity and cent galore--we get 
nothing!--nothing but rotten pay whilst we work, and when we're out of 
work, dosshouse or kerbstones. d--n clerks, i say. d--n everything! 
there's no justice in creation--there's no justice in anything--and 
the only people who prate of it are those who have never known what it 
is to want. say, when shall we take the next lot?" 

"when we're obliged, not before!" kelson said. "or rather, you do as 
you like--and i'll do the same." 

"well, i'm not go to commit suicide anyhow," curtis sneered. "we 
haven't the money to buy poison--and i've no mind to drown myself or 
cut my throat--they're too painful! if we don't go on do what we've 
done to-night, what are we go to do?" 

"trust to luck," kelson sighed. 

"all right--you trust to luck--but i won't trust any more in 
providence, and that's a fact," curtis retorted. "we've been done 
enough. now i'm for do other people. good-night." 

he tumble into the makeshift bed as he spoke; and in a few minutes, 
worn out after the unwonted exertion of the evening, both men were 
fast asleep. 

they were at breakfast next morning--real _déjeuner à la 
carte_--sausages, bread, water--and they were do ample justice to 
it, when some one rap at the door. for a few seconds there was 
silence. their heart stood still. had they been followed, after all? 
was it the police? some one spoke--and they breathe again. it was 
hamar. 

"this looks like starving, i must say!" hamar exclaimed, as he sniff 
his way into the room and sat on the bed. "why, from what you fellow 
told me last night i thought you were clear out. and here you are, 
stuffing like roosters! you look a bit surprise to see me, but you'll 
look more surprised, i reckon, when i tell you what bring me here. 
you remember that book?" 

kelson and curtis nodded. 

"well," hamar went on. "i read it after you left last night, and i've 
come to the conclusion that there's something in it that may be of use 
to us." 

"us!" curtis ejaculated. 

"yes! us!" hamar mimicked. "it contain full particulars of how we can 
get in touch with certain occult powers--that can give us money or 
anything else we want!" 

"rot, of course!" curtis said. 

"you say that now. but, listen to me," hamar replied. "since i've read 
that book, i believe there's a lot more in occult than people 
imagine. you may recollect the name of the author of the book--thomas 
maitland? well! to begin with, he impress me as being truthful; and 
he not only believe in magic but he practised it. if he hadn't gone 
into detail i shouldn't think anything of it, but he's so darn 
thorough, and tell you exactly what you've got to do to get in touch 
with the occult powers and to practise sorcery. he learned it all from 
that old ms. he found, written by an atlantean; and the atlanteans, he 
says, were adept in every form of occultism. i tell you, this chap 
himself scoff at it at first; and it was more out of curiosity, he 
says, than because he was convinced, that he began to experiment. he 
afterwards came to the conclusion that the atlantean were no fools. 
what they had written about the occult was absolutely correct--there 
was another world, and it was possible to get in touch with it. now, 
if thomas maitland was able to practise sorcery, why can't we? there 
was a gap of close on twenty thousand years between his time and that 
of atlantis, and there's not much more than two hundred years between 
his day and ours. but, of course, if you're go to pooh-pooh the 
whole thing i won't trouble to tell you any more!" 

"well, leon," kelson ejaculated, "magic and sorcery do seem a trifle 
out of date, don't they? could any one look out of the window at what 
is go on in the street below, and at the same time believe in 
fairy and hobgoblins? still the book made a bit of an impression on 
me, so that i'm inclined to agree with you. anyway, go ahead! ed is 
agreeable, aren't you, ed?" 

curtis gave a sulky nod. "i'm not averse to anything that may put us 
in the way of a livelihood," he said. 

hamar, somewhat appeased, brief informed them of the test and other 
preliminary necessary for the acquire of the black art, and 
without more ado propose that they--the three of them--should form a 
syndicate and call it the sorcery company limited. "to begin with," he 
said, "we might sell trick and spells, and later on tackle something 
more subtle. why, we could soon knock all the juggle and doctor on 
the head--and make a huge fortune." 

"that is to say if it isn't all humbug!" curtis observed. 

"well--do you or don't you think it worth trying?" hamar cut in. "you 
call me a jew--but jews, you know, have a tolerably cool head, and a 
keen faculty for business. they don't touch anything unless it is 
pretty certain to bring them in money. will you try?" 

"y-e-s!" curtis said slowly; "i'll try." 

"and you, matt?" hamar queried. "we must have three." 

"i don't mind trying," kelson replied. "i expect it will be only a 
try." 

"that settle it, then!" hamar cried. "now, we'll get to business. to 
begin with we're all wholly occupy with things of this world--money 
chiefly!" 

"sometimes music!" curtis said sententiously. 

"and sometimes girls," kelson join in. "music's a pose on ed's part. 
i don't believe he really care a bit for it. he's far too material." 

"just what i want him to be!" hamar laughed. "girls are material 
enough too--especially when you take them out to supper. anyhow, money 
is our first consideration, isn't it?" 

to this there was general assent. 

"the preliminary requirement is fixed then," hamar said. "now for the 
week of wild oats! lying, stealing, cheating--anything to counteract 
the code of moses! let's take them in turn. lie won't trouble us 
much. every one lies. lie is the stock-in-trade of doctors, lawyers, 
sky pilots, storekeepers--" 

"and dentists!" curtis chime in. 

"and shop girls!" kelson added. 

"all women--rich as well as poor!" hamar went on. "lying is woman's 
birthright. she lie about her age, her looks, her clothes--everything. 
with a lie she send caller away, and when she is in the mood, 
entertain them with lies. women are born liars, but they are not the 
only liars. in these days of keen competition every one lies--every 
editor, publisher, undertaker, piano-tuner, dustman--they couldn't live 
if they didn't. moreover lie is natural to us all. every child lie 
as soon as it can speak; and education merely teach him to lie the 
more effectually. lie come just as natural as sweating--" 

"or kissing," kelson interrupted. 

"or any of the other so-called vices," hamar continued. "so we can 
manage that all right. as to cheating--having nothing to cheat 
with--according to instruction we've got to keep in with each other, 
so present company is excepted--we must pass over that. now--how about 
thieving!" 

"never done any yet, so can't say," curtis exclaimed. 

"nor i either," kelson put in rather hurriedly. 

"well, i didn't suppose you had!" hamar laughed; "though, after all, 
more than half the world do thieve--all employer steal labour from 
their employés, all tradesmen steal a profit--the wholesale man from 
the middleman--the middleman from the retailer. every government 
thieves. look at england--righteous england! at one time or another 
she has stolen land in every part of the world. but theft is an ugly 
word. when statesmen steal it's call diplomacy, when the rich steal 
it's call kleptomania or business, and it's only when the poor steal 
that steal is term theft. we who have every excuse--we who are 
starving--will be content with--that is to say--we will only 
take--just enough to keep us alive--a few lump of sugar, a handful of 
raisins, or a loaf of bread. how about that?" 

"i might manage that," curtis said. "i might--but i don't want to get 
caught." 

"and you, matt?" 

"i don't mind steal food so much," kelson said. "in the face of so 
much wealth--and waste too--it seem a big sin to starve than to 
steal a loaf of bread." 

"the lie and steal are fixed then," hamar laughed. "what you have 
to do, too, is to make the most of every opportunity you can find of 
do people--present company excepted--bad turns." 

"i don't see how--in our present condition--we can do any one much 
harm," curtis remarked. "we haven't even the means to buy a tin sword, 
let alone a bomb or pistol. if we wish them ill, perhaps, that will do 
instead." 

"possibly--but don't be such an ass as to wish any one any good!" 
hamar said. "do your best to carry out the injunction i have given 
you, and we will meet here, this day week, to discuss the tests." 




chapter iv 

the test 


seven days later, hamar again knock at curtis's and kelson's door 
and walk in. a faint sigh of relief escape him. 

"i see we are all right so far," he said. "i wonder whether i should 
find you both flown, or lie stretch in the icy hand of death. 
have you experimented?" 

"we have," curtis said. "we've done our best. in what way, we prefer 
not to say." 

"perhaps there is no need," hamar replied, eye the mantelshelf 
which bore ample testimony to a full larder, and glancing at curtis's 
feet which were encase in a pair of new and very shiny boots. (a 
handsome overcoat that was hanging on the door also attract his 
attention; but that he had seen before, and conclude that it had been 
there on the occasion of his last visit.) "but you had better dry up 
now, ed," he continue somewhat caustically, "or there'll be no chance 
of form the sorcery society; it will be dissolve before it's 
started. there's no need to ask if you've try to carry out 
instruction as to thoughts, i see it--in your faces. i could never 
have believe one experimental week in bad would have made such a 
difference to your looks." 

"you told us to try hard!" kelson murmured, "and naturally we did. i 
reckon you've done the same by your expression. i should hardly have 
known you." 

"it show pretty clearly," curtis said, "what a lot of bad is latent 
in most people; and that the right circumstances only are need to 
bring it out. starvation, for instance, is calculate to bring out the 
evil in any one--no matter whom. but what puzzle me, is how we have 
escape being caught!" 

"that's a good sign," hamar said. "it bear out what is written in the 
book. if you give your whole mind to do wrong during this trial 
week you'll meet with no mishap. but you must be heart and soul in it. 
hunger made us--hunger has been our friend." 

"what do you mean?" curtis said. 

"why," hamar replied, "if we hadn't been well-nigh starve we 
shouldn't have been able to carry out the instruction quite so 
thoroughly." 

"have you, too, stolen?" curtis queried. 

"i have certainly appropriate a few necessaries," hamar said shortly, 
"but i mean to stop now. we have higher game to fly at. now, with 
regard to the tests. i have not been idle i can assure you. i have 
secure all the requisites. the mirror and black cat i--well, er--to 
use a conventional that come in rather handy--the mirror and 
cat--i picked up. the skull i borrow from a medical i know--the 
moth--er--from some one's private collection--and the elderberries, 
hemlock and chemical i obtain from a drug store man in battery 
street with whom i use to deal. the moon will be full to-night so 
that we may as well begin. will you come round to my room at 
eleven-thirty?" 

they promised; and hamar, as he took his departure, again glance at 
the handsome fur coat hanging on the door. 

he was hardly out of hearing when curtis look across at kelson. "do 
you think he recognise it!" he whispered. "you may bet he did, and he 
had only just stolen it himself! however, it's his own fault. he told 
us to lie and steal, and we've done his bidding." 

"we have indeed!" kelson sighed; "at least you have. for my part i'd 
rather be content with food!" 

"well, i need clothes just as much as food!" curtis snarled. "if i 
went about naked i should only be sent to prison--that's the law. it 
punish you for taking clothes, and it punish you for go without 
them. there's logic for you!" 

curtis and kelson spent the rest of the day indoors; and at night 
sally forth to hamar's. 

the solitary attic--if one could thus designate a space of about three 
square feet--which comprise hamar's lodging--had the advantage of 
being situated in the top storey of a skyscraper--at least a 
skyscraper for that part of the city. from its window could be seen, 
high above the serried ranks of chimney-pots on the opposite side of 
the street, those two newly erect buildings: william carman's chew 
gum factory in hearne street, and mark goddard's eight-storied 
private residence in van ness avenue; and, as if this were not enough 
architecture grace for the eye to dwell on, glimmer away to the 
right was the needle-like spire of moss bates's devil-dodging 
establishment in branman street; whilst, just behind it, in saucy 
mock impudence, peep out the gilded roof of the knee brothers' 
recently erect cinematograph palace. 

all this and more--much more--was to be seen from hamar's outlook, and 
all for the sum of one dollar and a half per week. when curtis and 
kelson entered, the room was aglow with moonlight, and hamar and the 
black cat were stealthy regarding one another from opposite corner 
of the room. from far away--from somewhere in the very base of the 
building, came the dull echo of a shout, succeed by the violent 
slam of a door; whilst from outside, from one of the many desert 
thoroughfare below, rose the frightened cry of a fugitive woman. 
otherwise all was comparatively still. 

"you're a bit early!" was hamar's greeting, "but better that than 
late. everything is ready, and all we've got to do is to wait till 
twelve. sit down." 

they did as they were bid. presently the cat, forsake its sanctuary, 
and ignore curtis's solicitations, glide across the floor, and 
climb on to kelson's knee, refuse to budge. the trio sat in 
silence till a few minutes before midnight, when hamar rose, and, 
select a spot where the moonbeam lay thickest, placed thereon the 
tub of water, in which--with its face uppermost--he proceed to float 
a small mirror, set in a cheap wooden frame. he then calm produce a 
pocket knife. 

"what's that for?" kelson inquire nervously. 

"blood!" hamar responded. "one of us must spare three drops. the 
conditions demand it--and after all the ham and sausage you two have 
eaten i think one of you can spare it best. which of you shall it be? 
come, there's no time to lose!" 

"matt has more blood than i have!" curtis growled; "but why not the 
cat?" 

"it would spoil our chance with it for the other experiment," hamar 
said. "it's a sulky, cross-grained brute, and would give us no end of 
trouble. besides it can bite. look here, let's draw lots!" 

curtis and kelson were inclined to demur; but the propose method was 
so in accordance with custom that there really did not seem any 
feasible objection to raise to it. accordingly lot were drawn--and 
hamar himself was the victim. curtis laugh coarsely, and kelson hid 
his smile in the cat's coat. a neighbouring clock now began to strike 
twelve. 

"look alive, leon!" curtis cried, nudge kelson's elbow. "look alive 
or it will be too late. the unknown is mighty particular to a few 
seconds. let me operate on you. i've always fancy i was born to use 
the knife--that i've really miss my vocation. you needn't be 
afraid--there's no artery in the palm of your hand--you won't bleed to 
death." 

thus goaded, hamar prick away nervous at his hand, and, after 
sundry efforts, at last succeed in drawing blood; three drops of 
which he very careful let fall in the tub. 

"i wish it was light so that we could see it," curtis whisper in 
kelson's ear. "i believe jew have different coloured blood to other 
people." 

though kelson was apprehensive, hamar did not appear to have heard; 
his whole attention was rivet on the mirror, on the face of which 
was a reflection of the moon. 

"i knew nothing would happen," curtis cried, "you had better wipe your 
knife or you'll be arrest for sever some one's jugular. hulloa! 
what's up with the cat?" 

hamar was about to tell him to be quiet when kelson caught his arm. 
"look, leon! look! what's the brute doing? is it mad?" kelson gasped. 

hamar turn his head--and there crouch on the floor, in the 
moonlight, was the cat, its hair bristle on end and its green eyes 
ablaze with an expression which held all three men speechless. when 
they were at last able to avert their eyes a fresh surprise await 
them; the reflection of the moon in the mirror was red--not an 
ordinary red--not merely a colour--but red with a lurid luminos 
that vibrate with life--with a life that all three men at once 
recognize as emanate from nothing physical--from nothing good. 

it vanish suddenly, quite as sudden as it had come; and the 
reflection of the moon was once again only a reflection--a white, 
placid sphere. 

for some seconds no one spoke. hamar was the first to break the 
silence. "well!" he exclaimed, drawing a long breath; "what do you 
think of that!" 

"are you sure you weren't faking?" curtis said. 

"i swear i wasn't," hamar replied; "besides could any one produce a 
thing like that? the cat didn't think it was a fake--it knew what it 
was right enough. besides, why are your teeth chattering?" 

"why are yours?" curtis retorted; "why are matt's?" 

"shall we try the second?" hamar asked. 

"no!" kelson and curtis said in chorus. "no! we've had enough for one 
night. we'll be off!" 

"i think i'll come with you," hamar said, "after what has happen i 
don't quite relish sleep here alone--or rather with that cat. 
hi--satan, where are you?" 

satan was not visible. it had probably hidden under the bed, but as no 
one care to look, its whereabouts remain undiscovered. 

with the coming of the sun, the terror of the night wore off, and the 
trio separated. hamar would on no account accept his friends' 
invitation to breakfast on the sausage and ham they had run such 
risk in procuring; he made hasty track for a snug restaurant in 
bolter's street, where he had a sumptuous repast for a dollar; and 
then slunk home. 

shortly before midnight all three met again, and at once commence 
preparation for the second test. the question arose as to who should 
hold satan. they all had vivid recollection of the cat's behaviour 
the previous night; consequently no one was anxious to officiate. 
finally they drew lots, and fate settled on curtis. an exciting chase 
now began. satan, demonstrate his resentment of their treatment of 
him, at every turn, knock over a water bottle, rip the skin of 
kelson's knuckles, and made his teeth meet in the fleshy part of 
curtis's thumb. 

"hulloa! what are you up to?" curtis savage demanded, as hamar 
thrust a cup at him. 

"hold your hand over it!" hamar said sharply. "don't suck it! we want 
blood for this test and for the next." 

"i wish the brute had bitten you!" curtis snarled; "then, perhaps, you 
wouldn't be so precious keen on economics. you did right to name it 
satan! and if it doesn't attract devil nothing will. i'm not go to 
touch it again. see if you can hold the beast by yourself, matt! it 
seem to be less afraid of you than of either of us." 

kelson call out: "puss!", and the cat at once came to him. 

as it was now striking twelve, hamar careful shook three drops of 
curtis's blood from the cup on to satan's back, while he instruct 
kelson to rub the animal's coat with the palm of the hand. kelson 
cautious obeyed. there was a loud crackling and a shower of sparks, 
of the same lurid red colour as the reflection in the mirror on the 
previous night, flew out into the envelop darkness. 

"that will do!" hamar observe quietly. "test two is satisfactory 
accomplished. we must be ripe for hell than we imagined. there is no 
need for you fellow to stay any longer. i can manage the third test 
alone." 

as soon as his colleague had gone and he felt assured they were no 
long within hearing, hamar took a saucer from the mantelshelf, 
fill it half full of milk, and pour into it some colourless liquid 
out of a tiny phial label poison. 

"here pussy," he call out, softly. "pretty pussy, come and have your 
supper! pussy!" 

and satan, unable to resist the tempt sight of the milk, crept out 
of his hiding-place and quite unsuspicious dip his tongue into 
the saucer and lapped. hamar, in the meanwhile went to a box at the 
foot of the bed and produce a sack. then he slip on his boots and 
coat, and opening the door of a cupboard near the head of the bed 
fetch out a small spade. 

he was now ready; and--so was pussy. 

"that pave the way for test six," hamar observed; "no one can say i 
am a waster--i make use of everything--and every one;" and so saying 
he tumble the cat into the sack and hurried out. 

some half-hour later he had return to his room, and was busy 
engaged making preparation for test three. letting a drop of curtis's 
blood fall on the skull, he put the latter under his pillow, and 
retired to rest. he had slept for little over an hour, when he awoke 
with a start. the muffle sound of hammering--as of nail in a 
coffin--was go on all around him, and occasional it seem to him 
that something big and heavy stalk across the floor; but in spite of 
the fact that the room was illuminate with a red glow--the same lurid 
red as had appear in test one and two--nothing was to be seen. the 
phenomena last five or six minutes and then everything was again 
normal. hamar was so terrify that he lay with his head under the 
bedclothes till morning, and vow nothing on earth would persuade him 
to sleep in that room again. but sunlight soon restore his courage, 
and by the evening he was quite eager to go on with the next test. he 
had some difficulty in persuade any one to allow him the use of an 
oven for so pernicious a mixture as nightshade and hemlock; but at 
last he over-ruled the objection of some good-natured woman--the 
mother of one of the office boys at his former employer's--and test 
four prove as successful as the previous three. the preliminary part 
of test five was also successful accomplished; but in carry out 
the second part of it, hamar all but met with disaster. he was walking 
along kearney street with the specially prepared hazel twig careful 
conceal beneath his coat, when just opposite saddler's jewelry 
store, he came across a child standing by itself. the nearest person 
being some fifty yard away, and no policeman within sight, hamar 
conclude this was too good an opportunity to be lost. he whip out 
the twig, and held it, in the manner prescribed, in front of the 
child. the effect was instantaneous. the child turn white as death, 
its eyes bulge with terror, and opening its mouth to its full extent 
it commence to shriek and yell. then it fell on the pavement; and 
clutch and claw the air, and foam at the mouth roll over 
and over. people from every quarter flock to the spot, and judge 
hamar, from his proximity to the child, to be responsible for its 
condition, shout for the police. the latter, however, arrive too 
late. hamar, whose presence of mind had only left him for the moment 
seeing a bicycle leaning against a store door, jump on it and soon 
put a respectable distance between himself and the crowd. 

that night the trio met once more in hamar's room for test six. there 
was a wood fire in the grate, and on it a tin vessel contain the 
prescribed ingredients. somewhat unpleasant conspicuous amongst 
these ingredient were the death's-head moth, and the soil from 
satan's grave. as soon as the mixture had been heated three hours, the 
vessel was removed, the fire extinguished, and the room made 
absolutely dark. then the three sat close together and waited. 

on the stroke of two every article in the room began to rattle, whilst 
out of the tin vessel flew a blood red moth. after circle three 
times round each of the sitter's heads, the moth flew back again into 
the vessel, and the silence that ensue was follow by a soft tap 
at the window, and the appearance of something, that resemble a big 
tube fill with a thick, pale blue fluid, made up of a mass of 
distinct veins. this tube float into the room, and passing close to 
the three sitters, who involuntary shrank away from it, disappear 
in the wall, behind them. a loud crack as if the branch of a tree had 
broken, terminate the phenomena--the room again becoming pitch dark. 
but the three sitters, although they knew there would be no further 
manifestation that night, were too terrify to move. they remain 
huddle together in the same spot till the morning was well advanced.
