the initiation 


san francisco possess one great advantage--you can easily get out of 
it. leave the pan-handle of the park behind one, and following the 
turn of the cars, one pass through a pretty valley, green and fair 
as any garden, and dot with small houses. an old cemetery lie to 
one side of it; where unconventional inscription and queer epitaph 
can be trace on the half-buried stones, cover with a tangle of 
vine and weeds. still moving forward one reach olympus, and 
climb to its heights, one see away below, in the far distance, the 
coast range--like a rampart of strength; the blue waters of the bay, 
sparkle and dance in the sunlight--steamers flash their path on 
its bosom; and tiny white speck scud in the breeze. below is the 
city, its houses, small, and closed in, like toy village in christmas 
boxes; whilst the slope around are green with fresh grass; and here 
and there are thick cluster of eucalyptus and pines. the ocean is 
partly hidden from view by a peak, which rise directly to the west, 
and is separate from that on which one is standing by a deep and 
thick wooded valley. descending, by means of a narrow winding path, 
one pass through dense clump of hickory, chestnut, mountain ash, 
and walnut trees, whose strong lateral branch afford ample 
protection from the sun, and at the same time furnish playground to 
innumerable bright-eyed squirrels. further down one come upon gentle 
elms, succeed by sassafras and locust--these, in their turn, 
succeed by the soft linden, red bud, catalpa, and maple; and at 
the foot of the declivity, and in the bottom of the valley, wild 
shrubbery, intersperse with silver willows, and white poplars. still 
following the path down the vale, in a southerly direction, one, at 
length, find oneself in an amphitheatre, shut in on all side by 
tree and bush of a still greater variety; here and there, a 
gigantic and much begnarle oak; here, a triple-stemmed tulip tree of 
some eighty feet in height, its glossy, vivid green leaves and profuse 
blossom present a picture of unsurpass beauty and splendour; 
there, equally beautiful, though in marked contrast, a tall and 
slender silver birch. the floor of the amphitheatre is, for the most 
part, grass--soft, thick, velvety and miraculous green. the silence 
is such as make it wholly inconceivable, that so vast a city as san 
francisco can be little over six mile distant. though one may strain 
one's ear to the utmost, nothing is to be heard but the occasional 
tinkle of a cow-bell, the low of cattle and the desultory note of 
birds. it is the perfect quiet which nature alone can give; and it so 
impress hamar that he at once decided that this was the very spot 
essential for the ceremony of initiation into the black art. 

the locality selected, the night had next to be chosen--and the 
conditions demanding that on the night of the initiation there must be 
a new moon, cusp of seventh house, and conjoin with saturn, in 
opposition to jupiter,[16] hamar and his confederate had to wait 
exactly three weeks, from the date of the conclusion of the tests, 
before they could proceed. 

shortly before midnight, on the spot already described, hamar, curtis 
and kelson met; and, after searching thorough amongst the tree and 
bush in the vicinity of the amphitheatre to make sure no one was in 
hiding, they commence operations. 

on a perfectly level piece of ground a circle of seven feet radius was 
clearly defined. this circle was cut into seven sectors; and an inner 
circle from the same centre and with a radius of six feet was next 
drawn. in each part of the sectors, between the circumference of the 
first and second circle, were inscribed, in chalk, the name of the 
seven principal vice (according to atlantean ideas), and the seven 
most malignant diseases. within the second circle, and use the same 
centre, was drawn a third circle, of five feet in radius, and in each 
part of the sectors, between the circumference of the second and 
third circles, were written the name of the seven type of spirits 
most antagonist to man's moral progress.[17] 

hamar had brought with him a sack--the same he had use to transport 
satan's corpse--and from out of it he produce a half-starved tabby, 
that obviously could harm no one, owe to the fact that its head was 
tie up in a muslin bag and its four leg strap together. 

"it's a good thing there is no member of the society for the 
prevention of cruelty to animal anywhere near," kelson exclaimed, 
eye hamar resentfully. "wouldn't a mouse or a rat have done as 
well?" 

"no!" hamar ejaculated, deposit the brute with a plump on the 
ground; "the conditions are that the animal sacrifice must be a cat. 
i got the poorest specimen i could find, for i dislike butcher just 
as much as you do." 

"how are you go to do it?" kelson asked. 

hamar pointed to a chopper. "the conditions say with steel," he said; 
"only with steel, and i should bungle with a knife. you must look the 
other way. now help me with the fire." 

besides the cat, the sack contained a dozen or so bundle of faggots, 
well steep in paraffin, several block of wood, a tripod, and a big 
tin saucepan. 

with the wood, a fire was soon kindle in the centre of the circle; 
and the tripod placed over it. two pint of spring water were then 
pour into the saucepan, and to this were add 1 ounce of oxalic 
acid, 1 ounce of verdigris, 1-1/2 ounce of hemlock leaves, 1/2 ounce 
of henbane, 3/4 ounce of saffron, 2 ounce of aloes, 3 drachm of 
opium, 1 ounce of mandrake-root, 5 drachm of salanum, 7 drachm of 
poppy-seed, 1/2 ounce of assafoetida, and 1/2 ounce of parsley. as 
soon as the saucepan contain these ingredient began to boil hamar 
threw into it two adders' heads, three toad and a centipede. 

"where on earth did you get all those horrors?" curtis asked, 
shrink away from the bag which had held them. 

"here," hamar said laconically. "it's extraordinary what a lot of 
nasty things there are amid so much apparent beauty. i say apparent, 
because nature is a champion faker. you have only to rake about in 
these bush and you'll find snake galore, whilst under pretty nearly 
every stone are centipedes. like both of you, who never by any chance 
poke your nose outside the city, i fancy snake and centipede were 
confine to the prairies. but i know better now. besides, where do you 
think i found the toads? why, in the cellar under meidlers'!" 

"what, our late governor's?" kelson cried. 

hamar nodded. "yes!" he said; "under the very spot where we use to 
sit. the water's a foot deep in that cellar, and if there are as many 
toad in the cellar of the other house in the block, then sacramento 
street has a corner in them. i'm go to be executioner now, so look 
the other way, matt!" 

kelson need no second bidding; and stick his finger in his ears, 
walk to some little distance. when hamar call him back, the deed 
was accomplished--the conditions prescribed in the rite had been 
observed--the tabby was in the saucepan on the fire, and its blood had 
been besprinkle on each of the seven sector of the circle. 

"we must now take our seat on the ground," hamar said; "i'd better be 
in the centre--you, matt, on the right, and you, ed, on the 
left--allowing three clear feet between us." 

hamar show them how to sit--with leg cross and arms folded. 

for some minutes no one spoke. the wind rustle through the bush and 
an owl hooted. kelson, feeling the night air cold, drew his overcoat 
tight around and the other follow suit. then curtis said-- 

"do you really think there's anything in it, leon? aren't we fool to 
go on waste our time like this?" 

to which hamar replied: "shut up! you were frightened enough do the 
tests!" 

from afar off, away on the shimmer bosom of the bay came the faint 
hoot of a steamer. 

"that's the _oleander_!" kelson murmured. 

"rot!" curtis snapped. "how do you know? you can't tell from this 
distance. it might be the _daisy_, or the _san marie_, or any other 
ship." 

kelson made no reply; hamar blew his nose, and once again there was 
silence. 

the effect of the moonlight had now become weird. from the tree and 
bush crept legion of tall, gaunt shadows, and whilst some of these 
were explicable, there were other that certainly had no apparent 
counterpart in any of the natural object around them. even curtis, 
in spite of his scoffing, show no inclination to examine them too 
closely; but kept his face resolute turn to the more cheery light 
of the fire. the soft, cool, sweet-scented air gradual act as an 
anæsthetic, and kelson and curtis were almost asleep, when hamar's 
voice recall them sharp to themselves. 

"it's just two!" he said. "sit tight and listen while i repeat the 
incantation, and for goodness' sake keep cool if anything happens. 
remember we are here with an object--namely--to get everything we can 
out of the other world." 

"trust you for that!" curtis sneered; "but all the same nothing's 
go to happen." 

"i am not sure of that," hamar said, and after a brief pause began to 
repeat these words[18]-- 

"morbas from the mountains, 
where flow malignant fountains. 
we are ready for you--come! 
vampire from the passes, 
where grow blood-sucking grasses, 
we are ready for you--come! 
vice elemental pretty 
give ear unto our ditty 
we are ready for you--come! 
planetians, form so fearful, 
we inform you, eager, tearful, 
we are ready for you--come! 
clanogrians, things of sorrow. 
postpone not till to-morrow, 
we are ready for you--come! 
barrowvians, shades seclusive, 
be not to us exclusive, 
we are ready for you--come! 
earthbound spirits of the dead 
approach with grim and noiseless tread-- 
we are ready for you--come!" 

he then got up and, go to the fire, sprinkle over the flame six 
drachm of belladonna, three drachm of drosera and one ounce of nux 
vomica; use in each case his left hand. return to his former 
position he drew with the forefinger of his left hand, on the ground, 
the outline of a club-foot; a hand with the finger clench and a 
long pointed thumb standing upright; and a bat. at his request kelson 
and curtis careful imitate the devices, each in the space allot 
to him. 

hamar then cried: "creastie havoonen balababoo!"; which hamar 
explain was atlantean for "devil of the damn appear!" 

"he won't!" curtis muttered, "because he doesn't exist. there are 
devils--meidler brother were devils--but there is no one devil! it's 
all----" he sudden stop and an intense hush fell upon them all. 

a cloud obscure the moon, the fire burn dim, and the gloom of the 
amphitheatre thicken till the men lost sight of each other. a cold 
air then rose from the ground and fan their nostrils. something 
flew past their head with an ominous wail; whilst from the direction 
of the fire came a hollow groan. 

"the advent of the unknown," hamar murmured, "shall be herald in by 
the shriek of an owl, the groan of the mandrake--there is 
mandrake in the saucepan--the croak of a toad--we haven't had that 
yet!" 

"yes, there it is!" kelson whispered--and whilst he was speak there 
came a dismal croak, croak, and the sway and crying of an 
ash--"hush!" 

they listened--and all three distinct heard the swish of a 
slender tree trunk as it hiss backwards and forwards. then, a cry so 
horrid, harsh and piercing that even the sceptical, sneer curtis 
gave vent to an expression of fear. again a hush, and increase 
dark and cold. kelson call out-- 

"don't do that, leon." 

"i'm not do anything," hamar said testily. "pull yourself 
together." a moment later he said to curtis, "it's you, curtis. shut 
up. this is no time for monkeying." 

"you are both either mad or dreaming," curtis replied. "i haven't 
stir from my seat. hulloa! what's that? what's that, leon? 
there--over there! look!" 

as curtis spoke they all three became conscious of living things 
around them--things that move about, silent and surreptitious and 
convey the impression of mockery. the hills, the valley, the tree 
were full of it--the whole place teem with it--teemed with silent, 
subtle, stealthy mockery. the senses of the three men were now keen 
alive, but a dead weight hung upon their limb and render them 
useless. and as they stare into the gloom, in sickly fear, the 
firelight flicker and they saw shadows, such as the moon, when low 
in the heaven, might fashion from the figure of a man; but yet they 
were shadow neither of man, nor god, nor of any familiar thing. they 
were dark, vague, formless and indefinite, and they quivered--quivered 
with a quiver that suggest mockery. 

sudden the shadow disappeared; the flicker of the flame ceased; 
and in the place of the fire appear a seething, writhe mass of 
what look like white luminous snakes. and in the midst of this mass 
sprang up a cylinder form, which grew and grew until it attain a 
height of ten or twelve feet, when it remain stationary and threw 
out branches. and the three men now saw it was a tree--a tree with a 
sleek, pulpy, semi-transparent, perspire trunk full of a thick, 
white, vibrating, luminous fluid; and that it was laden with a fruit, 
in shape resemble an apple, but of the same hue and material as the 
trunk. spread out on the ground around it, were its roots, twitch 
and palpitate with repulsive life, and bare with a bare that 
shock the senses. it was so utterly and inconceivable unlike what 
hamar, curtis and kelson had imagine the unknown--and yet, withal, so 
monstrous (not merely in its shape but in its suggestions), and so 
vivid real and livid, that they were not merely terrified--they were 
stricken with a terror that render them dumb and helpless. and as 
they look at it, from out the trunk, shot an enormous thing--white 
and glistening, and fashion like a human tongue. and after point 
derisive at them, it withdrew; whereupon all the fruit shook, as if 
convulse with unseemly laughter. they then saw between the foremost 
branch of the tree a big eye. the white of it was thick and pasty, 
the iris spongy in texture, and the pupil bulge with a lurid light. 
it stare at them with a steady stare--insolent and quizzical. hamar 
and his friends stare back at it in fascinate horror, and would have 
continue staring at it indefinitely, had not hamar's mercenary 
instinct come to their rescue. he recollect that time was pressing, 
and that unless he got into communication with the strange thing at 
once, according to the book, it would vanish--and he might never be 
able to get in touch with it again. thus egg on, he made a great 
effort to regain his courage, and at length succeed in force 
himself to speak. though his voice was weak and shake he manage to 
pronounce the prescribed mode of address, viz.:--"bara phonen etek 
mo," which being interpret is, "spirit from the unknown, give ear to 
me." he then explain their earnest desire to pay homage to the 
supernatural, and to be initiate into the mystery of the black art. 
when hamar had conclude his address, the anticipation of the three 
as to how it would be answered, or whether it would be answer at 
all--were such that they were forced to hold their breath almost to 
the point of suffocation. if the thing _could_ speak what would its 
voice be like? the seconds passed, and they were beginning to prepare 
themselves for disappointment, when sudden across the intervene 
space separate them from the unknown, the reply came--came in soft, 
silky, lisp tones--human and yet not human, novel and yet in some 
way--a way that defy analysis--familiar. strange to say, they all 
three felt that this familiarity belong to a far back period of 
their existence, no less than to a more modern one--to a period, in 
fact, to which they could affix no date. and, although a perfect unity 
of expression suggest that the utterance of the thing was the 
utterance of one being only, a certain variation in its tones, a 
rising and fall from syllable to syllable, led them to infer that 
the voice was not the voice of one but of many. 

"you are anxious to acquire knowledge of the secret associate with 
the great atlantean magic?" the voice lisped. 

"we are!" hamar stammered, "and we are willing to give our soul in 
exchange for them." 

"souls!" the voice lisped, whilst trunk and branch sway lightly, 
and the air was full of silent merriment. "souls! you speak in terms 
you do not understand. to acquire the secret of black magic, all you 
have to do is to agree that during a brief period--a period of a few 
months, you will live together in harmony; that you will make use of 
the powers you acquire to the detriment of all save yourselves; that 
you will never allow your mind to revert to anything spiritual; 
and--that you will abstain from--marrying." 

"and if we succeed in carry out the conditions?" hamar asked. 

[illustration: the initiation] 

"then," the voice replied, "you will retain free, untrammell 
possession of your knowledge." 

"for how long?" curtis queried. 

"for the natural term of your lives--that is to say, for as long as 
you would have live had you never been initiate into the secret of 
magic." 

"and if we fail?" 

"you will pass into the permanent possession of the unknown." 

"does that mean we shall die the moment we fail?" kelson inquire 
timidly. 

"die!" the voice lisped. "again you speak in terms you do not 
understand. you may be sent for." 

"you say--in perfect harmony." hamar put in. "does that mean without a 
quarrel, however slight?" 

"it means without a quarrel that would lead to separation. the moment 
you disunite the compact is broken." 

"what advantage will the secret bring us?" hamar inquired. "can we 
gain unlimited wealth?" 

"yes!" the voice replied. "unlimited wealth and influence." 

"and health?" 

"so long as you fulfil the conditions of the compact you will enjoy 
perfect health. will you, or will you not, pledge yourselves?" 

"i am ready if you fellow are," hamar whispered. 

"i am!" curtis cried. "anything is better than the life we are living 
at present." 

"and i, too," kelson said. "i agree with ed." 

"very well then," the voice once more lisped. "each of you take a 
fruit and eat it, and the compact is irrevocable struck. you cannot 
back out of it without incur the consequence already named. don't 
be afraid, step up here and help yourselves--one apiece--mind, no 
more." and again it seem to hamar, curtis and kelson as if the tree 
and everything around it was convulse with silent laughter. 

"come on!" hamar cried, somewhat imperatively. "don't waste time. 
you've decided, and besides, remember this affair may turn out trumps. 
i'll go first," and walking up to the tree he pluck a fruit and 
began to eat it. curtis and kelson slow follow suit. 

"i believe i'm eat a live slug, or a toad," curtis muttered, with a 
retch. 

"and i, too," kelson whispered. "it's filthy. i shall be sick. if i 
am, will it make any difference to the compact, i wonder?" 

what the fruit really taste like they could never decide. it remind 
them of many things and of nothing. it was sweet yet bitter; it 
repel but at the same time pleased them; it was as perplex as 
the voice--as enigmatical. when they had eaten it they resume their 
former position on the ground, and the voice once again address 
them. 

"the fruit you have consume has create in you a fitness to make use 
of the powers about to be conferred. you have acquire the faculty of 
sorcery--you will be initiate by stages, into the knowledge and 
practice of it. these stages, seven in number, will cover the period 
of your compact, _i.e._ twenty-one months, and at the end of every 
three months--when a fresh stage is reached--you will receive fresh 
powers. 

"in the first stage, the stage you are now enter upon, you will 
receive the power of divination. you will be told how to detect the 
presence of water and all kind of metals, and how to read people's 
thoughts. 

"in the second stage--exactly three month from to-day--you will 
receive the gift of second-sight; the power of separate your 
immaterial from your material body and project it, anywhere you 
will, on the physical plane; and, to a large extent, you will be 
enable to circumvent gravity. thus you will be able to perform all 
manner of jugglery tricks--tricks that will set the whole world 
gaping. profit by them. 

"in the third stage you will possess the secret of invisibility; of 
walking on the water; of breathing under the water; of tame wild 
beasts; and of understanding their language. 

"in the fourth stage you will understand how to inflict all manner of 
diseases, and work all sort of spells; such, for instance, as 
bewitch milk, cause people to have fits, bad dreams, etc. you 
will also know how to create plagues--plagues of insects, or of any 
other noxious thing. 

"in the fifth stage you will possess absolute knowledge of the art of 
medicine and be able to cure every ailment. 

"in the sixth stage you will acquire the power of produce vampire 
and werwolve from the human being, and of transform people from 
the human to any animal guise. 

"in the seventh and final stage you will be given the complete mastery 
of every art and science--including astrology, astronomy, necromancy, 
etc.; and for this stage is reserved the greatest power of 
all--namely, the complete dominion over woman's will and affections. 
the powers of create life, and of extend life beyond the now 
natural limit, and of avoid accidents, will never be confer on 
you. neither shall you learn, not at least during your physical 
existence--who or what we are, or the secret of creation. 

"each successive stage will cancel the preceding one--that is to say, 
the powers you have acquire in the first stage will be annul on 
your arrive at the second stage, and so on. but if you carry out 
your compact faithfully--that is to say, if at the end of the 
twenty-one month you are still united--all the powers you have held 
hitherto, in the different stages, temporarily, will return to you and 
remain in your possession permanently. have you anything to say?" 

"yes!" hamar answered; "i fully understand all you have explain to 
us and i like the idea of it immensely. the fear of our coming to any 
serious loggerheads and of dissolve partnership doesn't worry me 
much--but i must say, it seem very remote--the prospect of gain 
such tremendous powers--powers that will give us practically 
everything we want--save youth--" 

"youth you will never regain," lisp the voice. "and elixir of life, 
surely you must know, are no long sought after, by being of the 
planet earth. they are quite out of date. you will, of course, learn 
the most efficacious means of making yourself and other people 
youthful in appearance." 

"yes, but how shall we learn these secrets?" kelson nerve himself to 
ask. 

"they will be reveal to you in various ways--sometimes when asleep. 
you will receive preliminary instruction as to divination before this 
time to-morrow." 

"and meanwhile, we shall be in want of money," curtis remarked. 

"no!" the voice replied, "you will not be in want of money. have you 
anything more to ask?" 

no one spoke, and the silence that follow was interrupt by a loud 
rustling of the wind. the dark then lifted; but nothing was to be 
seen--nothing save the tree and bushes, moon and stars. 


footnotes: 

[footnote 16: this is a very sinister sign in astrology, denote 
the presence of evil influence of all kinds.--(_author's note._)] 

[footnote 17: according to atlantean idea these spirits were:--vice 
elementals; morba (or disease elementals); clanogrian (or 
malicious family ghosts, such as banshees, etc.); vampires; 
barrowvians, _i.e._ a grotesque kind of phantasm that frequent 
place where prehistoric man or beast has been interred; planetians, 
_i.e._ spirits inimical to dwell on this earth that inhabit 
various of the other planets; and earthbound spirits of such dead 
human being as were mad, imbecile, cruel and vicious, together with 
the phantasm of vicious and mad beasts, and beast of 
prey.--(_author's note_.)] 

[footnote 18: they are a literal translate of the atlantean by 
thos. maitland, and are very nearly identify with form of spirit 
invocation use in egypt, india, persia, arabia, and among the red 
indian of north and south america.--(_author's note_.)] 




chapter vi 

the first power 


after their rencontre with the unknown, hamar and his companion did 
not get back to their respective quarters till the sun was high in the 
heavens, and the street of the city were beginning to vibrate with 
the rattle and clatter of traffic. 

"it's all very well--this wonderful compact of ours," curtis grumbled, 
"but i'm deuced hungry, and matt and i haven't a cent between us. as 
we went all that way last night to oblige you, leon, i think it is 
only fair you should stand us treat. i'll bet you have some nickel 
stow away, somewhere, in those pocket of yours--it wouldn't be you 
if you hadn't! what do you say, matt?" 

"i think as you do," kelson replied. "we've stood by leon, he should 
stand by us. how much have you, leon?" 

"how much have you?" curtis echoed, "come, out with it--no jew-jewing 
pal for me." 

"i might manage a dollar," hamar said ruefully, as the prospect of a 
good meal all to himself, at his favourite restaurant, fade away. 
"where shall we go?" 

just then, kelson, happening to look behind him, saw a young woman of 
prepossessing appearance ascend the steps of a dive in clay street. 
he was instantly attracted, as he always was attract by a pretty 
woman, and something--a kind of intuition he had never had 
before--told him that she was a waitress; that she was discontented 
with her present situation; that she was engaged to be married to a 
pen driver at hastings & hastings in sacramento street; and that she 
had a mother, of over seventy, whom she kept. all this came to kelson 
like a flash of lightning. 

yielding to an impulse which he did not stay to analyse, he grip 
hamar and curtis, each too astonish even to remonstrate, by the arm, 
and, drag them along with him, follow the girl. 

the dive had only just been opened, and was being dust and swept by 
two slattern women with dago complexions, and voice like hyenas. it 
still reek of stale drink and tobacco. 

"what's the good of coming to a place like this?" hamar demanded, as 
soon as he had free himself from kelson's clutches. "we can't get 
breakfast here." 

"matt's mad, that's what's the matter with him," curtis add in 
disgust. "let's get out." 

he turn to go--then, halted--and stood still. he appear to be 
listening. "what's up with you?" hamar asked. "both you fellow are 
behave like lunatic this morning--there's not a pin to choose 
between you." 

"they're play cards, that's all," curtis said. "can't you hear 
them?" 

hamar shook his head. "not a sound," he said. "just look at matt!" 

while the other two were talking, kelson had follow the girl to the 
bar, and catching her up, just as she enter it, said in a manner 
that was peculiar to him--a manner seldom without effect upon girl of 
his class--"i beg your pardon, miss, are we too early to be served? 
jerusalem! haven't i met you somewhere before?" 

the girl look him square in the eyes and then smiled. "as like as 
not," she said. "i go pretty near everywhere! what do you want?" 

"well!" kelson soliloquized; "breakfast is what we are particularly 
anxious for--but i suppose that is out of the question in a dive!" 

"then why did you come here?" the girl queried. 

"because of you! simply because of you," kelson replied. "you 
hypnotize me!" 

"that being so, then i reckon you can have your breakfast," the girl 
laughed, "though we don't provide them as a rule before nine. indeed, 
the management have only just decided--this morning--on providing them 
at all." 

"how odd!" 

"why odd?" the girl questioned, taking off her hat and arrange her 
curl before a mirror. 

"why, that i should have happen to strike the right moment! had i 
come here yesterday it would have been useless. as i said, you 
hypnotize me. evidently fate intended us to meet." 

"do you believe in fate?" the girl asked, shrug her shoulders. "i 
believe in nothing--least of all in men!" 

"you say so!" kelson observed, before he knew what he was saying. "and 
yet you have just got engaged to one. but you've got a bad attack of 
the pip this morning, you have had enough of it here--you want to get 
another post." 

the girl cease do her hair and eye him in amazement. "well!" she 
said. "of all the queer men i've ever met you are the queerest. are 
you a seer?" 

"no!" hamar observed, sudden join in. "he's only very hungry, 
miss. hungry body and soul! hungry all over. and so are we." 

"well, then, go into the room over there," the girl cried, point in 
the direction of a half-open door, "and breakfast will be brought you 
in half a jiffy." 

"who's that play cards?" curtis asked. 

"how do you know any one is play cards?" the girl query with an 
incredulous stare. "you can't see through walls, can you?" 

"no! and i'm hang if i can explain," curtis said, "i seem to hear 
them. there are two--one is call arnold, and the other lemon, or 
some such name, and they are rehearse certain card trick they mean 
to play to-night." 

"that's right," the girl said, "two men name arnold and lemon are 
here. they were play all last night with two of the clerk in 
willow bank, in sacramento street, and they clear them out of every 
cent. you knew it!" 

"no! i didn't," curtis growled, "i don't lie for fun, and i'm just as 
much in a fog, as to how i know, as you are. let's have breakfast now, 
and we'll look up these two gents afterwards, if they haven't gone." 

"your friend's a brute, i don't like him," the girl whisper to 
kelson. "let him lose all he's got--you stay out here." 

"nothing i should like better," kelson said, "it's a bargain!" 

the breakfast was so good that they linger long over it, and the 
bar-room had a fair sprinkling of people when they re-entered it. 
leave kelson to chat with the girl, hamar and curtis, obey her 
directions, found their way to a small parlour in the rear of the 
building, where two men were loll over a card table, smoking and 
drinking, and reading aloud extract from a pink sporting paper. 

"it's a funny thing," one of them exclaimed, "we can't be allow to 
sit here in peace--when there's so much spare space in the house." 

"we beg your pardon for intruding," curtis said, "but my friend and i 
came in here for a quiet game of cards. we're farmer down missouri 
way, and don't often get the chance to run up to town." 

"farmers, are you!" the man who had not yet spoken said, eye them 
both closely. "you don't look it. my friend lemon, here, and i were 
also wanting to have a game--would you care to join us?" 

"by all means," curtis at once exclaimed. "what do you play?" 

"poker!" the man said, "nap! don! but i'll show you something first, 
which, being fresh from the country, you've probably never seen 
before, though they do tell me people in missouri are mighty cute." he 
then proceed to show them what he call the bull and buffalo trick, 
the secret of which he offer to sell them for ten dollars. 

"i wouldn't give you a cent for it!" curtis snapped. "any one can see 
how it is done." 

"you can't!" the man retorted, turning red. "i'll wager twenty dollar 
you can't." curtis accept the wager, and at once did the trick. he 
had seen through it at a glance--there appear no difficulty in it at 
all; and yet he was quite certain if he had been ask to do it the 
day before, he would have utterly failed. 

"now," he said, "give me the money,"--and the man comply with an 
oath. 

"any more tricks?" curtis ask complacently. 

"i know heaps," the man rejoined. "there's one you won't guess--the 
seven card trick." 

he did it. and so did curtis. 

"well i'm----" the man call lemon ejaculated. 

"he's the dandiest cove at trick we've ever struck. try him with the 
prince and slipper, arnold!" 

arnold rather reluctantly assented, and curtis burst out laughing. 

"why!" he said, "that's the simplest of all! see!" and it was done. 
"you two had better come to an understanding with us or you'll not 
shine to-night. how about a game of don?" 

lemon and arnold agreed, but they had barely begun before curtis cry 
out, "it's no use, lemon, i can see those deuce up your sleeve. 
you've some up yours, too, arnold--the deuce of club and the deuce of 
hearts. moreover, you can tell our cards by notch and thumb smear 
on the backs. i'll show you how." he told the cards correctly--there 
was no gainsay it. the men were overwhelmed. 

"what are you, anyway?" lemon asked; "tecs?" 

"never mind what we are!" curtis said savagely. "we know what you 
are--and that's where the rub come in. now what are you go to pay 
us to hold our tongues?" 

"pay you!" lemon hissed. "why, damn you--nothing. we're not bankers. 
all we've got to do is clear out and try somewhere else." 

"that might not be so easy as you imagine," hamar interposed. "we 
would make it our business to have a scene first. why not come to 
terms? we'll not be over exorbitant--and consider the convenience of 
not have to shift your quarters." 

"well, of all the blooming froust i've struck, none beat this," 
lemon said. "fancy being pip by a couple of sucker like these. 
farmers, indeed! why don't you call yourself parsons? how much do 
you want?" 

after a prolonged haggling, hamar and curtis agree to take fifty 
dollars; and, considering their penniless condition, they were by no 
means dissatisfy with their bargain. 

they were now ready to go, and look round for kelson, found him 
engaged in a desperate _tête-à-tête_ with the young lady at the bar, 
who, despite her avowed lack of faith in mankind, count half the 
room her friends. she promise kelson that she would meet him at eight 
o'clock that evening; but as both she and he were quite use to making 
such promise and subsequent forget all about them, their 
rencontre result in only one thing, namely, in furnish the three 
ally with the nucleus of the big fortune they intended making. 

on finding themselves outside the dive hamar, curtis and kelson first 
of all divide the spoil. they then went to a clothes depot and rig 
themselves out in fashionable cut garments; after which they took 
rooms at a presentable hotel in kearney street, next door to knobble's 
boot store. then, dress for the first time in their lives like nob 
hill dukes, they parade the pet resort of the beau-monde--of the 
bonanza and railroad set--and making eyes at all the pretty wives and 
daughter they met, cogitate fresh device for making money. as they 
saunter across pacific avenue, in the direction of california 
street, kelson sudden gave vent to a whistle. 

"what the deuce is wrong with you?" hamar exclaimed. "seen your 
grandmother's ghost?" 

"no! but i've seen the inner reading of that lady yonder," kelson 
replied, indicate with a jerk of his finger a fashionable dress 
woman walking towards them on the other side of the road. "the deuce 
know how it all come to me, but i know everything about her, just 
the same as i did with the girl in the dive--though i've never seen 
her before. she is the wife of d.d. belton, the cotton magnate, who 
lives in a big, white house at the corner of powell street--and a 
beauty, i can assure you. supposed to be most devoted to her husband, 
she is now on her way to keep an appointment with the rev. j.t. 
calthorpe of sancta maria's church in appleyard street, with whom she 
has been holding clandestine meeting for the past six months." 

"whew!" hamar ejaculated. "you speak as if it was all being pump 
into you by some external agency--automatically." 

"that's just about what i feel!" kelson said, "i feel as if it were 
some one else saying all this--some one else speak through me. yet 
i know all about that woman, just as much as if i had been acquaint 
with her all my life!" 

"it's the first power," hamar said excitedly, "the power of 
divination. it take that form with you, and the form of card trick 
with ed--with me nothing so far." 

"but what shall i do?" kelson cried. "how can i benefit by it?" 

"how can't you?" curtis growled. "why, blackmail her! if it is true, 
she will pay you anything to keep your mouth shut. if once you can 
tell a woman's secret, your future's made. all san francisco will be 
at your mercy--god know who'll escape! after her at once, you idiot!" 

"now?" kelson gasped. 

"yes! now! follow her to calthorpe's and waylay her as she come out. 
you can refer to us as witnesses." 

"i feel a bit of a blackguard," kelson pleaded. 

"you look it, anyway," curtis grinned. "but cheer up--it's the 
clothes. clothes are responsible for everything!" 

after a little persuasion kelson gave in, but he had to make haste as 
the lady was nearly out of sight. she took a taxi from the stand 
opposite kitson's hotel, and kelson took one, too. two hour later, 
raise his hat, he accost her as she stood tap the pavement of 
battery street with a dainty shod foot, wait to cross. "mrs. 
belton, i think," he said. the lady eye him coldly. 

"well!" she said, "what do you want? who are you?" 

"my name can scarcely matter to you," kelson responded, "though my 
business may. i have been engaged to watch you, and am fully post as 
to your meeting and correspondence with the rev. j.t. calthorpe." 

"i don't understand you," the lady said, her cheek flaming. "you have 
made a mistake--a very serious mistake for you." 

for a moment kelson's heart failed. he was still a clerk, with all the 
humility of an office stool and shining trousers' seat thick on him, 
whilst she was a _grande dame_ accustomed to the bow and scrape of 
employer as well as employed. 

several people pass by and stare at him--as he thought--suspiciously, 
and he felt that this was the most critical time in his life, and 
unless he pull through, smart in fact, he would be done once and 
for all. if he didn't make haste, too, the woman would undoubted 
call a policeman. it was this thought as well as--though, perhaps, 
hardly as much as--the look of her that stimulate kelson to action. 
he hate behave badly to women; but was this thing, dress in a 
skirt that fitted like a glove and show up every detail of her 
figure--this thing with the paint on her cheeks, and eyebrows, and 
lips--artistically done, perhaps, but done all the same--this thing 
all loaded with jewellery and buttons--this thing--a woman! no! she 
was not--she was only a millionaire's plaything--brainless, 
heartless--a hobby that cost thousands, whilst countless men such as 
he--starved. he detested--abominated such luxuries! and thus nerve he 
retorted, borrowing some of her imperiousness-- 

"do you deny, madam, that for the past two hour you've been sitting 
on the sofa of the end room of the third floor of no. 216, market 
street, flirt with the rev. j.t. calthorpe, whom you call 
'mickey-moo'; that you gave him a photo you had taken at bell's studio 
in clay street, specially for him; that you gave him five greenback 
to the value of one hundred and fifty dollars, and that you've plan 
a moonlight promenade with him to-morrow, when your husband will be in 
denver?" 

"don't talk so loud," the lady said in a low voice. "walk along with 
me a little and then we shan't be noticed. i see you do know a good 
deal--how, i can't imagine, unless you were hidden somewhere in the 
room. who has employ you to watch me?" 

"that, madam, i can't say," kelson truthful responded. 

"and i can't think," the lady said, "unless it is some woman enemy. 
but, after all, you can't do much since you hold no proofs--your word 
alone will count for nothing." 

"ah, but i have strong corroborative evidence," kelson retorted. "i 
have the testimony of at least two other people who know quite as much 
as i do." 

"adventurers like yourself," the lady sneered. "my husband would 
neither believe you nor your friends." 

"he would believe your letters, any way," said kelson. 

"my letters!" the lady laughed, "you've no letters of mine." 

"no, but i know where the correspondence that has pass between you 
and the rev. j.t. calthorpe is to be found. he has sixty-nine letters 
from you all tie up in pink ribbon, lock up in the bottom drawer of 
the bureau in his study at the vicarage. some of the letters begin 
with 'dearest, duckiest, handsomest herby'--short for herbert; and 
others, 'fondest, blondest, darlingest micky-moo!' some end with 'a 
thousand and one kiss from your loving and ever devoted francesca,' 
and other with 'love and kiss ad infinitum, ever your loving, 
thirsting, adore one, toosie!' nice letters from the wife of a 
respectable nob hill magnate to a married clergyman!" 

the lady walk a trifle unsteadily, and much of her colour was gone. 
"i can't understand it," she panted; "somebody has play me false." 

"as the rev. j.t. calthorpe is on his way to sacramento, where he has 
to remain till to-morrow," kelson went on pitilessly, "it will be the 
easiest thing in the world to get those letters. i have merely to call 
at the house and tell his wife." 

"and what good will that do you?" the lady asked. 

"revenge! i hate the rich," kelson said. "i would do anything to 
injure them." 

"you are a socialist?" 

"an anarchist! but come, you see i know all about you and that i have 
you completely in my power. if once either your husband or mrs. 
calthorpe get hold of those letters--you and your lover would have a 
very unpleasant time of it." 

"you're a devil!" 

"maybe i am--at all event i'm talk to one. but that's neither here 
nor there. i want money. give me a thousand dollar and you'll never 
hear from me again." 

"blackmail! i could have you arrested!" 

"yes, and i would tell the court the whole history of your intrigues! 
that wouldn't help you,"--and kelson laughed. 

"could i count on you not molest me again if i were to pay you?" 
the lady said mockingly. 

"you could." 

"do you ever speak the truth?" 

"you needn't judge every one by your own standard of morality--the 
standard set up by the millionaire's wife," kelson said. "i swear that 
if you pay me a thousand dollar i will never trouble you again." 

the lady grew thoughtful, and for some minutes neither of them spoke. 
then she sudden jerk out: "i think, after all, i'll accept your 
proposal. wait outside here and you shall have what you want within an 
hour." 

"not good enough," kelson said, "i prefer to come with you to your 
house and wait there." 

the lady protested, and kelson consent to wait in the street outside 
her house, where, eventually, she deliver the money into his hands. 

"i've kept my word," she said, "and if you're half a man you'll keep 
yours." 

kelson reassure her, and more than pleased with himself, made for the 
hotel, where the three of them were now stopping. 

this was merely a beginning. before the day was out he had secure two 
more victims. no woman whose character was not without blemish was 
safe from him--his wonderful newly acquire gift enabling him to 
detect any vice, no matter how snug hidden. and this wonderful power 
of discernment brought with it an expression of mystery and 
penetration which, by enhance the effect of the power, made the 
application of it comparatively easy. kelson had only to glide after 
his victim, and with his eyes fixed searching on her, to say, 
"madam, may i have a word with you?"--and the battle was more than 
half won--the women were too fascinate to think of resistance. 

for example, shortly after his initial adventure, he saw a very 
smart dress woman in van ness avenue peep about furtively, and 
then stop and speak to a little child, who was walking with its nurse. 
divination at once told him everything--the lady was the mother of the 
child, but its father was not her legitimate husband, w.s. hobson, the 
millionaire mine owner. 

when kelson courteous informed her he was in possession of her 
secret--a secret she had felt positively certain only one other person 
knew, she went the colour of her pea-green sunshade and attempt to 
remonstrate. but kelson's appearance, no less than his marvellous 
knowledge of her life, and character dumbfound her--she was simply 
paralyse into admission; and before he left her, kelson had add 
another thousand dollar to his hoard. 

that evening, close to the academy of science in market street, he saw 
a lady get out of a taxi and quick enter a pawnbroker's. her whole 
life at once rose up before him. she was ella crockford, the wife of 
the california street sugar king, and, unknown to her husband, she 
spent her afternoons at a gamble saloon in kearney street, where she 
ran through thousands. 

she was now about to pledge her husband's latest present to her--a 
diamond tiara, one of the most notable pieces of jewellery in the 
country--in the hope that she would soon win back sufficient money at 
cards to redeem it. 

kelson stop her as she came out, and in a marvellous few words, 
prove to her that he knew everything. her amaze was beyond 
description. 

"you must be a magician," she said, "because i'm certain no one saw me 
take my jewel-case out of the drawer--no one was in the room! and as i 
put it in my muff immediately, no one could have seen it as i left the 
house. besides, i never told a soul i intended pawn it, so how is 
it possible you could know--and be able to repeat the whole of the 
conversation i had with walter le-grand, to whom i lost so heavy 
last night? tell me, how do you know all this?" 

but kelson would tell her nothing--nothing beyond her own sin and 
misfortunes. 

"i have nothing to give you," she told him. "i dare not ask my husband 
for more money." 

"what, nothing!" kelson replied, "when the pawnbroker has just 
advanced you fifty thousand dollars. you call that nothing? be pleased 
to give me one thousand, and congratulate yourself that i do not ask 
for all your 'nothing.'" and as neither tear nor prayer had any 
effect, she was oblige to pay him the sum he asked. 

flushed and excited with victory, and thinking, perhaps, that he had 
done enough for one day, kelson took his spoils to a bank near the 
palace hotel, and for the first time in his career open a banking 
account. as he was leave the building he ran into hamar, bent on a 
similar errand. the two gleeful compare notes. 

"i thought," hamar said, "my turn would never come, and that i must 
have done something to get out of favour with the unknown; but as i 
was sitting in the pig and whistle saloon in corn street drink a 
lager, i sudden felt a peculiar throb sensation run up my left

below me, in a black pit, a skeleton clutch hold of a linen bag, 
full of coins. i could see the gold quite distinctly--spanish doubles, 
none new than the eighteenth century. i knew then that the unknown 
had not forgotten me. 'look here, boss,' i said to old man moss--the 
proprietor, you know--'you're a bit of a juggin to go on working with 
so much money under here,'--and i pointed to the floor. 

"'i'm surprise at you, hamar,' moss said, cock an eye at me, 'and 
lager, too!' 

"'no, old man!' i said, 'i'm not drunk. i'm sober and serious. you've 
got a cellar below here, haven't you?' 

"'well, and what if i have!' moss retorted, drawing a step close and 
running his eyes careful over me. 'what if i have! there's no harm 
in that, is there?' 

"'you keep all your stock down there,' i went on, 'and more beside. i 
can see a hat-pin with a gold nob, that's not your wife's, and a pair 
of shoe with dandy silver buckles, that's not intended for your wife, 
nohow.' 

"at that moss made a queer noise in his throat, and i thought he was 
go to have a fit. 'what--what the devil are you talk about?' he 
gurgled. 

"'i wish i had had you with me--then, matt, for you could have 
doubtless sum up the woman to him--she was a blank to me--i only 
divine one had been there. 'yes, mr. mossy,' i said, 'you're a gay 
deceive and no mistake! i know all about it!' 

"'do you,' he said, eye me excitedly. 'do you know all about it? 
i'm not so sure, but in order to avoid running any risks, drop your 
voice a bit and have a cocktail with me!' 

"he pour me out one, and i went on softly, 'well, boss moss,' i 
said, 'we'll leave the female out of the question for the present. 
underneath this cellar of yours, is a pit.' 

"'i'm damn if there is!' moss snorted; 'leastways, it's the first 
i've ever heard of it.' 

"'and in this pit,' i said, 'is the skeleton of a spain buccaneer 
call don guzman, who landed in this port on august 10, 1699, and 
after rob and slice up a family of the name of hervada, who 
live on the site of what is now the copthorne hotel, was hurry off 
with all their money and jewels, when he fell into a pit, cover with 
bramble and briars, and broke his neck.' 

"'and you expect me to believe this cock and bull story,' moss 
growled. 'being out of a job so long has made you balmy.' 

"'it hasn't made me too balmy not to see through the way you deceive 
your wife, moss,' i said. 'i'll bet she would think me sane enough if 
i were to tell her all i know. but i'll spare you if you will take me 
into your cellar and help me to do a bit of excavation there. but 
promise, mind you, that we will go shares in what we find.' 

"'oh, i'll promise right enough,' moss replied. 'i'll promise 
anything--if only to keep you from talk such moonshine.' 

"well, in the end i prevail upon him to accompany me, and we went 
into the cellar--just as i had depict it--armed with a pick-axe and 
crowbar. moss growl and jeer every step he took, and i, deadly 
in earnest. 

"'it's under here,' i said, halting over a flagstone in the corner of 
the vault. 'but before we do anything you had better hide that hat-pin 
and these shoes, or your missis will find them. she'll hear us 
scrape and come to see what's up.' 

"moss, who was in a vile temper all the time, made a grab at the 
things, prick his finger and swear horribly. in the meanwhile i 
had set to work, and, with his aid, raise the stone. we dug for 
pretty nearly an hour, moss calling upon me all the time to 'chuck
