close beside it, was the bag. you should have seen moss then. he was 
simply overcome--called me a wizard, a magician, and heaven alone 
know what, and fairly stood on his head with delight when we open 
the bag, and hundred of gold coin and precious stone roll out on 
the floor. he want to go back on his word then, and only give me a 
handful; but i was too smart for him, and swore i would tell his wife 
about the girl unless he gave me half. when we were leave the 
cellar, of course, he want me to go first, so that he could follow 
with the pickaxe, but here again i was too sharp for him--and i got 
safe out of the place with my pocket bulging. i went right away to 
prescott's in clay street, and let the lot go for three thousand 
dollars. i wonder how curtis has got on!" 

they walk together to the hotel, and found curtis busy engaged 
eating. "i've work hard," he said, "and now i'm in for enjoy 
myself. i've made them get out a special menu for me, and i'm go to 
eat till i can't hold another morsel. i've starve all my life and now 
i intend making up for it." 

"been successful?" hamar asked, wink at kelson. 

"pretty well! nothing to grumble at," curtis rejoined, pour himself 
out a glass of champagne. "first of all i went to simpson's dive in 
sacramento street, and start do the trick we discover 
yesterday. not a soul in the place could see through them, and i made 
about two hundred dollar before i left. i then had lunch." 

"why you had lunch with us!" hamar laughed. 

"well, can't i have as many lunch as i like?" curtis replied. "i had 
lunch, i say, at a place in market street, and there i read in a paper 
that peters & pervis, the tin food people, were offering a prize of 
three thousand dollar for a solution to a puzzle contained on the 
inside cover of one of their tins. i immediately determined to enter 
for it. i bought a tin and saw through the puzzle at once. bribe a 
policeman to go with me to see fair play, off i set to peters & 
pervis'. 

"'i want to see your boss,' i said to the first clerk i saw. 

"'which of them?' the clerk grunted, his cheek turning white at the 
sight of the policeman. 

"'either will do,' i replied, 'peters or pervis. trot 'em up, time is 
precious.' 

"away he went, but in a couple of minutes was back again, look 
scared, 'they're both engaged,' he says. 

"'then they'll have to break it off,' i responded, 'and mighty quick. 
i'm here to talk with them, so get a move on you again and give that 
message.' 

"if it hadn't been for the policeman i don't think he would have gone, 
but the policeman back me up, and the clerk hurried off again; and 
in the end the boss decided they had better see me. they look 
precious cross, i can assure you, but before i had done speak they 
look cross still. 

"'you say you've done that puzzle,'--they shouted--'the puzzle that 
has stuck all the mathematics gun at harvard and yale--you--a 
nonentity like you--begone, sir, don't waste our time with such humbug 
as that.' 

"'all right,' i said, 'give me some paper and a pen, and i'll prove 
it.' 

"'that's very reasonable,' the policeman chip in, 'do the thing 
fair and square--i'm here as a witness.' 

"well, with much grunt and grumbling they hand me paper and ink, 
and in a trice the puzzle was done; and it appear so easy that the 
policeman clap his hand and broke out into a loud guffaw. my eyes! 
you should have seen how the face of pervi and peters fell, and have 
heard what they said. but it was no use swear and cursing, the 
thing was done, and there was the policeman to prove it. 

"'we'll give you five hundred dollars,' they said, 'to clear out and 
say no more about it.' 

"'five hundred dollar when you've advertise three thousand,' i 
cried. 'what do you take me for? i'll have that three thousand or i'll 
show you both up.' 

"'a thousand, then?' they said. 

"'no!' i retorted; 'three! three, and look sharp. and look here,' i 
added, as my glance rest on some of the sample of their paste they 
had round them, 'i understand the secret of all these so-called 
patent of yours--there isn't one of them i couldn't imitate. take 
that "rabsidab," for instance. what is it? why, a compound of 
horseflesh, turnip and popcorn, flavour with lazenby's sauce--for 
the infringe of which patent you are liable to prosecution--and 
coloured with cochineal. then there's the stuff you label 
"ironcastor,"'--but they shut me up. 'there, take your three thousand 
dollars, write us out a receipt for it, and clear.'" 

"nine thousand dollar in one day! we've done well," kelson 
ejaculated. "what's the programme for to-morrow?" 

"same as to-day and plenty of it," curtis said, pour himself out 
another glass of champagne and making a vigorous attack on a chicken. 
"i think i'll let you two fellow do all the work to-morrow, and 
content myself here. waiter! what time's breakfast?" 




chapter vii 

san francisco ladies and divination 


curtis was as good as his word. the following day he remain indoors 
eating, and plan what he should eat, whilst hamar and kelson went 
out with the express purpose of add to their banking accounts. 

in a garden in bryant street, hamar saw a man rest on his spade and 
mop the perspiration from his forehead. as he stop mechanical 
to see what was being done, a cold sensation ran up his right leg into 
his right hand, the first and third finger of which were drawn 
violent down. with a cry of horror he shrank back. directly beneath 
where he had been standing, he saw, under a fifteen or sixteen feet 
layer of gravel soil--water; a huge caldron of water, black and 
silent; water, that gave him the impression of tremendous depth and 
coldness. 

"hulloa! matey, what's the matter?" the man with the spade call out. 
"are you look for your skin, for i never saw any one so completely 
jump out of it?" 

"so would you," hamar said with a shudder, "if you saw what i do!" 

"what's that, then?" the man said leer on the ground. "snakes! 
that's what i always see when i've got them." 

"so long as you don't see yourself, there's some chance for you!" 
hamar retorted. "what make you so hot?" 

"why, digging!" the man laughed; "any one would get hot digging at 
such hard ground as this. as for a little whippersnapper like you, 
you'd melt right away and only your nose would remain. nothing would 
ever melt that--there's too much of it." 

hamar scowled. "you needn't be insulting," he said, "i ask you a 
civil question, and i repeat it. what make you so hot--when you 
should be cold--or at least cool?" 

"oh, should i!" the man mimicked, "i thought first you was merely 
drunk; i can see quite clearly now that you're mad." 

"and yet you have such defective sight." 

"what make you say that?" the man said testily. 

"why," hamar responded, "because you can't see what lie beneath your 
very nose. shall i tell you what it is?" 

"yes, tell away," the man replied, "tell me my old mother's got twins, 
and that boss croker is coming to lodge with us. i'd know you for a 
liar anywhere by those teeth of yours." 

"look here," said hamar drawing himself up angrily, "i have had enough 
of your abuse. if i have any more i'll tell your employers. it is 
evident you take me for a bummer, but see,"--and plunge his hand in 
his pocket he pull it out full of gold. "kindly understand i'm 
somebody," he went on, "and that i'm stay at one of the biggest 
hotel in the town." 

"i'm damn if i know what to make of you," the man muttered, "unless 
you're a hoptic delusion!" 

"underneath where i was standing--just here,"--and hamar indicate the 
spot--"is water. any amount of it, you have only to sink a shaft 
fifteen feet and you would come to it." 

"water!" the man laughed, "yes, there is any amount of it--on your 
brain, that's the only water near here." 

"then you don't believe me?" hamar demanded. 

"not likely!" the man responded, "i only believe what i see! and when 
i see a face like yours holding out a potful of dollars, i know as how 
you've stolen them. git!"--and hamar flew. 

but hamar was not so easily nonplussed; not at least when he saw a 
chance of making money. enter the garden, and keeping well out of 
sight of the gardener, he arrive at the front door by a side path, and 
with much formality request to see the owner of the establishment. 
the latter happening to be crossing the hall at the time, heard hamar 
and ask what he wanted. 

hamar at once informed him he was a dowser, and that, chance to pass 
by the garden on his way to his hotel, he had divine the presence of 
water. 

"i only wish there were," the gentleman exclaimed, "but i fear you are 
mistaken. i have attempt several times to sink a well but never with 
the slightest degree of success. i have had all the ground careful 
prospect by figgin of sacramento street--he has a very big 
reputation--and he assure me there isn't a drop of water anywhere 
near here within two hundred feet of the surface." 

"i know better," hamar said. "will you get your gardener--who by the 
way was very rude to me just now when i spoke to him--to dig where i 
tell him. i have absolute confidence in my power of divination." 

the owner of the property, whom i will call mr. b. assented, and 
several gardeners, including the one who had so insult hamar, were 
soon digging vigorously. at the depth of fifteen feet, water was 
found, and, indeed, so fast did it begin to come in that within a few 
minutes it had risen a foot. the onlooker were jubilant. 

"i shall send an account of it to the local papers," mr. b. remarked. 
"your fame will be spread everywhere. you have increase the value of 
my property a thousandfold, i cannot tell you how grateful i am"--and 
he, then and there, invite hamar to luncheon. 

after luncheon mr. b. made him a present of a cheque--rather in excess 
of the sum which hamar had all along intended to have, and could not 
have refrain from demanding much longer. 

in the afternoon all the san francisco special were full of the 
incident, and hamar, seeing his name placard for the first time, was 
so overcome that he spent the rest of the evening in the hotel 
deliberate how he could best turn his sudden notoriety to account. 

at ten o'clock kelson came in, look somewhat fatigued, but, 
nevertheless, pleased. he, too, had had adventures, and he detailed 
them with so much elaboration that the other two had frequent to 
tell him to "dry up." 

"i began the morning," he commenced, "by accost a very fashionable 
dress lady coming out of bushwell's store in commercial street. 
divination at once told me she was the popular widow of j.k. bater, 
the biscuit king of nob hill, and that she was carry in her big 
seal-skin muff a gold hatpin mount with an emerald butterfly, a 
silver-backed hair brush, a blue enamel scent bottle, and a 
porcelain jar, all of which she had sly 'nicked,' when no one was 
looking. 

"i step up to her, and polite raise my hat said, 'good morning, 
mrs. bater. i've a message for you.' 

"'i don't know you,' she said eye me very doubtfully, 'who are 
you?' 

"'forgotten!' i said tragically, 'and i had flatter myself it would 
be otherwise. still i must try and survive. i want to ask you a 
favour, mrs. bater.' 

"'a favour!' she exclaim nervously, 'what is it? you are really a 
very extraordinary individual.' 

"'i was only go to ask if i might examine the contents of your 
muff? i think you have certain articles in it that have not been paid 
for--and i believe i am right in saying this is by no means the first 
time such a thing has happened.' 

"she turn so pale i thought she was go to faint. 'why, whatever 
do you mean,' she stammered, 'i've nothing that do not belong to 
me.' 

"'opinions differ on that score, mrs. bater,' i replied, 'you have a 
pin, a hair brush, a scent bottle and a jar,' and i describe them 
each minutely, 'whilst in your house you have on your dressing-table a 
silver-backed clothes brush, a silver manicure set you kleptomaniad--if 
you prefer to call it so--from deacon's in sacramento street; a 
tortoiseshell manicure set, and an ivory card case you obtain in the 
same manner from varter's in market street; a set of silver buttons, a 
glove stretcher, and a mauve pin-cushion--you likewise help yourself 
to--from selter's in kearney street; but i might go on detail them 
to you till further orders, for your house is literally cram with 
them. you have done very well, mrs. bater, with the san francisco 
storekeepers.' 

"'good god, man, what are you?' she gasped. 'you seem to read into the 
innermost recess of my soul, and to know everything.' 

"'you are right, madam,' i said, try to appear very stern and 
almost failing, she was so pretty. by jove! you fellows, i wonder i 
didn't kiss her; she had such fine eyes, my favourite nose, a rip 
mouth and--" 

"oh! go on! go on with your story. never mind her looks," curtis 
interrupted, "i've got a touch of indigestion." 

"as i was saying," kelson went on complacently, "i could have kiss 
her and i felt downright mean for upset her so. 

"'now you have found me out,' she said, 'what do you intend doing? 
show me up in there?' and she pointed shuddering at the store. 

"'no,' i said, 'not if you are sensible and come to terms. i will 
agreeto say nothing about either this or any of your other--ahem!-- 
thefts--if you let me escort you home, and write me out a cheque for 
a thousand dollars!' 

"'beast!' she hissed, 'so you are a blackmailer!' 

"'a black beetle if you like,' i responded, 'but i assure you, mrs. 
bater, i am letting you off cheap. i have only to call for a policeman 
and your reputation would be gone at once. besides, i know other 
things about you.' 

"'what other things?' she stuttered. 

"'well, madam!' i replied, 'some things are rather delicate--er--for 
single men like me to mention, but i do know that--er--a lady--very 
like--remarkably like--you, has in her pocket at this moment a rattle 
which she bought and paid for in oakland's late last night. and as, 
madam, mr. bate has been dead over two years--let me see--yes, two 
years yesterday--one can--!' 

"'stay! that will do,' she whispered; 'come to my house and i will 
give you the thousand dollars. you must pretend you are my cousin.' 

"'i will pretend anything, mrs. bater,' i murmured, helping her into a 
taxi, 'anything so long as i can be with you.'" 

"you got the money?" hamar queried. 

"yes," kelson said with a smile, "i got the money--in fact, everything 
i ask for." 

there was silence for some minutes, and then hamar said, "what next?" 

"what next!" kelson said, "why i thought i had done a very good day's 
work and was on my way back here to take a much need rest, when i'm 
dashed if the unknown hadn't another adventure in store for me. coming 
out of a garden in gough street, within sight of goad's house, was a 
lady, young and very plain, but rig out in one of those latest 
fashion costumes--a very tight, short skirt, and huge hat with high 
plume in it. by the bye, i can't think why this costume, which is so 
admirable suit to pretty girls--because it attract attention to 
them--should be almost exclusively adopt by the ugly ones. but to 
continue. i knew immediately that she was ella barlow, the 
much-pampered and only daughter of j.b. barlow, the vinegar magnate; 
that she was in love, or imagine herself in love with herbert delmas, 
the manager of the columbian bank--a young, good-looking fellow, whom 
she had been try to set against his fiancée, dora roberts. dora is 
only nineteen, very pretty and a trifle giddy--nothing more. but this 
failing of hers--if you can call it a failing, was just the very 
weapon ella barlow wanted. she work on it at once, and by send 
delma a series of anonymous letters made him mad with jealousy. this 
result in a breach between delma and dora, and ella barlow, much 
elated, at once try to step into her shoes. she has been go out a 
good deal with delmas, who is in reality still very much in love with 
dora, and consequently exceedingly miserable. this morning ella, 
anxious to show off a magnificent set of diamonds, given her by her 
father, telephone to delma to take her to the baldwyn theatre, where 
she has engaged a box for this evening--fondly hope that the 
diamond will bring him up to the scratch, and that he will propose to 
her. when i saw her she was on her way to a notorious quack doctor and 
beauty specialist in california street. she suffer from some nasty 
skin disease, and is in mortal terror lest delma should get to know 
of it, and also of the fact that all her teeth are false, and that two 
of her toe are badly deformed." 

"by jupiter!" hamar ejaculated, "this divination of yours beat mine 
into fits--nothing escape you!" 

"no!" kelson laughed, "nothing! ella barlow, metaphysics and physical 
was laid before me just as bare as if the almighty had got hold of her 
with his dissect knife. i saw everything--and what is more i said 
to myself--here's plenty i can turn to a profitable account. well! i 
didn't stop her--i let her go." 

"let her go!" curtis growled, his mouth full of almond and raisins. 
"you squirrel!" 

"only for a time," kelson said, "i went to see delmas!" 

"delmas!" hamar interlocuted, "why the deuce delmas?" 

"impulse!" kelson explained, "purely impulse." 

"yes, but impulse is often a dangerous thing!" hamar said, "it is 
essential for us three, especially, to be on our guard against 
impulse. what did you get out of delmas?" 

"nothing!" kelson said look rather shamefaced, "but the matter 
hasn't end yet. i'm go to the theatre after i've had something to 
eat. i'll tell you what happens, to-morrow." 

it was late ere kelson came down to breakfast the following day, and 
hamar and curtis were comfortable seat in armchair reading the 
_examiner_, when he join them. 

"well!" hamar said, look up at him, "what luck?" 

but kelson wouldn't say a word till he had finished eating. he then 
loll back in his seat and began:-- 

"arriving at the baldwyn i went straight to box one. a tall figure 
rose to greet me, and then, an angry voice exclaimed, 'why it's not 
herbert! who are you, sir? do you know this box is engaged?' 

"'i humble beg your pardon, miss barlow,' i said, 'i do know it is 
engaged, but i came as mr. delmas' deputy and friend.' 

"'came as herbert's deputy and friend,' ella barlow repeated--and by 
jove the diamond did shine--she was simply a mass of them, hair, 
neck, arms and fingers--and she had been so well fake up for the 
occasion that she was almost good-looking; but i thought of all i knew 
about her--and shuddered. 

"'i will explain myself,' i said, 'mr. delma telephone to you this 
afternoon, did he not?' 

"she nodded. 

"'saying that he very much regret he could not leave business in 
time to escort you here. would you mind very much go by yourself, 
and he would join you as soon as possible.' 

"'yes,' ella barlow said, 'he told me all that.' 

"'very well, then,' i went on, 'he rang me up some minutes later and 
ask me if i would take his place for the first hour or so, and he 
would be here by the end of the first act.' 

"'but it is most unheard of,' ella barlow ejaculated, 'i don't know 
you--i've never seen you before!' 

"'that is, of course, very regrettable,' i said, 'but i will do all i 
can for the past. i've something to say that i'm sure will interest 
you. have i your permission?'--and without wait for her reply i sat 
next to her. the box was a big one, big enough to hold half a dozen 
people, and we sat in the extreme front of it. the lights were not 
full up, as the orchestra had not start playing. i kept her 
attention fixed on my face so that she was unaware what was taking 
place, immediately behind her. 

"'what is it?' she said, 'whatever can you have to say that can be of 
any possible interest to me?' 

"'why,' i replied, 'to begin with i know something about your 
character!' 

"'then you're a fortune teller!' she exclaim eagerly, 'can you read 
hands?' 

"'i can read everything,' i said look hard at her, 'hands, head, 
and feet. i am psychometrist, dentist, physician, metaphysician all in 
one!' 

"'i don't understand,' she said look queer, 'what is the meaning of 
all this?' 

"'it means,' i said slowly, 'that i have discover who sent those 
anonymous letters to herbert delmas!' 

"'anonymous letters! how dare you!' she cried, 'what have anonymous 
letters to do with me?' 

"'a very great deal, madam,' i replied, 'shall i remind you of their 
contents and the occasion on which you wrote them?' i did so. i 
recite every word in them and told her the hour, day and 
place--namely, when and where each was written, and i sum up by 
ask what she would pay me not to tell delmas. 

"for some minutes she was too overcome to say anything; she sat grim 
and silent, her pale eyes glaring at me, her freckle finger toy 
with the diamonds. she was baffle and perplexed--she did not know 
what course to pursue! 

"'well,' i repeated, 'what have you to say? do you deny it?' 

"she rouse herself with an effort. 'no,' she said venomously, 'i 
don't deny it. denial would be useless. how did you find out? through 
one of the maids, i suppose. they were bribe to spy on me!' 

"'how i discover it is of no consequence,' i said, 'but what is of 
consequence to you as much as to me--is the payment for hush it 
up!' 

"'payment!' she cried, raise her voice to a positive shriek in her 
excitement, 'pay _you_--you nasty, beastly, cadge toad. you--' but i 
can't repeat all she said, it would make you both blush! i let her go 
on till she had worn herself out and then i said, 'well, miss barlow, 
why all this fuss--why these fireworks! it can't do you any good. we 
must come to business soon or later. if you don't pay me handsome 
i shall tell miss robert as well as mr. delmas.' 

"'mr. delma won't believe you,' she hissed, 'you've no proof at 
all!' 

"'perhaps not,' i said, 'but i've proof of this. i know you have two 
deform toe on your left foot, that all your teeth are false, and 
that you go to that charlatan, howard prince, in california street to 
be fake up. i must be brutal--it's no use being anything else to 
women of your sort. you've got a certain species of eczema, and you 
flatter yourself that no one but you and prince are aware of it. what 
have you got to say now, miss barlow?' but ella barlow had fainted. 
when she came to, which i manage after vigorous application of salts 
and water--the effects of the latter on her complexion i leave you to 
imagine--i again broach the subject. 

"'what is it you propose?' she said feebly. 

"'why this,' i said, 'you hand me over all those diamonds, and your 
defect will--as far as i am concerned--always remain a secret. 
refuse, and miss robert and mr. delma shall know all there is to be 
known at once.' 

"for some minutes she sat with her face bury in her 
hands--shivering. then she look up at me--and jerusalem! it was like 
look at an old woman. 'take them,' she said, 'take them! i shall 
never wear them again, anyhow. take them--and leave me.' 

"well, you fellows, i steel my heart, and slip every jack one 
that was on her into my pocket. 

"'you won't tell them,' she whispered, catching hold of me by the arm, 
'you swear you won't.' i won't try and remember exactly what i 
answered--but outside the door of the box delma join me. he had 
been conceal within and had heard everything that passed. 

"'i can't say how grateful i am to you,' he said. 'it's a bit low 
down, perhaps, but, then, we were dealing with a low-down person. you 
thorough deserve those diamonds--will you accept an offer for them 
from me? i should like to buy them for miss robert and present them 
to her on our reconciliation.' we came to terms then and there, and he 
'phoned through to me an hour ago to say that he had made it up with 
miss roberts, that she was delight with the diamonds, and that they 
are go to be married next month." 

"so out of evil good comes," hamar said, "the maxim for us, remember, 
is--out of evil evil alone must come. what are you go to do to-day, 
you two?" 

"rest!" said kelson, "i'm tired." 

"eat!" said curtis, "i'm hungry!" 

"now look here, this won't do," hamar remarked, "you've earn your 
rest, matt, but you haven't, ed. you can't go on eat eternally." 

"can't i?" curtis snapped, "i'm not so sure of that, i've years to 
make up for." 

"then do the thing in moderation, for goodness sake!" hamar 
expostulated, "and recollect we must, at all costs, act together. we 
have now twelve thousand dollar between us in the bank--that is to 
say, the capital of the firm of hamar, curtis and kelson represent 
that amount. it is our ambition to increase that amount--and to go on 
increase it till we can fairly claim to be the richest firm in the 
world. now to do that we must work, and work hard, if we are to live 
at the pace ed is setting us--but there is no reason why we should 
remain here, and i propose that we move elsewhere. i've got a scheme 
in my head, rather a colossal one i admit, but not altogether 
impossible." 

"what is it?" kelson asked. 

"yes, out with it," curtis grunted. 

"it is this," hamar said, "i suggest that we go to london--london in 
england--i guess it's the richest town in the world--and there set up 
as sorcerers--the sorcery company ltd. we should begin with divination 
and juggling, and go on, according to the seven stages. we should of 
course sell our cure and spells, and there is not the slightest doubt 
but that we should make an enormous pile, with which we would 
gradual buy up, not merely london, but the whole of england." 

"that's rather a tall order," kelson murmured. 

"a small one, you mean," curtis sneered, "you could put the whole of 
england twice over in california, and from what i've heard i don't go 
much on london. i reckon it isn't much big than san francisco." 

"still you wouldn't mind being joint owner of it," hamar laughed." 

"no, perhaps not," curtis said rather dubiously. "i guess we could buy 
the crown and wear it in turn. sam westlake up at meidler's always 
use to say the britisher would sell their soul if any one bid high 
enough. they think of nothing but money over there. when shall we go?" 

"at the end of our week," hamar said, "that is to say on wednesday--in 
three days' time." 

"first class all the way, of course," curtis said, "i'll see to the 
arrangement for the cater and berths." 

"all right!" hamar laughed, as he fill three glasses with champagne. 
"here, drink, you fellows, 'long life, health and prosperity--to 
hamar, curtis and kelson, the modern sorcery company ltd.'" 




chapter viii 

two dream 


"do you believe in dreams?" glady martin inquired, as, fresh from a 
stroll in the garden, she join her aunt, miss templeton, in the 
breakfast room at pine cottage. 

"i believe in fairies," miss templeton rejoined, smile indulgent 
as she look at the fair face beside her. "what was the dream, 
dearie?" 

glady laugh a little mischievously. "i don't quite know whether i 
ought to tell you," she said. "it might shock you." 

"perhaps i'm not so easily shock as you imagine," miss templeton 
replied. "what was it?" 

"well!" glady began, fling both arms round her aunt's neck and 
play with the pleat in her blouse, "i dream that i was walking 
in the little wood at the end of the garden, and that the tree and 
flower walk and talk with me. and we dance together--and, first 
of all, i had for my partner, a red rose--and then, an ash. they both 
made love to me, and squeeze my waist with their hot, fibrous hands. 
a poppy piped, a bramble play the concertina, and a lilac grew 
desperate jealous of me and try to claw my hair. then the dance 
ceased, and i found myself in the midst of bluebell that shook their 
bell at me with loud trill of laughter. and out from among them, 
came a buttercup, point its yellow head at me. 'see! see,' it 
cried, 'what glady is carry behind her. naughty gladys!' and tree 
and flowers--everything around me--shook with laughter. then i grew 
hot and cold all over, and did not know which way to look for my 
confusion, till a willow, have compassion on me said, 'take no 
notice of them! they don't know any better.' 

"i beg him to explain to me why they were so amused, and he grew 
very embarrass and uncomfortable, and stammered--oh! so funnily, 
'well if you really wish to know--it's a bud, a baby white rose, and 
it's clinging to your dress.' 

"'a baby! a baby rose!' shriek all the flowers. 

"'and it means,' a bluebell said, step perky out from amidst 
its fellows, 'that your lover is coming--your lover with a 
troll-le-loll-la--and--well, if you want to know more ask the 
gooseberries, the gooseberry that hang on the bushes, or the parsley 
that grow in the bed,'--and at that all the flower and tree 
shriek with laughter--'ta-ta-tra-la-la'--and with my ear full of 
the rude laughter of the wood i awoke. what do you think of it? isn't 
it rather a quaint mixture of the--of the sacred--at least the 
artistic--and the profane?" 

"quite so," said miss templeton with an amuse chuckle, "but i 
shouldn't ask for an interpretation of it if i were you." 

"not for an interpretation of the tree and flowers?" glady ask 
innocently. "i'm sure tree and flower have a special significance in 
dreams." 

"very well then, my dear, ask mrs. sprat." 

"what! ask the vicar's wife!" glady ejaculated, "when i never go to 
church." 

"certainly," miss templeton replied, laugh again, "mrs. sprat will 
quite understand. and i've always been told she is very interested in 
anything to do with the occult. but hush! here's your father. you'd 
better not tell him your dream. he's tired to death, he says, of 
hearing about your lovers, and agree with me--there's no end to 
them." 

"never mind what he says--his bark's worse then his bite," glady 
rejoined, "he doesn't really care how many i have so long as they keep 
within bounds, and i like them! father!" 

john martin, who enter the room at that moment, went straight to his 
daughter to be kissed. 

"i wish you wouldn't always select that bald spot," he said testily, 
"i don't want to be everlastingly remind i'm lose my hair." 

"where do you want me to kiss you, then?" glady argued, "on the tip 
of your nose? that's all very well for you, john martin, but i prefer 
the top of your head. but the poor dear looks worried, what is it?" 

"i didn't have a very good night," her father replied, "i dream a 
lot!" glady look at miss templeton and laughed. 

"did you?" she said gently. "what a shame! i never dream. what was it 
all about?" 

"flowers!" john martin snapped, "idiotic flowers! roses, lilac, 
tulips! bah! i do wish you would have some other hobby." 

glady look at her aunt again, this time with a half serious, half 
questioning expression. 

"shall i be a politician?" she cooed, "and fill the house with 
suffragettes? you bad man, i believe you would revel in it. don't you 
think so, auntie?" 

"i think, instead of tease your father so unmercifully, you had 
better pour him out a cup of tea," miss templeton replied. "jack, 
there's a letter for you." 

"where? under my plate! what a place to put it. that's you," and john 
martin frowned, or rather, attempt to frown, at gladys. "why it's 
about davenport--dick davenport. he's very ill--had a stroke 
yesterday, and the doctor declare his condition critical. his nephew, 
shiel, so anne says, has been sent for, and arrive at sydenham last 
night! if that's not bad news i don't know what is!" john martin said, 
thrust his plate away from him and leaning back in his chair. "it's 
true i can manage the business all right myself--and there's the 
possibility, of course, that this young shiel may shape all right. i 
suppose if anything happen he will step into dick's shoes. i've never 
heard dick mention any one else. poor old dick!" 

"i am so sorry, father!" glady said, lay her hand on his. "but 
cheer up! it may not be as bad as you expect. shall you go and see how 
he is?" 

"i think so, my dear! i think so," john martin replied, "but don't 
worry me about it now. talk to your aunt and leave me out of it, i'm a 
bit upset. my brain's in a regular whirl!" 

undoubted the news was something in the nature of a blow: for dick 
davenport, apart from being john martin's partner--partner in the firm 
of martin and davenport, the world-renowned conjurors, whose hall in 
the kingsway was one of the chief amusement place in london, was john 
martin's oldest friend. they had been chum at cheltenham college, had 
enter the army and gone to india together, had quit the service 
together, and, on return together to england, had start their 
conjure business, first of all in sloane street, and subsequent in 
the kingsway. from the very start their enterprise had met with 
success, and, had it not been for davenport's wild extravagance, they 
would have been little short of millionaires. but davenport, though a 
most lovable character in every respect, could not keep money--he no 
soon had it than it was gone. his house in sydenham was little short 
of a palace; whilst, it was said, he almost rival royalty, in 
magnificent display, whenever he entertained. the result of all this 
reckless expenditure was no uncommon one--he ran through considerably 
more than he earn and--as there was no one else to help him--he 
invariable came down on john martin. it was "jack, old boy, i'm damn 
sorry, but i must have another thousand;" or, "jack! these infernal 
scamp of creditor are worry the life out of me, can you, will 
you, lend me a trifle--a couple of thousand will do it"--and so on--so 
on, ad infinitum. john martin never refused, and at the time of 
davenport's illness, the latter owe him something like a hundred 
thousand pounds. 

fortunately john martin, though far from parsimonious, was careful. he 
had an excellent business head, and, thanks to his sagacious share in 
the management, the business remain solvent. he knew davenport's 
capacity--that nowhere could he have found another such a brilliant 
genius in conjuring--nor, apart from his thriftlessness, any one so 
thorough reliable. in davenport's keeping all the great trick they 
had invented--and great trick they undoubted were--were absolutely 
safe. 

despite the fact that they had repeatedly offer big sum of money to 
any one who could discover the secret of how they were done, every 
attempt to do so had utterly failed. the mystery of martin and 
davenport's home of wonder, in the kingsway, baffle the world. of 
course one thing had help them enormously--namely, they had no 
rivals. so colossal was their reputation, that no one else had ever 
even thought of setting up in opposition. 

and now one of the two great master-minds, that had accomplished all 
these marvel and acquire such universal fame, was stricken down, 
checkmate by the still greater power of nature; and his 
colleague--the only other man in existence who share his 
knowledge--was oblige to rack his brain as to what was now to be 
done--done for the continuance and prosperity of the firm. 

after finish her breakfast glady join her aunt in the garden. 

"to dream of flower and tree evidently means bad news," she said. 
"but as i feel in a mood for a walk, i shall call at the vicarage." 

"what, now! at this hour!" miss templeton cry aghast. 

"why not?" glady said imperturbably. "i'm not go to pay a call. 
they haven't call on us. i shall say i've merely come to make an 
inquiry. can she tell me of any one who interpret dreams? come with 
me!" 

but as her aunt plead an excuse, glady went alone. 

the vicar was in the garden in his shirt sleeves, and though obviously 
surprise to see gladys, seem quite prepared to enter into 
conversation with her. but glady was not enamoured of clergymen. her 
ways were not their ways, and she had come strict on business. 
consequently she somewhat curt demand to be conduct into the 
presence of his wife, who received her very affably. 

"why, how very strange," she observe when glady had state the 
object of her visit. "i was ask a similar question only yesterday. a 
miss rosenberg, who is stay with us, had an extraordinary dream 
about tree and flowers--only it took the form of a poem, which she 
awoke repeating. there were several verses--quite doggerel it is 
true--but nevertheless rather remarkable for a dream. she wrote them 
down, and ask me if i could tell her whether there was any hidden 
meaning in them. here they are," and she hand glady two page of 
sermon paper on which was written-- 

"in the greenest of green valleys, 
aglow with summer sun, 
live a maiden fair and radiant, 
more radiant there was none. 

"the flower gave her their friendship; 
her couch was on the ground. 
a happier, gay maiden, 
was nowhere to be found. 

"the air was fill with music 
sung by the babble brook. 
sweet lullaby with chorus clear 
in which the flower partook. 

"this maiden knew not sorrow, 
until an evil day; 
when riding lone across the moors, 
a hunter lost his way. 

"and chance on this valley, 
he met the maiden sweet. 
her beauty overwhelm him; 
he fell love-sick at her feet. 

"despite the fervent warning 
of her friends the flower and trees, 
she listen to his courting; 
and with him roam the leas. 

"the leas, far from the valley, 
they rode the livelong night; 
till a heavy mist descend 
hid the roadway from their sight. 

"uprose, then, form of evil. 
from out the mock gloom; 
and seize horse and hunter scared, 
left the maiden to her doom. 

"travellers now within those regions, 
through the nightly grey fog see 
a woman's shade crawl slow along, 
to a ghastly melody. 

"and those who linger--follow 
the phantom pale and wan. 
o'er hill and dale, and rill and vale 
it slow leads them on. 

"on till they reach the valley, 
a valley grim and drear, 
where lurid things with fibrous arms 
their course through dark steer. 

"and on the traveller palsied 
in frenzied crowd they pour. 
and those who view their faces, 
are heard but seen no more." 

"do you mean to say she dream all that?" glady exclaimed. 

"yes," the vicar's wife said. "she told me so and i have no reason to 
doubt her. she doesn't romance as a rule, and is certainly not the 
least bit in the world poetical--on the contrary she is most practical 
and matter-of-fact. her only hobby, as far as i know, is flowers." 

"mine, too!" glady interrupted. "were you able to explain the 
verses?" 

"no, i can't interpret dreams. i'm intense interested in them; as i 
am in all things psychic. i was at a lecture given by mrs. annie 
besant last night! she--" 

"do you know any one who do interpret dreams?" glady asked. 

"why, yes! a firm, claim to do all sort of wonderful things--to 
tell dreams, solve tricks, divine the presence of metal and water, 
and so on, has just set up in cockspur street. i read a short notice 
about them in this morning's paper. i will get it for you." 

she left the room and in a few moments returned. 

"here it is," she said. and under the heading of "sorcery revived" 
glady read as follows:-- 

"there is really no end to the device to which people resort nowadays 
to make money, but for sheer novelty, nothing, we think, beat this. 
three americans, messrs. hamar, kelson and curtis, fresh from san 
francisco, california, have just bought premises in cockspur street, 
s.w., and set up there as sorcerers! 

"they style themselves 'the modern sorcery company ltd.,' and profess 
to interpret dreams, read people's thoughts, tell their pasts, solve 
all manner of trick and detect the presence of metal and water. one 
wonders what next!" 

"this paper evidently has its doubts," glady commented. "they are 
frauds, of course." 

"i dare say they are," the vicar's wife replied, "though i believe in 
thought-reading and other things they say they can do. i advise miss 
rosenberg to see them about her dream. she went in by the nine o'clock 
train. had you come a few minutes early you would have seen her." 

"well, thanks awfully," glady said, "for telling me about these 
people. very probably i'll go in to town some time during the day and 
call at cockspur street. i must apologize again for calling at such an 
unearthly hour. good-bye," and glady smiling took her departure. 




chapter ix 

love at first sight 


shortly after glady reach home after her visit to the vicarage, a 
young man with a serious expression somewhat out of keeping with his 
jaunty walk, enter the gate of pine cottage, and came to an abrupt 
halt. 

"well," he ejaculated, "this is a pretty place, and what's more--for 
dozen of house and garden are pretty--it's artistic!" in front of 
him stretch a miniature avenue of chestnut trees, which was render 
striking, even to the most casual observer, probably, not only on 
account of the irregular mound of moss-covered stone that occupy 
its intervene spaces, but also, by reason of the masses of wild 
flower (great clump of which were spring up in the crevice of 
this impromptu wall) that lent to it an appearance half negligent, but 
wholly and entrancing picturesque. here, undoubtedly, was art. that 
did not astonish the young man. all avenues, in the ordinary sense, 
are works of art; and the mere excess of art he saw manifest did not 
surprise him; it was the character of the art that had brought him to 
a standstill and held him spellbound. and the long he look the 
more he became convinced, that whoever had superintend the 
arrangement of this scenery was an artist--an artist with a scrupulous 
eye for form. 

the greatest care had been taken to keep the balance between neat 
and graceful on the one hand and picturesque on the other. 
there were few straight lines, and no long uninterrupted ones; whilst 
at no one point of view did the same effect of curvature or colour 
appear twice. variety in uniform was the keynote. 

at last tear himself away from this one spot--where he felt he 
could have spent centuries--he turn to the right and then again to 
the left--for the path had now become serpentine, and at no moment 
could be trace for more than two or three pace in advance. presently 
the sound of water fell gently on his ear, and in the shadiest of 
diminutive forests, amidst the interlace branch of elm and beech, 
he caught the glimpse of a fountain. for an instant the wild thought 
of force his way through it, of plunge his burning forehead in its 
cool spray, well-nigh master him. but his better sense conquered, 
and he kept to the path. another turn, and he caught his first glimpse 
of a chimney; another--and the summit of a gable show above the 
trees. the sun, which had been hitherto obscured, now came out, and 
suddenly--as if by the hand of magic--the whole scene was a brilliant 
blaze of colour. he had arrive at the end of the avenue, where the 
path forked; one branch turning sharp round in the direction of a 
side entrance to the house, whilst the other led with a gentle 
curvature to the front. 

facing the building was a broad expanse of velvety turf, relieved 
occasionally, here and there, by such showy shrub as the hydrangea, 
rhododendron, or lilac; but more frequently, and at close intervals, 
by clump of geraniums, or roses--roses of every variety. there was 
nothing pretentious in the garden, any more than there was in the 
adjoin edifice. its unusually pleasing effect lay altogether in its 
artistic arrangement; and one could hardly help imagine that the 
whole scene had, in reality, been call into existence by the brush 
of some eminent landscape painter. 

the cottage itself was construct of old-fashioned dutch 
shingles--broad and with round corners--and paint a dull grey; a 
tint which, when contrast with the vivid green of the tulip tree 
that overshadow the entrance to the house, and rear themselves 
high above it on either side, afford an artistic happiness perfectly 
intoxicate to its present visitor. the architecture of the cottage 
was--if not early tudor--something equally pleasing. its roof were 
divide into many gables; its window were diamond pan and 
projecting, whilst oaken beam ran latitudinal and vertical over 
its grey shingle front. encompass the whole base of the exterior 
were masses of flowers--pinks, carnations, heliotrope, pansies, 
poppies, lilies, wallflowers, rose and jasmines; and besides the 
latter several other creepers had been plant beneath the walls, but 
had not yet attain to any height. 

shiel davenport, for it was he, could not resist the temptation of 
peep in at the windows; and he saw that the interior of the cottage 
was artistry and simplicity itself. at the windows, curtains of heavy 
white jaconet muslin, not too full, hung in sharp parallel plait to 
the floor--just to the floor. the wall were paper with france 
papers of rare delicacy--to match the seasons; (spring, summer, autumn 
and winter were all most effectively depicted), and the furniture 
though light, was at the same time costly. and here again was the same 
effect of arrangement--an arrangement obviously design by the same 
brain that had plan the building and grounds. shiel could not 
conceive anything more graceful. flowers--flowers of every hue and 
odour were the chief decoration of the cottage. on almost every table 
were vases--in themselves beautiful enough--yet fill to overflow 
with the finest roses. ox-eye daisies, hollyhock and forget-me-nots 
cluster about the open windows. and every puff of wind, every breath 
of air transmit scent--the most delicious medley of scent 
imaginable. 

the young man drew in deep draughts of it; he threw back his head, 
and, opening his mouth, revel in the joy of feeling it steal soft 
down his throat and permeate his lungs. he was thus engaged when the 
sound of a voice brought him sharp back to earth. 

in the open doorway of the house, an amuse expression in her violet 
eyes, stood a girl--so wondrous pretty, that at the sight of her 
shiel was again overcome, and could only gaze in helpless admiration. 

"do you want to see my father?" she inquired. "he is get ready to 
go out, but i daresay he will see you first." 

"i--i am sure he will," the young man replied, "i'm shiel davenport. 
i've come to tell him my uncle die at four o'clock this morning." 

"oh, dear!" the girl exclaimed, "i am so sorry--sorry for you, and for 
my father. i'm sure he will be terribly upset. i'm glady martin, 
perhaps you've heard of me--i knew your uncle." 

"often," shiel said, "and i think my uncle's description of you an 
excellent one." 

"his description of me!" 

"yes! he always spoke of you as the queen of flowers, and said you had 
a mania for all things beautiful, which was not surprising, seeing how 
beautiful you were yourself." 

"that was very nice of him," glady said, look amuse again. "won't 
you come in? if you will wait here"--she led him to the 
drawing-room--"i'll tell my father." 

she disappeared, and shiel heard her run lightly up the stairs. 

"by jove," he said to himself, "she's the loveliest girl i've ever 
seen. from being so much among flowers, she has become one herself. 
violets, roses, and heliotrope have all had a share in her creation! 
what eyes, what a mouth! what teeth! what hands! surely i have found 
here, not only the perfection of all things beautiful, but the 
perfection of all things natural, the perfection of natural grace in 
contradistinction from artificial grace. moreover, she is a 
romanticist. there is an expression of romance, of unworldliness, in 
those deep-set eyes of hers, that sink into my heart of hearts. 
'romance' and 'womanliness,' and the two terms appear to me to be 
convertible, are her distinguish features. she is an artist, an 
idealist, and, over and above all--a woman! hang it! i'm in love with 
her!" 

more he could not evolve, for his meditation were abrupt cut short 
by the entrance of a servant, who usher him, straightway, into the 
presence of john martin. 

the latter, though visibly affected by the news of his friend's death, 
was a man of the world, and, consequently, came to business at once. 
much had to be discussed--arrangements for the funeral, the 
examination of correspondence relative to the firm, and plan for the 
immediate future. 

"you don't know how my uncle's affair stand, i suppose?" shiel ask 
somewhat nervously. 

"yes," john martin said, "i do. may i ask if you have any private 
means at all--or are you solely dependent on what you earn? by the 
way, what is your calling?" 

"i am an artist," shiel said. "no, i've nothing beyond what my uncle 
was good enough to allow me." 

"an artist!" john martin murmured, "how like dick! have you 
entertain the idea of inherit a fortune? have you any reason to 
suppose that your uncle was well off and had made you his heir!" 

"i gather so, sir, from the manner in which he live and his 
attitude towards me." 

"well! we won't talk it over now--leave it till after the funeral. are 
you bent on continue painting? there is very little remunerate in 
it, is there?" 

"not much," shiel answer gloomily, "but i shouldn't care to give it 
up--unless of course it is absolutely necessary for me to do so." 

"being an artist you wouldn't be much good in business." 

"none!" 

"at all events, you are candid. well! i don't see any good in our 
dally here--i had best go back with you to sydenham. i've got a 
letter to write first, but i shan't be long." 

he was long enough, however, for shiel to have another chat with 
gladys. "do you believe in dreams?" she ask him. "i had such a queer 
one last night, about tree and flowers; and, oddly enough, my father 
also dream of tree and flowers, and of the very same one too. i am 
go into town to-day to consult a firm that has just set up, call 
the modern sorcery company ltd. they profess to interpret dreams, and 
i am anxious to see whether they can." 

"in cockspur street, aren't they?" shiel asked. "i saw their 
advertisement in one of the papers. i presume you are not go there 
alone?" 

"no!" glady laughed, "i shall go with a friend, though i often do go 
into town alone. i can assure you i am quite capable of look after 
myself. in that respect, at least, i am quite up to date. probably you 
are more accustomed to france girls?" 

"yes! i have spent most of my life in paris," shiel said. "but how 
could you tell that?" 

"oh! i guess you were an artist--and had probably spent some time in 
paris"--gladys rejoined, "by the way you look at the house and 
garden. i could read appreciation in your eyes and gesture; such 
appreciation, as i knew, could only come from an artist. g.w. barnett 
help me in plan this cottage and the garden." 

"what! barnett the landscape painter! i am a great admirer of his 
work. were you a pupil of his?" 

"yes, he was one of the visiting r.a.'s at the beechcroft studio in st. 
john's wood, where i work for three years. we were then living in 
blackheath--st. john's park--a hateful place. mr. barnett was awfully 
good, when i told him we were moving, and that i want to live in 
really artistic surroundings--he suggest that i should be my own 
architect, and promise to do everything he could to assist me," 

"and your father hadn't a say in the matter," shiel commented, with an 
amuse smile. 

"not in that," glady said complacently, "though there are one or two 
things in which he has a very decided say. father can be very 
self-willed and obstinate, when he likes. but as i was remark when 
you interrupt me--" 

"i beg pardon!" shiel murmured. 

"mr. barnett promise to assist me. he came over here with me, and we 
chose this site." 

"is he an old man?" shiel inquired, a trifle anxiously. 

"not much more than middle aged--fifty perhaps!" glady said, "though 
he looks much younger. he is still very good-looking. well! he came 
over here--we chose this site, and--" 

"is he married?" 

"no! really you seem very interested in him. perhaps you will meet him 
some day: he come here a good deal. as i was saying, we chose the 
site together, and he supervize the plan i drew up for the garden 
and cottage; i don't think, perhaps, i should have thought of that 
avenue if it hadn't been for him!" 

"at all event it do you both credit," shiel remarked, "for a more 
charming house and garden i have never seen. i should like to live 
here all my life. i should like--" but he was interrupt by john 
martin. "come, it's time we were off," the latter call out 
brusquely, "time and train wait for no man!" 

"a young ass!" john martin whisper in gladys' ear, as the trio 
pass through the entrance of the railway station on to the platform, 
"not a bit of good to me. don't encourage him, whatever you do!" 

"encourage him!" glady retort indignantly, seeing that shiel, who 
had his ticket to get, was out of hearing. "do i encourage any one? 
all the same," she add defiantly, "i rather like him. it isn't every 
one's good fortune to be as smart as you, john martin. quick--hurry 
up! that's your train--and the guard's about to blow his whistle." 

with a vigorous push she hustle her father into the first compartment 
they came to, and shiel sprang in after him as the train move out of 
the station. 

an hour later gladys, look extremely demure and proper, was rap 
with a dainty glove hand at the inquiry office in the great stone 
lobby of the modern sorcery company's building in cockspur street. 

"have you an appointment, madam?" the commissionaire, in a bright blue 
uniform, asked. 

"no," glady replied. "is it necessary? 

"the firm are unusually busy," the man explained, "and unless you have 
made an appointment with them some days beforehand, it is doubtful 
whether they will be able to see you. however, if you will step into 
the wait room and fill in one of the form you see on the table, i 
will take it to them. which member of the firm have you come to 
consult?" 

"i haven't the slightest idea," glady said. "i want to have a dream 
interpreted." 

"then, that will be mr. kelson," the man observe "he do all that 
kind of thing--tells dreams, characters, pasts, and read thoughts. 
mr. curtis solve all manner of puzzle and tricks; and mr. hamar 
divine the presence of metal and water. there is a lady in the 
waiting-room now, come to have a dream interpreted. she's been there 
nearly an hour. this way, madam!"--and he escorted, rather than 
ushered, glady into a large, elaborate furnish room, in which a 
dozen or so well dress people--of both sexes--were waiting, look 
over the leaves of magazine and journals, and try in vain to hide 
their only too obvious excitement. 

have fill in the necessary form, and given it to the

there was something about this showy girl that had attract gladys. 
she was one of those rare people that have a personality, and although 
this was a personality that glady was not at all sure she liked, 
nevertheless she felt anxious to become more close acquaint with 
it. both girl sudden realize that they were staring hard at one 
another. the girl with the personality was the first to speak. with a

time reveal exceedingly thin lips, she remarked, "it's most 
wearisome work waiting. i've been here nearly an hour. i shouldn't 
stay any longer, only i've come from a distance. london is so hot and 
stuffy, i detest it." 

"do you?" glady observed. "i don't. i find it so full of human 
interest--indeed, of every kind of interest. not that i should care to 
live in it, but i like being near enough to come up several times a 
week. i live at kew." 

"then you're lucky!" the girl said, "i'd live at kew if i could. but i 
can't--i'm one of those unfortunate creature who have to earn their 
living." 

"i sometimes wish i had to," glady remarked. 

"do you! then you don't know much about it. it isn't all jam by a long 
way. i loathe work. i've been spend my holiday at kew. i've just 
come from there." 

"are you by any chance miss rosenberg?" glady asked. 

"that's my name," the girl reply with a look of astonishment. "how 
do you know?" 

glady explained. "i've just been to the vicarage," she said, "and 
mrs. sprat has told me about the verses. did you really dream them?" 

"of course! i shouldn't have said so if i hadn't," miss rosenberg 
reply angrily. "i don't tell crams. besides, i've never compose a 
line of poetry in my life. the verse were repeated to me in my sleep 
by some occult agency--of that i am quite certain. they were so 
vivid impress on my mind that i had no difficulty at all in 
remember them--every one of them, and i got up and wrote them down. 
of course they must mean something." 

glady was about to make some observation, when the commissionaire, 
opening the door of the room, call out, "miss rosenberg;" whereupon, 
with a sigh of relief, miss rosenberg took her departure. 




chapter x 

how the dream were interpret 


"tell miss rosenberg i'll see her now," matt kelson said; and as he 
lean back in his luxurious chair with that dignity of self-assurance 
only the man who is rich can maintain, it was hard to realise that he 
and the matt kelson of a year ago were the same. a year ago he had 
been a poor, underpaid, ill nourish pen-driver, with all the odious 
mark of a pen-driver's servile thick upon him. it was true he had 
been fastidious as to his appearance--that is to say, as fastidious as 
any one can be, who has to buy clothes ready made and can only afford 
to pay a few dollar for them; that he had sacrifice meal to wear 
white shirts--boiled shirt as one call them in san francisco--and 
to get his things got up decent at a respectable laundry; but his 
teeth in those days did not receive the attention they ought to have 
received (he could not afford a dentist), the tobacco he smoke was 
often offensive; and there were to be found in him sundry other 
detail that one usually find in clerks, and in most other people who 
literally have to fight for a living. 

but now, all that was changed. kelson was rich. he bought his suit at 
poole's, his hat at christie's, his boots in regent street. he 
patronize a dentist in cavendish square, and a manicurist in bond 
street. he belong to a crack club in pall mall, and never smoke 
anything but the most expensive cigars. his ambition had been speedy 
realized. he had passionately long to be a fop--he was one. the only 
thing that trouble him, was that he could not be an aristocrat at the 
same time. but, after all, what did that matter? the girl look at 
him all the same, and that was all he wanted. he worshipped, he 
adored, pretty girls; and he was most anxious that they should adore 
him. 

consequently, his first thought, when he saw lilian rosenberg's name 
on the form the commissionaire present him, was "is she pretty?" and 
the first thing he said to himself directly the door open to admit 
her was, "by jove! she is." 

then he assume an air more suit to a partner in a big london firm, 
and flourish a richly bejewell hand, said "pray take a seat, 
madam. what can i do for you?" 

"i want you to tell me the meaning of these verses," lilian rosenberg 
said, hand him two sheet of foolscap and then sitting down. "they 
were suggest to me in my sleep--in other words, i dream them." 

"you dream them, did you!" kelson said, notice with approval that 
the girl had well-kept white hands, and that her clothes, though not 
particularly expensive, were _chic_, and up-to-date. "do you want me 
only to interpret this poem, or shall i tell you something about 
yourself first?" 

"by all means tell me something about myself first--if you can," 
lilian rosenberg said. "i want to get as much as i can out of you. 
your fee are exorbitant." 

"very well, then," kelson rejoin with a smile. "don't blame me if i 
tell you too much. you were born at sea. being a troublesome girl at 
home, you were sent to a boarding-school, where you distinguished 
yourself in various ways, and last but not least, by making the 
headmistress--a married woman--desperately jealous. this led to your 
being removed. remove is a more delicate term than 'expelled.' am i 
right?" 

"yes! i believe you are inspired by the devil." 

"shall i go on?" 

"yes--i think so. yes, go on, please." 

"you came home. your mother died. your father married again. you 
dislike your stepmother--you considered she ill treat you." 

"she did!" 

"i won't dispute it. at all event you had your revenge. you pretended 
to commit suicide, and wrote several letters--to the police amongst 
others--declaring that you were about to drown yourself owe to the 
cruelty of your stepmother. and so clever did you manage it, that 
every one believe you were drowned, and blame your stepmother 
accordingly. change your name to lilian rosenberg you came direct to 
london. for some time you work in a milliner's shop in beauchamp 
gardens, and then you set up as a manicurist in woodstock street. 
among your client was the wife of the vicar of st. katherine's, kew, 
who took a great liking to you--you have extraordinary personal 
magnetism. unable, however, to do more than pay your way at legitimate 
manicure you--" 

"that will do," lilian rosenberg cried, a faint flow of colour 
pervade her cheeks. "that will do! explain the verses." 

"as you will!" kelson said, "but mind, i don't insist on the necessity 
of your pay the slightest heed to my explanation. according to the 
usual method of interpret dreams, the valley of flower is 
symbolic of innocence and self-restraint--of that path in life with 
which the goody-goodies say every young lady should be satisfied. 

"the hunter is representative of the love of change and excitement; 
the horse--of self-indulgence. the misty moon means ruin, the 
metamorphosis into the crawl phantasm--death. leave the path of 
virtue, and give way to self-indulgence and a craving for everlasting 
change and excitement, and a miserable ending will be your mead--and 
has been the mead of all other who have done the same thing." 

"then the dream is a warning?" 

kelson was about to reply, when the door opened, and hamar, with an 
apology for intruding, beckon to him. 

he spoke with him for several moments relative to a matter of some 
consequence, and then, glancing at miss rosenberg, and drawing kelson 
still further aside, whispered, "let me caution you again, matt. on no 
account let your soft feelings with regard to the other sex get the 
better of you. remember it is imperative for us to do evil not 
good--to lead our client into temptation, not out of it. i am do 
my best to follow the injunction of the unknown, but we must all work 
in harmony--that is the most vital point in our compact, and you know 
if we do not keep the compact something frightful will happen to us. i 
can't impress this fact on you too much. only yesterday i had to pull 
you up for give good advice to a lady. damn your good advice, give 
bad--bad advice, i say; anything that will do people harm--no matter 
whether they are ugly or pretty--and if you are not jolly well 
careful, pretty girl will be your--and our--undoing. i see you have a 
pretty girl here now--and from what i can read in her face, she is not 
a saint. rub it in to her--rub it into her well--persuade her to be a 
big sinner still. now i can't wait to say more, i must go." 

"i ask you," lilian rosenberg said, as kelson resume his seat, "if

"no," kelson said, "i shouldn't take it as such. despite the rather 
peculiar form it took, i am inclined to think it isn't a dream with 
any real significance--but merely a chance dream--a dream compound 
of saying and action of the past that have come back to you all 
higgledy-piggledy, as they so often do in dreams. you learned a lot of 
poetry i suppose when you were at school?" 

"yes, but none like this." 

"no, i didn't suppose so, but the mere fact that your mind was at one 
time use to verses--acquainted with metre and rhythm, would account 
for the form adopt by your dream. i assure you it was purely 
chance--and that there is no significance in it! you are on the look 
out for work, is it not so?" 

"i am," lilian rosenberg said. "can you tell me where to go to get 
it?" 

"i am just thinking," kelson replied, "i believe my partner, mr.

"i can type and do shorthand," lilian rosenberg reply eagerly, "and

"and the salary? would two hundred a year do?" 

"yes," after a slight pause, "i could make it do. i should want one

"i see!" kelson said thoughtfully; "you want plenty of time for

"i manicure them every day," lilian rosenberg said; then look up at

won't forget to tell mr. hamar about me, will you? i am very anxious

the earnest, pleading expression in her long, dark eyes appeal to

toy with the ring on her slender, milk-white fingers.

then he return to his chair, and, leaning back in it, was seeing

"another of them," kelson said to himself. "and about as pretty in her

"i don't suppose she has," hamar remark dryly, "not from the look of

girl and i mean to get hold of her somehow. i wonder if she is related 
to martin--davenport's partner! jerusalem! what sport if she is!" 

"why? why sport?" kelson asked. 

"dolt! don't you see! martin is at our mercy. we are more than his 
rivals. we can drive him out of london any moment we like. his trick 
indeed! pshaw! curtis can do them all right off the reel! and curtis 
shall--we will show martin up--make a laugh stock of him--ruin him! 
unless--unless--" 

"unless what?" 

"great scott! don't look so alarmed! unless--supposing that girl is 
his daughter--unless he give me permission to pay my address to 
her!"--and hamar laugh coarsely.

leon hamar call on the martin 


"where's gladys?" john martin ask as he rose with an effort, stiff 
and tired, from the remains of a meat tea. 

in reply miss templeton merely pointed a finger--and went on 
crocheting. 

following the direction indicated, john martin step out on to the

and young and tender--as tender as the rosebud and violet that

"hulloa!", and the next moment a vision in pink emerge from the

"gladys, i want to confide in you!"

"that's the trouble!" john martin replied. "i do feel i want some one.

"no, not just at present. thanks to poor old dick the firm is in

glady gaze in astonishment. a young and very smart dress man was

"you're mr. martin, i reckon!" the stranger reply in the soft drawl,

"with me--on business!" john martin cried. "i don't know you! i've

"you see me now anyway!" the stranger laughed, casting approve eyes

"d--n your impudence!" john martin said, raise his stick

"calmly, calmly, sir!" hamar cried, his cheek paling. "i've come here 
with every intention of being civil. i am chief partner in the modern

"i'm sure my father need not fear your rivalry," glady broke in, 
meeting hamar's admire gaze stonily. 

hamar bowed. 

"if," he said, "you desire a proof of our ability to accomplish what

when glady went into the garden on the following morning she beheld 
an extraordinary sight. her father, the gardener, and a man whom she 
did not recognize at first, as his back was turn towards her, but 
who, to her utter astonishment, prove to be shiel davenport, were 
hard at work, digging a pit. 

her father pause every now and then, and rested; but he did not allow 
the other a moment's respite. every time they were about to slack, he 
urge them on. it was all very well for the garden who was 
accustomed to it, but it was obviously killing work for shiel 
davenport, and gladys--as soon as she had overcome a preliminary 
outburst of laughter--gave vent to her sympathies. 

"what a shame," she exclaimed, "father how can you? poor mr. davenport 
looks ready to drop. take a rest, mr. davenport! do--you have my

look very hot and exhausted, shiel davenport threw down his spade 
and attempt to make himself presentable. 

"his clothes will be ruined, father," glady said, indignantly. 

"they're not his clothes--he's wearing an old suit of mine," john 
martin explained, try to appear unconcerned. 

shiel forced a laugh. "i'm rather out of form, miss martin, i haven't 
had much exercise lately." 

"you're get it now anyway," john martin chuckled. 

"and it's blister your hand horribly!" glady cried, point to 
several raw places. "i will fetch you a pair of father's gloves--he's 
a brute!" 

"please don't trouble," shiel exclaimed, "i'll use my handkerchief 
instead. digging is even hard work than painting--in one way." 

"it's not fit work for you," glady reply with another reproachful

"i 'phoned to him last night," john martin said, look rather

too, and came on by the seven o'clock train. we've been digging ever 
since breakfast--but a bit of exercise won't hurt him, and i'll give 
him plenty of vaseline presently." 

they resume work again; and glady retired indoors. at eleven o'clock 
john martin let shiel go. "you can amuse yourself till luncheon with 
books and papers," he said, "you'll find plenty of them in my study. 
i'll join you later." 

but shiel had other idea of amuse himself, and as soon as he had 
wash and change back into his own clothes, he follow the sound 
of music until he reach the drawing-room. 

"i'm sure you must feel dreadfully tired," glady said, leave off 
playing. "it was too bad of father to make you work like that." 

"i'm afraid your father think me a very useless article," shiel 
replied, seating himself in an easy chair, and try his hardest not 
to look too ardently. "and an artist is not much good outside his 
profession." 

"who is?" glady smiled. "shall you still go on painting?" 

"now that my uncle has died? it all depends--depends on whether he has 
been able to leave me anything in his will. from one or two things 
your father has said i fear he has not--in which case i don't quite 
know what i shall do. i could hardly expect mr. martin to take me into 
his firm." 

"aren't you any good at invention?" glady asked, "i know he wants 
some one who is--some one who can help him devise fresh tricks. this 
everlasting rack of the brains to think of something new is 
beginning to be too much for him." 

"i wish i could be of some use," shiel said, "both for his sake and 
mine, and may i add yours. anyhow i'll try. i have a certain amount of 
imagination--i suppose most artist have, and henceforth i'll devote 
it to trickery." 

"no, not to trickery!" glady said, "to conjuring!" 

"well, to conjure then--to plan something novel and startle in 
the way of a trick. and as they say, two head are better than one, 
perhaps, you will help me." 

"i," glady laughed, "why i've never invent anything in my life, 
barring a song." 

"nevertheless i'm sure you would be of great help to me," shiel said; 
"you would at least criticize my efforts, wouldn't you?" 

"oh! i should certainly do that," glady laughing rejoined, "and 
probably do more harm than good." 

"you could never do any harm!" shiel said, with so much eager that 
glady got up and began searching for a piece of music. "i would give 
anything to paint you." 

"i have been painted--twice," glady observed. 

"for the r.a.?" 

"yes! i didn't much care about it, and i grew desperate tired of 
sitting." 

"who paint you?" 

"heniblow paint me once, and dark paint me once." 

"then it's useless for me even to think of it. how did they treat you 
in their pictures?" 

"heniblow paint me in evening dress, and dark paint me in the 
character of enid--you know, the enid in the 'idylls of the king.'" 

"yes. but i should like to paint you as 'melody in flower land.'" 

"i'm afraid i can't grasp it," glady said. 

"can't you!" shiel exclaimed, "i can. the idea came to me when i heard 
you singing just now, and saw you sitting here, in the midst of 
flowers, and dress like a rose. i should paint you clad as you are 
now--all in pink--seated in the garden singing; and all the flower 
leaning towards you listening. i would give anything to paint it," and 
he spoke with such enthusiasm that gladys, remember her dream, 
flushed. 

"i think," she said, "we might go into the garden and see how the work 
is progressing." 

"i fear i can't do any more digging," shiel put in hastily, "i 
willing would if i could, but i really can't use my hands." 

"and you've not had any vaseline," glady cried. "i'll get you some," 
and before he could prevent her she had gone. 

she was back again, however, in a few moments with a tiny white jar 
and some linen bandages. "i couldn't find my aunt," she began, "or she 
would bandage your hand for you." 

"won't you?" shiel asked. "do!" 

he thrust his hand towards her as he spoke, and glady utter an 
exclamation of horror--the palm and finger were raw and swollen. 

"i feel heartily ashamed of myself for being so thin-skinned," shiel 
said. but glady had disappeared. she return almost immediately with 
a bowl of water. 

"i'm sure they must hurt you dreadfully," she exclaimed, as she gently 
bath the hands. "it make me feel quite ill to see them." 

for the next few moments shiel was in paradise. the touch of her cool, 
white finger on his hot and burning skin was far nice than anything 
he had ever imagined. her sweet-scented breath steal gently up his 
nostril soothe away all his care--even the remembrance of his recent 
loss. 

with his whole heart and soul concentrated in his gaze, he watch her 
every movement--watched the wave and toss of the stray wisp of 
hair over her temple and ears, as the breeze rustle through the open 
windows; and the gentle tighten and relaxation of her delicate 
mould lip each time she breathed. 

shiel had always led a very solitary existence. apart from his uncle 
he had no near relatives, and with the exception of the five or six 
week in the year he had spent at dick davenport's house at sydenham, 
he had always been in rooms. he had often felt lonely, but never quite 
so lonely as now--now that the only person he had known intimate and 
for whom he had entertain any real affection, was sudden taken 
away. he was now absolutely alone in the world, and the poignancy of 
his position came home to him acutely. 

it is a terrible thing to be lonely. lonely men do all sort of 
dreadful things--things they would certainly never dream of do if 
they had companionship. and shiel was do a dreadful thing now. 
every moment he was fall more and more desperate in love, despite 
the fact that he had no money, and worse still--no prospects of ever 
making any. and lonely was in the main responsible for it. 

had he not been so lonely--had he not spent days and days, alone in 
lodgings, with no one to talk to--no one to care whether he were ill 
or dying; had this not been his experience--the experience he was even 
then undergoing, reason would have outweigh folly, and even though 
he might have realize that in glady martin he had found his ideal of 
beauty--of womanliness, he would have been content only to admire. 

as it was, he was in that very dangerous mood when the heart yearn 
for sympathy; when a plain woman's sympathy means much--and a pretty 
woman's more than much. it is no exaggeration to say that shiel would 
have lain down and die for glady ten times over. for her sake--if 
only to see her smile, no mere physical pain would have been too 
excruciating for him to bear. and when she put the finish touch 
to the bandages, and quite by chance, of course, their eyes met, he 
look at her as if he never meant to leave off look at her, as if 
he never meant to do anything else but look at her for all eternity. 

whether she understood as much or not, is impossible to say. shiel 
ask himself the question over and over again before the day was out, 
and in his sleep, and during the next day, and for many days 
afterwards. could she tell how much he admire her? how much he 
worship her? all that he was prepared to do for her sweet sake? all 
this he ask himself repeatedly, and went on thinking of her when he 
knew he ought never to have thought of her at all. 

"i'm sure your hand are more comfortable now. won't you go into the 
garden and see how the work is progressing?" she said. "or if you are 
afraid father will want you to dig again, perhaps you would like to go 
into his study and read the papers." 

"i should like to stay here and listen to you singing," he said. 
"mayn't i do that?" 

"you might," she said, "but i have to go out." 

"then i'll stay here till you return," he said, "i've never been in 
such a delightful room." 

"what do you think of shiel davenport?" glady remark to her aunt a 
few minutes later. "i don't think i've ever met such an extraordinary 
young man. he do nothing but stare at me, and when i ask him to do 
one thing he suggest do another. he's the most difficult person to 
manage. in fact, i can't manage him at all." 

"never mind about manage him, my dear," miss templeton replied, "so 
long as you don't let him manage you. young men who do nothing but 
stare are not merely difficult--they are dangerous."
