the great challenge 


when john martin came into tea that afternoon, he gave glady a shock. 
despite the fact that he had been in the sun all day and was much 
tan in consequence he had never looked--so glady thought--so old 
and haggard. 

"you dear old daddie!" she said, hasten to pour him out some tea, 
"you shouldn't work so hard--this silly digging has quite knock you 
up! haven't you finished?" 

"yes, i've finished!" john martin said, catching his breath. "i've 
found water!" 

"nonsense!" 

"it's true all the same. we struck it at exactly the distance he 
said--twenty feet." 

"then of course he knew." 

"how? how the deuce could he have known?" 

"i can't say," glady replied. "all i know is, that he's not straight, 
and that there's some underhand trickery go on. but do have your 
tea now, and dismiss it from your mind. anyhow, he can do you no 
harm." 

"here's a letter for you, john," mrs. templeton exclaimed, enter 
the room at that moment. 

john martin took it from her, and tore open the envelope curiously. it 
was a handwriting he did not know, and did not like--its 
characteristic were sinister. 

"i knew it!" he cried; "i knew the fellow was a scoundrel. what the 
deuce do you think he has the impertinence to do now?" 

"he!" glady said, look anxious at her father. "whoever do you 
mean?" 

"why, that confounded young bounder who came here last night--leon 
hamar he sign himself. in this letter he declare that he can perform 
any of our tricks, and will accept the wager i offer for their 
solution some little time ago. he also say that unless i consent to 
see him, and to listen courteous to what he has to say, he will 
public announce his intention of taking up the wager, at our hall, 
in kingsway, to-night." 

"do you think there is any possibility of his have discover the 
secret of your tricks?" glady asked. "could he have bribe any one 
to tell him?" 

"i don't think so," john martin said. "the only people who have any 
clue as to how they are done are my two attendants--both as you know 
native of cashmere, and men who, i feel pretty certain, could not be 
'got at.'" 

"in that case," glady remarked, "i fail to see what there is to worry 
about. your course is perfectly clear--take no notice of it." 

john martin was silent--dazed. he did not know what to think or do! 
there was something painful ominous to him in the discovery of the 
money and the water--something that accentuate the impression hamar's 
sinister appearance had made on him. the man did not look 
ordinary--his manner, gestures, walk and expression were decidedly 
abnormal--in fact they put him in mind of the superphysical. the 
superphysical! might not that account for his knowledge? bah! there 
was no such thing as the superphysical. the man was extraordinary--but, 
after all, only a man--his knowledge only that of a man. and it must 
be as the shrewd glady conjectured--he had put the money in the tree 
himself and had learned of the presence of water through some subtle 
artifice--perhaps only guess at it. he would defy him--let him do 
what he would! 

this was john martin's decision as he finished tea. an hour later he 
had change his mind, and was speak to hamar on the telephone, 
express his willing to grant him a brief interview if he came 
at once. 

in rather less than an hour a motor drew up at the martins' door and 
hamar step out of it. 

"glad to find you in a more tractable mood, mr. martin," he exclaim 
on being usher into the latter's presence. "i reckon you would 
sing to a different tune when you found that water. would you like me 
to give you a few more sample of my skill, before we proceed to 
business?" 

"name your business at once," john martin reply gruffly; "i haven't 
many minutes to spare." 

"no!" hamar said, "that's a pity; because part of what i have at the 
back of my brain may take more than a few minutes arranging. the 
situation in a nutshell is this. you have a pretty daughter, mr. 
martin?" 

"how dare you, sir?" john martin broke in, clench his fist. 

"gently, gently, mr. martin!" hamar observed, backing towards the 
door. "gently--you promise to give me a courteous hearing. i meant no 
offence. i say i admire your daughter immensely--she take the shine 
out of our america girls." 

"the deuce she does!" john martin foamed. 

"she does, you bet!" hamar went on. "and i see no reason if she likes 
me, why we couldn't get engaged. i would do the thing handsome as 
far as money goes. what do you say?" 

"i say that unless you're very careful i shall break my promise and 
kick you." 

"i would pay you a big lump sum to take me into partnership," hamar 
went on complacently, "and i would introduce a number of new trick 
that would stagger creation. i shouldn't be in any hurry to marry--the 
length of the engagement would be for you to decide." 

"then it would be _ad infinitum_," john martin said grimly, "for 
you'll never get my consent to a marriage." 

"never is a long day--and even a john martin may change. you want new 
blood and new capital in your firm--you would have both in me. i 
assure you your show would boom as it has never boom before!" 

"and the only condition on which you offer me all this is my 
daughter?" 

"you have said it--that is the one and only condition. your 
daughter--my brains, my dollars." 

"i have decided!" john martin said. 

"good!" hamar exclaimed; "i guess you would! there's nothing like 
the almighty dollar, is there?" 

"yes!" john martin rejoined; "the almighty fist--and that's what 
you'll get if you don't clear out of this house instantly. and if you 
ever come skulk round here again, or write me any more letters i'll 
set my. solicitor on to you." 

"then it's war--war to the knife!" hamar sneered. "how melodramatic! 
but it won't last long. i shall yet be your partner--and i shall yet 
have miss gladys! au revoir--i won't say good-bye!" and with a mock 
bow he hurried took his departure. 

that night messrs. martin and davenport's entertainment had progress 
as usual for about half an hour when it sudden came to a full stop. 
a man in the lowest tier of box had risen and was address the 
audience in a loud voice: "ladies and gentlemen!" 

in an instant all head swung round and there were stentorian shout 
of "silence!" 

but curtis--for it was he--was not easily daunted. "do you call this 
fair play!" he demanded; "i am here to-night to make a sporting offer, 
and one which will afford you vast entertainment." 

cries of "shut up!" "silence!" "he's drunk!" "turn him out!" merge 
into one loud roar forced him to pause. several uniformed official 
now invade the box, but hamar--who, as well as kelson, was with 
curtis--fixing them with his big dark eyes that gleam eerie in the 
half-lowered lights of the house--for the stage only at that moment 
was fully illuminated--held them in check, and they hung back not 
knowing what to do. this move of hamar's took with a large section of 
the audience--some of whom were possessed with sporting instincts, 
whilst other were merely curious--and the somewhat premature cries of 
"turn him out!" etc., were soon lost in vociferous shout of: "let 
them alone!" "let them speak!" "let us hear what they have to say." it 
was in the midst of this hubbub that john martin in a great state of 
nervous agitation came to the front of the stage and inquire the 
cause of the commotion. the shouting still continued, and gladys, who 
had come to the performance anticipate something of the sort, call 
to her father, from the wings, bidding him give curtis permission to 
speak. 

"you will lose all sympathy if you don't, father," she added; "and 
besides you have nothing to fear. it's sheer bravado and impudence on 
their part." 

thus advised, for glady was a level-headed girl, john martin gave in; 
and the audience show their approval by a vigorous round of 
clapping. 

"i wish i were spokesman," kelson sighed, his eyes glisten at the 
sight of so many pretty upturned faces. "go on, old man!" he added, 
give curtis a nudge. "fire away, and show them you know a bit about 
elocution, for the credit of the firm." 

curtis need no encouragement. what little bashful he had once 
possessed he had certainly left behind in san francisco, for he lean 
over the front of the box and smile familiarly at the audience. 

"i am edward curtis," he said, "one of the director of the modern 
sorcery company ltd. messrs. martin and davenport have so often 
boast that no one outside their firm can perform their trick that i 
have come here to-night resolve to disillusion them. i not only 
accept their offer of ten thousand pound for the solution of their 
tricks, but i agree to pay them double that amount--cash down--if i do 
not do everything they do--from 'the brass coffin' to their 
world-famed 'pumpkin puzzle.' with messrs. martin and davenport's 
permission i will explain one and all of their trick to you to-night, 
and the only thing i ask of you, ladies and gentlemen, is to see that 
i get fair play." 

a spontaneous outburst of clap follow this speech, and as soon 
as it had cease one of the audience who had risen and was wait to 
speak, said: "i trust messrs. martin and davenport will accept this 
challenge, and allow the modern sorcery company the opportunity here, 
in this hall to-night, of display their skill--or their ignorance, 
as the case may be. if messrs. martin and davenport's trick cannot be 
perform by any outsider--the firm in accept this challenge will 
merely be twenty thousand pound the richer--and if--as is hardly 
likely, messrs. martin and davenport should be outwitted, i am sure 
they themselves will be amongst the first to congratulate their 
successful rivals. i, for one, am quite ready to act as referee." 

"i too!" shout a dozen other voices. "be a sport and accept his 
bet!" 

"ladies and gentlemen," john martin reply with dignity, "you have 
given me no alternative; i accept the challenge. perhaps those who 
have so kindly volunteer to act as referee will see that order is 
maintain whilst i go on with my performance, at the conclusion of 
which mr. curtis--i think that is the name of my rival--will be quite 
at liberty to try his exposition of my tricks." 

the performance then proceeded, and when it was over, curtis, hamar 
and kelson, accompany by six of those of the audience who had 
volunteer to act as referees, step on to the stage. seat were 
provided for the referees--three on the one side of the stage and 
three on the other; and have seen that everything was fair and 
square john martin retired to the o.p. wing, behind which glady was 
concealed. 

a brief description of "the brass coffin" trick, which was the first 
messrs. hamar, curtis and kelson proceed to explain, will, perhaps, 
suffice. 

a massive construct brass-bound coffin is hand round to the 
audience, who careful examine it, and being unable to discover 
anything amiss, pronounce themselves satisfy that it is genuine. 

the operator then summons an assistant, joking refer to him as "the 
corpse"--puts him into a sack, made to represent a winding-sheet, 
secure bind the sack with a piece of cord, and ask one of the 
audience to seal it. the sack and its contents are then placed in the 
coffin which is lock and corded. the operator then throw a sheet 
over the coffin, let it remain there for a few seconds, and on 
remove it and opening the lid, the coffin, is found to be empty. a 
shout from the front of the house make every one turn round, when, to 
their amazement, "the corpse" is seen standing up at the back of "the 
pit," holding the sack with the rope and seal--intact--in his hand. 
such was the marvellous feat which had been accomplished in martin and 
davenport's hall night in and night out for years, the solution of 
which no one as yet had been able to discover. one can imagine, in 
these circumstances, the tremendous excitement of the audience at the 
prospect of seeing this notorious puzzle tackled--and tackle by a 
member of a firm which was already reputed to be do all kind of 
weird and extraordinary things. but, whereas it was quite obvious that 
john martin was greatly perturb (his eyebrow were working 
nervously, and his lip and finger twitching), curtis, on the other 
hand, was as cool as possible--he literally did not turn a hair. 

"now, gentlemen," he said, turning to the referees, "keep your eyes 
well skin and observe everything i do. ladies and gentlemen," he 
went on, raise his voice, "i am now about to show you how the coffin 
trick is done. observe me--i'm 'the corpse'--mr. kelson, here, is the 
operator--" and matt kelson, rather to hamar's annoyance advanced, 
down the stage to take part in the proceedings. 

"watch me get into the sack!" he step into it as he spoke. "look at 
what i have in my hand," he went on, holding up his right hand in full 
view of the audience. "i have a plug of wood cover with the same 
material as this sack. as soon as i stoop down and the sack is pull 
over me i shall thrust this plug into the mouth of it and mr. kelson 
will bind the sack round it. i shall then be put into the coffin. you 
think you know this coffin but you don't. see!"--and step out of 
the sack he tap the head of the coffin, which was very broad and 
deep. "come closer!" and he beckon to the referees, whose numbers 
were now augment by three newspaper reporters--representatives of 
the _daily snapper_, the _planet_ and the _hooter_ respectively. "here 
is a secret panel work by a spring. i will press, and you will press 
too." 

and amidst a breathless silence--the nine member of the audience on 
the stage following every movement--curtis put his hand inside the 
head of the coffin and touched a very slight elevation in the wood. in 
an instant, by a wonderful neat piece of mechanism, a panel slid 
back, leave just sufficient room for a man of moderate dimensions to 
squeeze through. 

everyone now look at john martin--he was leaning back in his chair, 
breathing hard, his eyes start out of his head, his cheek white. 
hamar saw him and grinned, grin malevolently, but the smile die 
out of his face when he glance at gladys--the scorn in the girl's 
eyes made his blood boil. 

"all right, miss martin," he mutter between his teeth; "you adopt 
that attitude now, but you will adopt a very different one later on! 
i'll win you body and soul, or my name is not what it is." 

he was interrupt in this amiable reflection by curtis. "i'm too 
stout to play the rôle of the corpse, and so is matt," curtis said to 
him; "you must undertake that part. now!" he went on, "take this plug 
and get into the sack," and he whisper a few instruction in his 
ear. then he tie the top of the sack--in reality tie it round the 
plug hamar was holding--and one of the audience seal the knot. 
curtis and kelson then lift hamar into the coffin, shut the lid and 
cord it. then curtis, turning to the audience, said: 

"what is now happening inside the coffin is this--'the corpse' pull 
the plug out of the mouth of the sack from the inside. the cord thus 
become loose and 'the corpse' is able to open the sack. he at once 
touch the spring i pointed out to you in the head of the coffin, and 
the panel slide back--so!" 

and as the audience looked, they saw the panel slide back, and first 
of all hamar's head, and then his body, wriggle through the aperture 
thus made. 

"the reason why you, audience, cannot see him make his escape is 
this," curtis explained; "the head of the coffin is always turn away 
from you and placed against a mirror which you can't see, and which to 
you appear but the continuation of the stage. in this mirror exactly 
opposite the head of the coffin is an aperture, and it is through this 
'the corpse' make his exit to the back of the stage. i will show it 
you. here it is"--and beckon to the referee to come quite close, 
he pointed to a glass screen, in the centre of the base of which was a 
glass trap-door, corresponding in height and girth to the head of the 
coffin. "here, corpse!" curtis said, "crawl through"--and hamar, 
look as if he by no means appreciate the undignify task of 
wriggle on his stomach before so many eyes, drew himself as tight 
together as he could, and squirm through. 

"does that satisfy you, gentlemen?" curtis inquired. 

"perfectly!" the referee answered. "nothing could be plainer. we see 
exactly, now, how the trick is done." 

at this there was a loud outburst of clapping, and curtis bowed in the 
elegant manner in which he had been patient and assiduous coach 
by kelson. 

he then proceed to the second trick--"eve at the window," a trick 
almost, if not quite, as famous as "the brass coffin," and for the 
solution of which martin and davenport had frequent offer huge 
sum of money. 

a large pane of glass some nine by six feet in area, and set in 
a frame, made to represent that of a window, is placed on the 
stage, about eighteen inch from the floor. thirty-six inch 
from the ground a wooden shelf is placed against the window. an 
assistant--usually a woman--then mount on the shelf and, look out 
of the glass, proceeds to kiss her hand vigorously. the operator in a 
shock voice ask her to desist. she refuse and, to the amusement of 
the audience, carry on her pantomime flirtation more desperate 
than before. the operator pretend to lose his temper, and snatch 
up a screen place it at the back of her. he then fire a pistol, 
pull aside the screen, and she has vanished. as the top, bottom and 
side of the window, all in fact except the very middle, have been in 
full view of the audience, and as the window has been tight closed 
all the time, the disappearance of the girl completely mystify the 
audience. 

curtis explain it all. he pointed out that the keynote to the 
illusion lay behind the wooden shelf, which was so placed as to 
conceal the fact that the lower part of the window was made double, 
the bottom of the upper part being conceal from view by a second 
sheet of silver glass placed in front of it. the shelf cover the 
line of junction and enable the window frame to be scrutinize by the 
audience. 

as soon as the screen is put in front of the lady on the shelf--the 
glass pane slide up about a foot and a half into the top of the 
frame, purposely made very deep. the bottom of the window is cut away 
in the middle, leave an aperture about two feet square, which was 
previous hidden from view by the double glass at the base. eve make 
her exit through this hole, and slide on to a board placed behind the 
window in readiness for her. the pane of glass then slide down again, 
the screen is removed, and the window appear just as solid as before. 

when curtis conclude his verbal explanation he gave the audience a 
practical illustration of how the thing was done; he manipulate the 
screen and pistol, whilst hamar pose as eve, and directly he had 
finished there was another outburst of applause. kelson dare not look 
at john martin or gladys. the brief glance he had taken of them at the 
conclusion of the give away of the first trick had shock him--and 
he purposely stood with his back to them. with hamar it was 
otherwise--the joy of triumph was strong within him, and the picture 
of john martin, leaning forward in his chair, with his mouth half open 
and a dazed, glassy expression in his eyes, only thrill him with 
pleasure; he laugh at the old man, and still more at gladys. 

"that's the way to treat a girl of that sort," he whisper to kelson; 
"scoff at her--scoff at her well. let her see you don't care a snap 
for her--and in the end she'll run after you and haunt you to death." 

"i'm not so sure," kelson said. "it might act in some cases, perhaps, 
but i don't think you can quite depend on it." 

"pooh! you are no judge of women, in spite of all your experience," 
hamar retorted. "i'll bet you anything you like she'll come round and 
make a tremendous fuss of me." 

"supposing you fall in love with her, how about the compact?" kelson 
asked. "you've warn me often enough." 

"oh, but i'm not like you," hamar replied. "there's nothing soft in my 
nature. i fall in love! not much! why, you might as well have 
apprehension of my join the salvation army, or wanting to become a 
militant suffragette--either would be just about as possible. no--! i 
shall make the girl love me--and we shall be engaged for just as long 
as i please. if i find some one that attract me more, i shall throw 
her aside--if not, maybe, i shall marry her--but in either case there 
will be no question of love--at least not on my part. she shall do as 
i want--that is all! hulloa! curtis is beginning again." 

there were five other trick on the programme--all of which were world 
renowned. they were "the floating head"; "the mango seed"; "the 
haunt bathing-machine," "the girl with the five eyes," and "the 
vanish bicycle" illusion. as with the first two tricks, so curtis 
did with the following five--he explain them, and then, aid by 
hamar and kelson, gave practical demonstration of their solutions; 
and so thorough and clearly were these solution demonstrate that 
the referee ask no questions--they were absolutely satisfied. 
turning to the audience--at a sign from curtis--they announce that 
the whole of messrs. martin and davenport's trick had been solve to 
their entire satisfaction, and that messrs. hamar, curtis and kelson 
of the modern sorcery company ltd. had, without doubt, won the wager. 

"have you anything to say?" curtis asked, address john martin. 

"i acknowledge my defeat, though i do not understand it!" john martin 
said with very white lips. "i shall pay you the ten thousand pound 
to-night." 

"don't worry about that," hamar interposed; "we don't want to take 
your money, all we want to do was to prove to you we could perform 
the trick you believe to be insoluble. 

"ladies and gentlemen!" he went on, raise his voice, "the modern 
sorcery company ltd. has given you some proof to-night of their 
capabilities in the conjure line, and if you will give us the 
pleasure of your company to-morrow night--we invite you all free of 
charge for the occasion--we will give you a still further 
demonstration of our powers. may we count upon your patronage?" 

a terrific storm of clap was the reply, and as the audience slow 
file from the hall, john martin stagger into the wing, reel past 
glady ere she could catch him, and sank helpless on to the floor. 




chapter xiii 

the modern sorcery company ltd. give a gratis performance 


the days that follow were dark days for gladys. her father, whom she 
loved--and, until now, had never realize how much she loved--lay 
seriously ill. he had had a stroke which, although fortunately slight, 
must, as the doctor said, be regard as a prelude to what would 
happen, unless he was kept very quiet. and to keep him quiet was not 
an easy thing to do. his mind continual revert to what had just 
taken place, and he was for ever ask glady to tell him whether 
anything further had occur in connection with it, whether there was 
anything about it in the papers. 

gladys, of course, was oblige to dissemble. she hate anything 
approach dissimulation, but on this occasion there was no help for 
it, and what she told john martin was the reverse of what she knew to 
be actually happening. the papers were full to overflow with 
account of that fatal night's proceedings, and of the marvellous 
gratis exhibition given on the succeed evening by the modern 
sorcery company ltd. 

the _hooter_, for example, had a full column on the middle page head 
in large type-- 

extraordinary scene at martin and davenport's 
the greatest conjure trick in the world solved! 

whilst the _daily snapper_, determined to be none the less sensational, 
began thus: 

mystery no longer! 
"the brass coffin trick" and "eve at the window" done at last! 
martin and davenport lose their prestige 

this was bad enough, but the _planet_ publish a paragraph that was 
even more galling, viz.-- 

"now that messrs. martin and davenport's great illusion have been 
explain and their hall in kingsway, so long famous as the home 
of puzzledom, of necessity shorn of its glamour, one need not be 
surprise if those who delight in this kind of mystery, should 
turn elsewhere for their amusement. the britain public, which is 
above all things enamoured of novelty, will, doubtless, now resort 
to the modern sorcery company, whose house in cockspur street bid 
fair to become the future home of everything uncanny. their 
programme--to the uninitiated--presents possibilities--and 
impossibilities." 

so said the _planet_, and as the number of attendance at martin and 
davenports' fell from 820 on the night of the challenge to 89 on the 
succeed night, whilst the modern sorcery company's hall was fill 
to overflowing, there was every prospect of its prediction being 
verified. the solution of martin and davenports' trick had taken 
place (hamar had so plan it) on the last night the trio possessed 
the property of divination, and, consequently, on the night that 
terminate the first stage of their compact. the following night they 
would be in possession of new powers, such powers as would warrant 
them give a gratis exhibition--an exhibition of jugglery absolutely 
new and unprecedented. that the exhibition was successful may be 
gather from the following article in the _daily cyclone_-- 

"marvellous display of psychic phenomena in cockspur street. 

"the modern sorcery company ltd., in their new premises in 
cockspur street, gave the most remarkable display of phenomena it 
has ever yet fallen to our lot to report. indeed, the performance 
were of such an extraordinary nature that the huge audience, _en 
masse_, was scared; not a few people fainted, whilst every now and 
again were heard scream of terror intermingle with long 
protract 'ohs!'" 

a brief _résumé_ of the entertainment ran as follows:--the first part 
of the modern sorcery company's programme was carry out by mr. leon 
hamar, solus, who, step to the front of the stage, announce that 
he was about to give a display of clairvoyance. without further 
prelude he pointed to various member of the audience, and describe 
spiritual presence he saw standing behind them. he did not say he 
could see a spirit, answer to the name of james or george--or some 
such equally familiar name--and then proceed to give a description of 
it, so elastic, that with very little stretch it would undoubted 
have fitted nine out of every ten people one meet with every day, but 
unlike any other clairvoyant we have known, he describe the 
individual physical and moral trait of the people he professed to 
see. for example: to a lady sitting in the third row of the stalls, he 
said: "there is the phantasm of an elderly gentleman standing behind 
you. he has a vivid scar on his right cheek that looks as if it might 
have been cause by a sabre cut. he has a grey military moustache, a 
very marked chin; wear his hair part in the middle, and has 
light-blue eyes that are fixed ferocious on the gentleman seat on 
your left. do you recognize the person i am describing?" 

"i think so," the lady answer in a faint voice. 

"i will spare you a description of his person," hamar went on, "but i 
should like to remind you that he met with a rather peculiar accident. 
he was look over some engineering works in leeds, when some one 
pushed him, and he was instantly whip off the ground by a piece of 
revolving mechanism and dashed to pieces against the ceiling. am i 
right?" 

there was no reply--but the sigh, we think, was more significant than 
words. 

mr. hamar then turn to a lady in the next row. "i can see behind 
you," he said, "an old dowager with yellow hair. she wear large 
emerald drop earrings, black satin skirt, and a heliotrope bodice of 
which she appear to be somewhat vain. she is cough terribly. she 
die of pneumonia, brought about by the excessive zeal of--ahem!--of 
her relatives--for the open-air treatment. contrary to expectations, 
however, all her money went to a society in hanover square--a society 
for the anti-propagation of children. i think you know the lady to 
whom i refer." 

mr. hamar had again hit the mark. 

"only too well!" came the indignant and spontaneous reply. 

mr. hamar then turn to a man in the fifth row. "hulloa!" he 
exclaimed. "what have we here--an ireland terrier answer to the name 
of 'peg.' it is standing upright with its two front paw rest on 
your knees. it is look up into your face, and its mouth is open as 
if anticipate a lump of sugar. from the mark on its body i should 
say it has been kill by being run over?" 

again mr. hamar was correct. "what you say is absolutely true," the 
gentleman replied; "i had a dog name peg. i was greatly attach to 
it, and it was run over in piccadil by a motor cyclist. i hate the 
very sight of a motor bicycle." 

after a brief interval of awestruck silence a voice from the gallery 
call out-- 

"you are in league with him!" 

then the man in the stalls stood up, and essay to speak; but his 
voice was drown in a perfect tornado of applause. he had no need--he 
was instantly recognized--he was j---- b----. with a few more example 
of clairvoyance mr. hamar continue to entertain his audience for half 
an hour or so, by the end of which time, we have no hesitation in 
saying that every one was convinced that he actually saw what, he 
said, he saw. 

the second part of the programme was entire in the hand of mr. 
curtis, who now came forward with a bow. "ladies and gentlemen," he 
said; "you all know that man is complex--that he is compose of mind 
and matter, the material and immaterial. i now propose to give you a 
physical demonstration of this fact. will twelve of the audience 
kindly come up on the stage and sit around me, so that you may feel 
quite certain that i have here no mechanical device to assist 
me?"--and amongst other well-known people who respond to mr. 
curtis's request, were lord bayle, sir charles tenningham and the 
right hon. john blaine, m.p. have arrange these twelve volunteer 
in a semi-circle at the back of the stage, mr. curtis, standing in the 
centre of the stage, again address his audience. "ladies and 
gentlemen," he said; "the secret of separate the mind--or what 
spiritualists, who love to bolster up their pretended knowledge of the 
other world by the invention of pretentious nomenclature, call the 
'ethical ego'--from the body, lie in intense concentration. if you 
wish to acquire the power, practise concentration--concentrate on 
being in a certain place. if nothing happen at first, don't be 
discouraged, but keep on trying, and a time will come when you will 
sudden leave your body, in a form, which is the exact counterpart of 
the body you have left. you will visit the place whereon you are 
concentrating. perhaps the best method of practise projection is to 
put your forehead against a door or wall, and concentrate very hard on 
being on the other side. it may take week before you get a result, 
but if you persevere, you will eventually succeed in leave your 
physical form and passing through the door, or wall, into the space 
beyond. now watch me! i shall concentrate on project my immaterial 
body, and of walking in it, three times round my material body." 

mr. curtis closed his eyes, and for some seconds appear to be 
thinking very hard. then the audience witness a remarkable 
phenomenon--a figure, the exact counterpart of mr. curtis, step 
out, as it were, from his body, and slow walking round it three 
times, deliberate glide into it, and apparently amalgamate with 
it. the twelve member from the audience who were within a few feet of 
the allege ethereal body, as it walk past them, declared they saw 
it most vividly, and that feature for feature, detail for detail, it 
was the exact counterpart of mr. curtis, whose material body remain 
standing, upright and motionless, with its eyes tight closed. our 
representative question several of these eye-witnesses very closely, 
and they were all most emphatic in their belief that what they had 
seen was a _bona-fide_ case of spiritual projection. at the request of 
a large part of the audience, mr. curtis repeated his demonstration, a 
further complement of men from the stalls join those already on the 
stage to witness the operation. 

several test were now applied to the ethereal body of mr. curtis, as 
it walk round his material body. one man, clutch at its sleeve, 
try to detain it, but his hand pass through the sleeve, and 
held--nothing. another man put out an arm to act as a barrier, and the 
projection, without swerve from its course, pass right through it; 
and, on the completion of the third round, disappear as before. 

in answer to inquiries, mr. curtis state that the phenomenon might be 
taken as a good illustration of projections; and that he was prepared 
to project himself once again, in order to prove that it was erroneous 
to suppose that phantasm could not do all manner of physical actions. 
a deal table (upon which stood a tumbler and jug of water), a 
grandfather clock, and a piano were brought on to the stage, and mr. 
curtis once again project his spirit form. the latter at once walk 
to the table, and, taking up the tumbler, fill it with water from 
the jug; after which it wound up the clock, and, sitting down on a 
seat in front of the piano, play "killarney" and "the star-spangled 
banner." and then, amidst the wildest applause--the first time 
assured "a ghost" has ever received public plaudit in recognition 
of its services--it modest re-entered its physical home. 

mr. curtis then announce that not only could he project his ethereal 
body from his material body in the manner he had already demonstrated, 
but that with his ethereal body he could amalgamate with inorganic 
matter. he bade those on the stage approach the table in convenient 
numbers, _i.e._ two or three at a time, and listen attentively. he 
then took his stand on one side of the stage, about fourteen feet from 
the table; and the audience approach the table and listen 
attentively, first of all heard it pulsate as with the throb of a 
heart, and then breathe with the deep and heavy respiration of some 
one in a sound sleep. the table then raise itself some three or four 
inch from the ground and move round the stage; at the conclusion of 
which feat mr. curtis informed the audience that "table-turning"--when 
not accomplished through the trickery of one of the sitters--was 
frequent perform by the work of some earth-bound spirit--usually 
an elemental--that could amalgamate with any piece of furniture, in 
precisely the same way as his own projection had amalgamate with the 
table in front of them. "elementals," mr. curtis continued, "are 
responsible for many of the foolish and purposeless trick perform 
at séances; and for the unintelligible and useless kind of answer the 
table so often rap out. the best you can hope for, from an elemental, 
is amusement--it will never give you any reliable information; nor 
will it ever do you any good." 

with these words mr. curtis's share in the entertainment concluded. he 
retired to the wings, whilst mr. kelson step forward--begged those 
several gentlemen who, on mr. curtis's exit, had reseat themselves 
among the audience, once again to step up on to the stage. 

"be good enough," he said address them in his most polite manner, 
"to observe me very closely. i am about to give you a few further 
example of what intense mental concentration can do, thus prove to 
you to what an unlimited extent mind can gain dominion over matter. 
you all know that will-power can overcome any of the internal physical 
forces; for instance, when you have tooth or ear ache--you have only 
to say to yourselves: 'i shan't suffer'--and the suffering ceases. but 
what you may not know--what you may not have realized, is that 
will-power can over-rule external forces and principles--as for 
example--gravity. as a matter of fact, airship and aeroplane are 
absolutely superfluous--and the time, money and labour they involve is 
a prodigious waste. any man with strong mental capacity can fly 
without the aid of mechanism. he has only to will himself to be in the 
air--and he is there. look!" and to the amazement--the indescribable, 
unparalleled amazement--of all present, mr. kelson knit his brows, as 
if engaged in intense thought, and, jump off his feet, remain in 
the air, at a height of some four feet from the floor. 

at his request member of the audience came up to him, and pass 
their hand under, over and all around him, to make sure there were no 
wires. he then struck out with his hand and leg after the manner of 
a swimmer, and moving first of all round the stage, and then over the 
stalls and pit, gradual ascend higher and higher, till he reach 
the level of the boxes, to the occupant of which he spoke. 

such an extraordinary spectacle--which apparently give the lie to all 
our preconceived notions of gravity--has certainly never before been 
witnessed, and the effect it had on those who saw it, baffle 
description. when mr. kelson return to the stage, and the terrific 
applause that greet his arrival there had subsided, he gave the 
audience a few valuable hint as to how they, too, might accomplish 
this feat. 

"practise concentration," he said, "and develop your will power, if 
only by a very little, every day. jump off a stool to begin with, 
saying to yourself as you do so: 'i will remain in the air. i won't 
touch the ground,'--and though you may fail for the hundredth time, if 
only you keep on try you will eventually succeed. to keep your 
equilibrium on a bicycle is a feat which would have been pronounced 
utterly impossible by your ancestor of two hundred years ago; but 
just as that power came to you--after many futile efforts, all at 
once--so, in the end, will flying come to you. see, i am now go to 
rise to the highest point in the building. gravity pull me back, but 
i say to myself: 'i will rise--i will fly there'--and fly there i 
do!"--and, spring off the ground, he struck out with his arms and 
legs, flew swift and easily to the dome of the hall, which he 
touched--and then flew back again to the stage. 

this complete the evening's entertainment. if only on the strength of 
its first performance, the modern sorcery company, in our opinion, has 
more than justified its name; and although we understand they will 
give no more performance gratis, we feel confident in prophesy 
that, for many a long night, there will be no fall off in the 
attendance. 




chapter xiv 

shiel to the rescue 


glady did not feel too happy when she read notice such as these; she 
could not do other than see in them destruction to her father, and the 
worst of it all was she could do nothing to help him. who could? who 
could possibly invent anything as wonderful as the marvel of the 
modern sorcery company ltd.? and yet unless john martin gave up 
altogether, that is what he must do. nay, he must do more--he must not 
only equal the modern sorcery company's marvels, he must eclipse them. 
but after the affair of the challenge, it seem to glady that there 
was no help for it--the hall would have to be closed for a time. now 
that dick davenport was dead, there was no one to take her father's 
place. on the night succeed the catastrophe, she had persuade one 
of the indian attendant to undertake the rôle of operator, but his 
skill was not equal to the tax upon it, and the audience--a poor 
one--was very lukewarm in its applause. the following day she talk 
the matter over with her father. the latter was in favour of keeping 
the show on at any cost; gladys, for close it temporarily. 

"a bad performance is worse than no performance," she said, "much 
better to close till you have invent some new tricks." 

john martin groaned. "i fear my days of invention are over," he 
muttered. "if i can read the papers and write letters, that will be 
about as much as i shall be able to do." 

"couldn't you retire?" 

"i would if i were not a britisher," john martin replied, "but being a 
britisher i'd soon shoot myself than give in to a d----d yank!" 

and gladys, in terror lest her father should over-excite himself, 
promise she would see that the entertainment was carry on as usual, 
and that the indian continue in the rôle of operator. 

but when out of her father's presence, glady gave way to despair. how 
could she--a woman--hope to cope with such a difficult situation? and 
she was rack her brains to know how to act for the best, when shiel 
was announced. 

a wave of relief swept over her. she could explain her difficulty to 
shiel, in a way that she could not to any one who had no knowledge at 
all of her father's affairs--and she told him just how matter stood. 

"look here!" he exclaimed, when she had finished, "why not let me take 
your father's place at the kingsway? i have done a little amateur 
acting, and am not nervous at the thought of appear in public. your 
father confide in you so much--you must know all his trick by 
heart--couldn't you coach me!" 

glady look at him critically. 

"it wouldn't be half a bad idea," she said. "supposing you come with 
me to the hall, i can explain the trick better if i show you the 
apparatus at the same time." 

shiel thorough enjoy that journey up to town. he knew it was wrong 
of him to think of his own pleasure, when the affair of his companion 
were in such a critical condition. he knew he ought not to look at her 
in the way he did--as if she was the most precious thing in the world, 
and he would give her his soul if she want it--he knew that he--a 
penniless artist without any prospects--had no right to behave thus. 
but her beauty appeal to him with a force he was entire incapable 
of resisting, and he went on look at her in the way he knew he 
ought not to look at her, simply because he couldn't help it. 

he lunch with her at her club in dove street, and then they taxi 
to the kingsway. 

the door-keeper, the only living creature in the building, saving 
themselves, seem to share in the general depression hanging over 
everything--the great, empty front of the house with its gloomy, 
cavernous box and grim, grey gallery--the dark, dismal flies--the 
chilly wings--all hush and still, and impregnate with the sense of 
desertion. but with this man beside her, who, she knew, would do 
anything he could to help, the place did not look quite so bad to 
glady as it had done the day before. there was a ray of light now 
where, before, ebon black had prevailed. 

without delay glady rang up the indian attendant on the telephone, 
and occupy the time prior to their arrival by describe to shiel 
how each of the trick was done. 

her pupil prove far more able than she had anticipated. after several 
rehearsal he was able to go through the whole performance without a 
hitch. 

when they had finished, glady stretch out her hand impulsively. "i 
don't know how to thank you enough," she said. "you are a brick, and 
if only you do half as well this evening as you have done now, we 
shall get on swimmingly--that is to say, as well as we can expect, 
until we can arrange a fresh programme. if only you were an inventor!" 

"if only i were. if only i had money!" 

"why, what would you do?" glady ask curiously. 

"give it to you! give you every halfpenny of it!--but as i haven't 
any, i mean to give you all the energy i possess instead." 

"why me? my father you mean!" 

"no, you!" shiel said impulsively, "both of you if you prefer it, but 
you first." 

"me first! that doesn't seem very lucid--but i can't stay to hear an 
explanation now, for if i miss the four-thirty train i shall miss my 
dinner, which would indeed be a calamity!" and slip on her gloves, 
she hurried off, forbidding shiel to escort her further. 

left to himself, shiel stroll along the strand into the victoria 
gardens, where he bought an evening paper, and sat down to read it. 
the first thing that caught his eye was-- 

"magic in london" 

"this morning the west end received a shock. about twelve o'clock, 
a gentleman, fashionable dressed, turn into bond street from 
piccadilly, and when opposite messrs. truefitt's prepared to cross 
over. the street happen just then to be block by a long line 
of taxis. the gentleman, however, had no intention of wait till 
they had passed. measure the distance from one pavement to the 
other with his eyes, he jump about fifteen feet into the air and 
clear the intervene space without the slightest apparent 
effort--a feat that literally paralyse with astonishment all who 
beheld it. on being remonstrate with by a policeman, who was 
highly perplexed as to whether such extraordinary conduct 
constitute a breach of the peace or not, the gentleman calm 
leap over the policeman's head, and striking out with arms and 
leg swam through the air. 

"continuing in this fashion, the cynosure of all eyes--even the 
traffic being suspend to watch him--he pass along bond street 
into oxford street, where he once more alight on his feet. on 
being question by a representative of the press, it transpire 
he was mr. kelson, one of the partner in the modern sorcery 
company ltd., whose wonderful performance at their hall, in 
cockspur street, have already been report in these columns." 

"i should well like to know how that flying trick is done," shiel said 
to himself. "according to kelson it is entire a question of will 
power. i'll see if i can't develop my concentrate faculty and 
introduce a few of the same performance in our show. i'll go to the 
hall and try them now." 

but his preliminary effort were certainly far from successful. he 
jump off chair saying to himself, "i'll fly! i will fly," and he 
struck out heroic each time, but the result was always the 
same--gravity conquered--he fell. 

had he not been so much in love with gladys, he would have desisted; 
as it was, the more he bump and bruise himself, the more determined 
he was to go on trying. in fact, flying with him became a mania; and 
according to the daily journals, his was by no means the only case. 
all over england people were try to fly. an old lady, in gipsy 
hill, appear in the police court to answer a charge of cause 
annoyance to her neighbour by practise flying, from off her bed, at 
night. her bulk being large and her will power apparently small, she 
yield to gravity and landed on the ground with prodigious bumps, 
which set everything in the room vibrating, and which could be plainly 
heard in the adjoin houses, through the thin brick wall on either 
side of her room. 

an old gentleman in guilsborough had an extremely narrow escape. being 
warn on no account to practise flying in the house or garden, lest 
his grandchildren should see him and want to do the same, he retired 
to the seclusion of an old, disused and dilapidated coach house. here, 
in the upper storey, he practised by the hour together. he climb on 
to a stool which he had taken there for the purpose, and when he 
fancy he had acquire the right amount of concentration, he sprang 
into the air, arriving, presumable through want of will power, on the 
floor. for two whole days he practised--bump--bump--bump--and the more 
he bumped, the more he persevered. at last, however, the floor gave 
way, and with loud cries of "i will! i will!" he fell on the ground 
floor, ten feet below! he was unable to go on experimenting, owe to 
a broken leg and a fracture collar-bone. 

in aylsham, norfolk, there had been a perfect epidemic among the 
children for try aeronic gravity. rudolph crabbe, age five, after 
listen to an account of the performance at the modern sorcery 
company's hall, which his father had read aloud, sprang off the 
dining-room table crying out "i will fly! i will stay in the air." 
fortunately, he fell on the tabby cat, which somewhat broke the shock 
of concussion, and he escape unhurt. 

in college road, clifton, bristol, an octogenarian thinking he would 
add novelty to the jubilee celebration at the college, leap off the 
roof of his house, crying, "i'll fly over the close! i will fly over 
the close!"--and broke his neck. 

in st. ives, cornwall, where the treatment of animal is none too 
humane, a fisher-boy threw a visitor's pomeranian over the malakoff 
saying, "you shall fly! you shall remain in the air;" whilst at bath a 
girl of ten, snatch her baby brother from the perambulator, leap 
over beechen cliff, calling out, "we will fly together! we will fly 
together!" 

these are only a few of the many similar case shiel read in the 
paper, and which he narrate afterwards to glady martin. 

"i am quite convinced," glady said, "that kelson do his flying 
through supernatural agency. his assertion that it can be done through 
mere will power, is sheer humbug. it wouldn't be a bad idea to consult 
a clairvoyant. what do you think?" 

shiel thought it was an excellent suggestion. he saw in it an 
opportunity of spend yet another afternoon in gladys's company, and 
ask her to go with him to an occultist the very next day. when she 
assented, the pleasure of it tingle through every pore of his skin. 
of course, glady assured herself there was no harm in her acceptance 
of shiel's escort--that neither he nor she meant anything by it--that 
it was on her part merely a sort of an acknowledgment that he had been 
awfully good to her in her present predicament. besides, if she need 
further excuse, she had no reason for supposing shiel to be in love 
with her--and had her father not spoken to her about it, she would not 
have remark anything different in his glances, from the glances--for 
the time being, perhaps, earnest enough--bestowed upon her by other 
young men; which excuse, was, certainly, in gladys's case, a more or 
less honest one. 

they had some difficulty in select a psychometrist--so numerous 
were those who advertised, in an equally allure manner--but they at 
length decided in favour of madame elvita, whose consulting rooms were 
in new bond street. when they arrive there, madame elvita was, of 
course, engaged. shiel was delighted--it gave him an extra half-hour 
with gladys. when madame was free, she had much to tell them. first of 
all she spoke to them of karmas, kamadevas, rupadevas, vitalize 
shells, ether doubles, the nermanakaya, and afterwards solemn 
announce that she must relapse into a state of clairvoyance, in order 
to get in touch with tillie toot, a certain spirit from whom she could 
learn all that glady and shiel want to know. accordingly, in the 
manner of most other two-guinea clairvoyants, she compose herself in 
a graceful and recumbent attitude, made a lot of queer grimace and 
still queer noises, and spoke in a falsetto voice, which purpose to 
be that of tillie toot, once a barmaid in edinburgh, now one of 
madame's familiar spirits. and the gist of what "tillie" told them was 
that hamar & co. derive their powers from black magic; and that the 
secret thereof could only be learned from madame, after a series of 
sitting with her--sittings for which madame would only require a fee 
of fifty guineas: a most moderate, in fact quite trifling, sum, 
considering the wonderful instruction they would receive. 

but madame's magnanimous offer tempt neither glady nor shiel; and 
they abrupt took their departure. 

kateroski (_née_ jones) in regent street, whom glady and shiel had 
agree to consult in the event of a non-successful visit to madame 
elvita in bond street, also told them that black magic was the key to 
hamar, curtis & kelson's performances. she advise them to get on the 
astral plane, where they would meet spirits who would give them all 
the information they desired. 

madame kateroski's instruction were simple. "it is really a matter of 
faith," she said. "all you have to do is to go to some secluded 
spot--the privacy of your bedroom will do admirably--sit down, close 
your eyes, look into your lid and concentrate hard. after a while you 
will no long see your eyelids--your lid will fade away and you will 
be on the astral plane, and see strange creatures, which, although 
terrifying, won't harm you. when you get use to them, you will 
communicate with them, and learn from them all you want to know." 

"shall we try?" glady remark laughing to shiel, as they step 
into the street. "but if faith is essential to success, i fear 
failure, as far as i am concerned, is a foregone conclusion. i know i 
shouldn't have sufficient faith." 

"nor i either," shiel said. "but, perhaps, we could acquire a 
necessary amount of it, if we were to experiment together. supposing 
we try in that delightful secluded copse in your garden." 

glady shook her head. "i'm afraid it would be useless. besides, if my 
father were to hear of it, he would fear worry had turn my brain, 
and most likely have another fit. no, we must think of something more 
practical. in the meanwhile, if you will keep on with the part, you 
have so generous undertaken, you will be do me an inestimable 
service." 

"then i'll keep on with it for ever," shiel replied, and before she 
could stop him, he had kiss her hand. 




chapter xv 

how hamar, curtis and kelson enter the astral plane 


in order to explain the manner in which hamar, kelson and curtis were 
initiate into their new properties, i must now go back to the day 
preceding the gratis performance of the modern sorcery company, that 
is to say the last day of stage one of the compact. 

to kelson the day had been one of surprise throughout. when he 
arrive at the building in cockspur street (he prefer living alone, 
and, consequently, rent a handsome suite of rooms in john street, 
mayfair), he was not a little astonish to meet lilian rosenberg on 
the staircase. 

"i thank you so much!" she exclaimed, shake hand with him most 
effusively. "it is all owe to you i got the post." 

"then hamar has engaged you," kelson ejaculated. 

"why, yes! didn't you know!" lilian said with a smile. "i had a letter 
from him the very evening of the day i call here." 

"did you! he never told me anything about it! how do you think you 
will get on?" 

"oh, splendidly! the work is interesting and full of variety. 
moreover, i like the atmosphere of the place, it is so weird. i 
believe the three of you really are magicians!" 

"if that be so," kelson said, "then we have only act in accordance 
with our character in engaging the service of a witch--a witch who 
has already bewitch one member of the trio. now please don't go to 
the expense of lunch out: lunch with me instead. lunch with me 
every day." 

"it is very kind of you," lilian rosenberg replied, "and i will gladly 
do so when i am not lunch with mr. hamar. but he has invite me to 
have all my meal with him." 

"that doesn't mean you are oblige to have them with him every day!" 
kelson cried. "lunch with me this morning." 

"i am very sorry," lilian rosenberg replied, look at kelson with 
mock pleading eyes, "please don't scold me, but i've really promise 
mr. hamar." 

"have tea with me, then," kelson said. 

"i've promise him that, too." 

"supper then!" kelson said, savagely. 

"i'm awfully sorry, but i'm engaged all this evening, and practically 
every evening." 

"with mr. hamar?" kelson ask suspiciously. 

"oh no! my own private business," lilian rosenberg replied. "do 
forgive me. i should so like to have been able to accept your 
invitation. now i must hurry back to my work," and she gave him her 
hand, which kelson held, and would have gone on holding all the 
morning, had he not heard hamar's well-known tread ascend the 
stairs. 

"look here!" he said, as they enter his room together, "i want miss 
rosenberg to have luncheon with me one day this week, and she tell me 
you have already invite her. let her come with me to-morrow." 

"it is impossible," hamar said. "now i'll tell you what it is, matt, i 
anticipate this the moment i saw you two together, and its got to 
stop. you would genuine fall in love with that girl--or as a matter 
of fact any other pretty girl--if you saw much of her--and love, i 
tell you, would be absolutely disastrous to our interests. you must 
let her alone--absolutely alone, i tell you. i have given her strict 
orders she is to confine herself to her work, and to me." 

"i think you take a great deal too much on yourself. i shall see just 
as much of miss rosenberg, when she is disengaged, as i please." 

"then she never shall be disengaged. but come, do be sane and put some 
restraint on this mad infatuation of yours for pretty faces. can't you 
keep it in check anyhow for two years--till after the term of the 
compact has expired! then you will be free to indulge in it, to your 
heart's content. for heaven's sake, be guide by me. harmony between 
us must be kept at all costs. don't you understand?" 

"oh, yes! i understand all right," kelson said, "and i'll try. but 
it's very hard--and i really don't see there would be any danger in my 
taking her out occasionally." 

"well, i do," hamar replied, "and there's an end. to turn to something 
that may spell business. just before i got up this morning i saw a 
striped figure bend over me!" 

"a striped figure?" 

"yes! a cylinder figure, about seven feet high, without any visible 
limbs; but which gave me the impression it had limbs--of a sort--if it 
care to show them." 

"you were frightened?" 

"naturally! so would you have been. it didn't speak, but in some 
indefinable manner it convey to me the purport of its visit. 
to-night, at twelve o'clock, we are to go to the house of a hindu, 
call karaver, in berner street, where we shall be initiate into 
the second stage of our compact." 

"i hope to goodness we shan't see any spectral tree or striped 
figures--i've had enough of them," kelson said. 

"then take care you don't do anything that might lead to the break 
of the compact," hamar retorted, "otherwise you'll see something far 
worse." 

shortly before midnight, hamar, curtis and kelson, obey the 
injunction hamar had received, set off to berner street, where they 
had little difficulty in finding karaver's house. 

to their astonishment karaver was expect them. 

"how did you know we were coming," curtis asked. 

"a gentleman call here early this morning and told me," karaver 
explained. "he said three friends of his particularly wish to be on 
the astral plane, at twelve o'clock this evening, and that they would 
each pay me a hundred guineas, if i would show them how to get there. 
i demurred. the secret that have come down to me through generation 
of my cashmere ancestors, i tell only to a chosen few--those born 
under the sign of dejellum brava. 

"the stranger showing me the sign--written plain than i have ever 
seen it--in the palm of his hand, i at once consented, and i had no 
soon done so than he vanished. i knew then that i had been speak 
to an elemental--a spirit of my native mountains." 

"my nerves are not in a condition to stand much. is there anything 
very alarm in this astral business?" kelson asked. 

"it depend on what you call alarming," the indian said coldly. "i 
shouldn't be alarmed." 

"don't be a fool, matt," hamar interposed. "i never saw such a 
frightened idiot in my life. you ought to be ashamed of yourself. 
think of what there is at stake." 

"think of lilian rosenberg," curtis whispered, "and be comforted." 

karaver took them upstairs into a dim light attic. in the centre 
of the carpetless floor was a tripod, around which the three were told 
to sit. karaver then proceed to pour into an iron vessel a mixture 
compose of: 1/2 oz. of hemlock, 3/4 oz. of henbane, 2 oz. of opium, 1 
oz. of mandrake roots, 2 oz. of poppy seeds, 1/2 oz. of assafoetida, 
and 1/4 oz. of saffron. 

"are these preparation absolutely necessary?" kelson asked. 

"absolutely," karaver said. "english clairvoyant will, doubtless, 
tell you they are not necessary. it is their custom, with a few 
slipshod instructions, to lead you to suppose that get on the 
astral plane is mere child's play. it is not! it is extremely 
difficult and can only be done, in the first place, through the 
guidance of a skilled oriental occultist." 

he then took a sword, and with it making the sign of a triangle in the 
air, afterwards scratch a triangle on the floor, over which, in red 
chalk, he superscribe a tree, an eye, and a hand. then he heated the 
mixture in the iron vessel over an oil stove. as soon as fumes arose 
from it, he placed it on the tripod, crying, "great spirits of the 
mountains, river and bowels of the earth, invest me with the heavy 
seal, in order that i may conduct these three seek after knowledge 
to the realm of thy eternal phantoms." 

immediately after this oration karaver, dip a twig of hazel in the 
fumigation, wave it north, south, east and west crying "give me 
authority! give me ka-ta-la-derany;" and then kneel down in front 
of the brazier, in a drone voice repeated these words: 

"green phantom figures of the air, 
a ready welcome see that you prepare. 
black phantom figures from the earth, 
of friendly salutation see there is no dearth. 
red phantom figures of the furious fire, 
for kindly greeting change your usual ire. 
grey, grizzly googy from the wood and dells, 
to gentle whisper change your harrowing yells. 
flagae, devas, mara rupas,[19] hie to the plane, the astral plane, 
and to these three poor fools, explain, explain 
the secret that they wish to learn, to learn!" 

the mixture in the iron vessel was now give off such dense fumes that 
hamar, curtis and kelson felt their senses slow ebb away. the 
dark, lithe form of karaver, his swarthy face and gleam teeth 
recede farther and farther into the background, whilst his voice 
appear to grow faint and fainter. they were dim conscious that 
he spray them all over with some sweet-smelling scent,[20] and that 
he whisper (in reality he spoke in his normal tones) these words: 
"darkona--droomer--doober--parlar--poohmer--perler. a--ta-rama-- 
skatarinek--ook--drooksi--noomig--viartikorsa."[21] then there came a 
temporary blank, which was broken by a sudden burst of light. the 
light, at first, was so blind that they involuntary closed their 
eyes. it was quite different to any light they had been accustomed 
to--it was far more vivid, and was in a perpetual state of vibration. 
when they had got sufficient use to this dazzle effect to keep 
their eyes open, they became aware that they were standing, apparently 
on nothing, that the atmosphere was not compose of air such as they 
knew, but of an indescribable something that render the act of 
breathing wholly unnecessary, and that all around them was no ground, 
no scenery, but only--space! 

they had barely finished remark on these facts, when there sudden 
glide across their vision, forms--of every conceivable shape, _i.e._, 
those resemble corpse of human being and animals, with bloodless 
faces, glassy eyes and stiff limbs--some apparently just dead and 
other in an advanced state of decomposition, all possessed and 
propel by impersonate elementals; phantom of actual earthbound 
people--misers, murderers, etc., several of whom approach the trio 
and try to peer into their faces. 

"for heaven's sake keep off!" kelson shrieked, as the vibrate form 
of an epileptic imbecile, with protrude blue eyes and pimple cheeks, 
came up to him, and thrust its face into his. 

"this is a bit thick," hamar said, vain attempt to elude the 
phantom of a short, stout woman with a big head and purple face, who, 
putt out a large black, swollen tongue, leer at him. 

"curse you! d--n you!" curtis screamed, throw out his hand in a 
vain endeavour to beat off the phantom of two idiot boys, who were 
try to bite him with their loose, dribble mouths. "a little more 
of this, and i shall go mad!" 

seeing a tall, grey phantom with a man's body and wolf's head bound 
up to them, kelson would have run away, had not hamar, whose presence 
of mind never quite desert him, grip him by the arm. "if you 
leave us, matt," he said, "we are lost. i feel our safety depend on 
our keeping together. if i'm not mistaken this is a cunning dodge on 
the part of the unknown to separate us. if that happens, i feel we may 
never get back to our bodies--and the compact will then be broken. we 
must hang on to each other at all costs." so saying, he slip his 
free arm through that of curtis, and the three stood link together. 

hamar clung on to the other two, until his hand grew numb, and 
the sweat stood on his chest and forehead in great beads. as figure 
after figure stealthy and noiseless approach them, kelson and 
curtis writhe and shrieked; and, at times, it seem as if the 
chain must be broken. but alarm as were these harrowing type of 
vice-elementals--_i.e._, nude things with head of beast and body of 
men and women; grotesque heads; malevolent eyes; mal-shaped hands; 
headless beasts, etc.; none had so dangerous an effect on the unity of 
the trio as the allure type of vice-elementals, _i.e._, shape of 
beautiful women that smile seductive at kelson, and resort to 
every device to entice him away with them. it was then that hamar was 
tax to the utmost, that he exhaust voice, strength, and patience, 
in holding kelson back. 

he was about to give in, when to his astonishment these vice-elementals 
vanished, and a phantasm, the exact counterpart of karaver, only much 
taller, appear before them, and commence give them instruction 
as to stage two. 

"you," he said, address hamar, "will possess the property of second 
sight, _i.e._, the power to see, at will, earthbound spirits, 
conditionally, that you fumigate your room, for ten minutes every 
night, before retiring to rest, with a mixture compose of 2 drachm 
of henbane, 3 drachm of saffron, 1/2 oz. of aloes, 1/4 oz. of 
mandrake, 3 drachm of salanum, 2 oz. of assafoetida; that you abstain 
from animal food and wine, and give up smoking; that, three times 
every day, you bathe your face in distill water, to which has been 
add three drops of the juice of the whortleberry, one drop of the 
juice of the mountain ash berry, 1 oz. of lavender water, 1 oz. of 
nitre, and 1/2 oz. of tincture of arnica; and that, just before go 
to sleep, you look for three minutes, without blinking, at an 
equilateral triangle, transcribe in blood, on white paper, and 
compose of these letters and figures." and he hand hamar a piece of 
paper, on which were written these symbols: 

k.t.o.p.i.6.x.7.4.h.i.p.3.s.4.w.v.2.8. 

"so long as you observe these conditions the power will remain with 
you. to-morrow, only, it will be award you without any 
preparations." 

"you," he went on, turning to kelson, "will possess the property of 
projection, _i.e._, the power of leave your body, and of visiting, 
where you will, on the material plane. you will continue to possess 
the same, conditionally, that you carry out the same rule as leon 
hamar, with the exception that, instead of look at a triangle 
before go to sleep, you will repeat these words. see, i have 
written them down for you." and he hand kelson a slip of paper, on 
which were transcribe "darkona, droomer, doober, parlar, poohmer, 
perler. a--ta--rama--skatarinek--ook--drooksi--noomeg--viartikorsa." 

"you," he said, turning to curtis, "will be endow with the property 
of overcome gravity, _i.e._, you will be able to fly, to jump great 
heights, and to lift and move prodigious weights; and this property 
will remain in your possession during the prescribed period, provided 
you abstain from all animal food, from smoking and from drink 
alcohol; and observe the same rule with regard to fumigate your 
sleep apartment, and bathing your face, as hamar and kelson. but, 
always, before you attempt to fly or to jump, it will be necessary for 
you to set in motion certain vibrations, in the ether, that counteract 
the attraction of gravity. you must repeat the words 'karjako 
mandarbsa guahseela,' which i have written on this blue paper; and 
when you want to move or lift objects, you must first repeat the words 
'perabibo henlilee oko-kokotse,' which i have written on this green 
paper. gravity, as you will see, is entire dependent on sound--sound 
can move mountains. it did so in atlantis, it did so in egypt." 

making the sign of a triangle, an eye, and a tree in the air, with the 
forefinger of his left hand, he slow repeated the words 
"barjakva--ookpoota--trylisa." and the conclude syllable was no 
soon uttered, than the trio found themselves standing in berner 
street. but of karaver's house--the house they had just quitted--there 
was no trace.
