with a ghastly groan he roll off his seat on to the floor, where he 
writhe and grovel in the most dreadful agony, whilst his assailant 
continue to stab and jab at him. 

in all probability, she would have succeeded, eventually, in reach 
some vital part of his body, had not one of the frenzied passenger 
pull the communication-cord and stop the train! 




chapter xix 

a series of misadventure 


with the advent of the guard, hamar's assailant was drag off him, 
and he was lock up in a separate compartment, "to be given in 
charge," so the indignant official announced, directly they got to 
brighton. but hamar ordain it otherwise. as soon as he had 
sufficient recover from the effects of the severe castigate the 
female furioso had inflict on him, he became invisible, and when the 
train drew up at the brighton platform, and a couple of policemen 
arrive to march him on, he was nowhere to be found! this was his 
first experiment with the newly acquire property. "in future," he 
said to himself, "before i try any tricks, i'll take very good care 
there are no suffragette about." 

in london there was, of course, no need for him ever to pay fares. all 
he had to do, was to become invisible as soon as the taxi stopped, 
calm step out of the vehicle, and walk away. as for meals, he was 
able to enjoy many--gratis. he simply walk into a restaurant, fed on 
the very best, and then disappeared. of course, he could not repeat 
the trick in the same place, and cautious though he was, he was at 
last caught. it appear that a description of him had been circulate 
among the police, and that private detective were employ to watch 
for him in the principal hotel and restaurants. consequently, 
directly he enter the grill room at the piccadil hotel, he was 
arrest and handcuff before he had time to swallow a pill. 

he was now in a most unpleasant predicament--the tightest corner he 
had ever been in. supposing he could not escape--his sentence would be 
at the least two years' penal servitude--what would happen? curtis and 
kelson would never work the show without him. curtis would give 
himself entire up to eat and drinking, kelson would marry lilian 
rosenberg; the compact with the unknown would be broken; and after 
that--he dare not think. he must escape! he must get at the pills! the 
police took him away in a taxi, and all the time he sat between them, 
he struggle desperate to squeeze his hand through the small, cruel 
circle that held them. "it's all right for curtis and kelson!" he said 
to himself, "all right at least--now! they know nothing! they have 
never try to think what the break of the compact means! their 
weak, silly mind are entire centre on the present! the present! 
damn the present! they are fools, idiots, imbecile who think only of 
the present--it's the future--the future that matters!" he scrape the 
skin off his wrists, he sweated, he swore! and it was not until one of 
the detective threaten to rap him over the head, that he sullen 
gave in and sat still. 

the taxi drew up in front of the gerald road police station, and hamar 
was conduct to an ante-room, prior to being taken before the 
inspector. just as a policeman was about to search him, he made one 
last desperate effort. 

"look here," he said, "if i pledge you my word i'll not attempt to do 
anything, will you let me have my hands--or at least one of my 
hands--free a moment. some grit has got in my eye and i cannot stand 
the irritation." 

"that game won't work here," one of the detective said, "you should 
keep your eyes shut when there's dust about, or else not have such 
protrude ones." 

hamar threaten to report him to the home secretary for brutal 
conduct, but the detective only laughed, and hamar had to submit to 
the mortification of being searched. 

"what are these?" a detective said, fingering the seaweed pill 
gingerly. 

"stomachic pills!" hamar said bitterly, "they are taken as a digestive 
after meals. you look dyspeptic--have one." 

"now, none of your sauce!" the detective said, "you come along with 
me,"--and hamar was haul before the inspector. 

"can i go out on bail?" hamar asked. 

"certainly not," the inspector replied. 

"then i shan't give you my name and address," hamar said. "i shan't 
tell you anything." 

the inspector merely shrug his shoulders, and after the charge 
sheet was read over, hamar was conduct to a cell. 

"this is awful," he said, "what the deuce am i to do! to send for 
curtis and kelson will be fatal, and it will be equally fatal to leave 
them in ignorance of what has happen to me. i am, indeed, in the 
horn of a dilemma. i must get at those pills." 

up and down the floor of the tiny cell he paced, his mind torture 
with a thousand conflict emotions. and then, an idea struck him. he 
would ask to be allow to see his lawyer. 

"cotton's the man," he said to himself, "he will get the pill for 
me!" 

the inspector, after satisfying himself that cotton was on the 
register, rang him up, and after an hour of terrible suspense to 
hamar, the lawyer brisk enter his cell. 

they confer together for some minutes, and have arrange the 
method of defence, cotton was prepare to depart, when hamar 
whisper to him-- 

"i want you to do me a particular favour. in the top right hand drawer 
of the chest of drawers in my bedroom, in cockspur street, i have left 
a red pill-box. these pill are for indigestion. i simply can't do 
without them. will you get them for me?" 

"what, to-night?" the lawyer ask dubiously. 

"yes, to-night," hamar pleaded. "i'll make it a matter of business 
between us--get me the pill before eight o'clock, and you have £1000 
down. my cheque book is in the same drawer." 

the lawyer said nothing, but gave hamar a look that meant much! 

again there was a dreadful wait, and hamar had abandoned himself to 
the deepest despair when cotton reappeared. he shook hand with his 
client, slip the pill into the latter's palm. whilst the lawyer 
was pocket his cheque, hamar gleeful swallow a pill, and crying 
out "bakra--naka--takso--mana,"--vanished! 

"heaven preserve us! what's become of you?" cotton exclaimed, putt 
his hand to his forehead and leaning against the wall for support. "am 
i ill or dreaming?" 

"anything wrong, sir?" a policeman inquired, opening the cell door and 
look in. "why, what have you done with the prisoner--where is he?" 

"i have no more idea than you," the lawyer gasped. "he was talk to 
me quite naturally, when he sudden left off--said something 
idiotic--and disappeared." 

hamar did not dally. he quiet slip through the open door, and 
dart swift along a stone passage, found his way to the entrance, 
which was block by two constable with their back to him. 

"i'll give the brute something to remember me by," hamar chuckled, 
and, taking a run, he kick first one, and then the other with all 
his might, precipitate them both into the street. he then sped past 
them--home. 

hamar, by astute inquiries, learned that the police had decided to 
hush up the affair, not being quite sure how they had figured, or, 
indeed, what had actually occurred. as to cotton, the shock he had 
undergone, at seeing hamar sudden melt away before his eyes, was so 
great that he went off his head, and had to be confine in an asylum. 

after this adventure hamar shun restaurants, and manipulate his 
new property sparingly, and with the utmost caution, warn kelson and 
curtis to do the same. 

"i'll bet anything," he said to them, "it was a put-up job on the part 
of the unknown--a cunning device to make us break the compact." 

"oh, we'll be careful enough as far as that goes," curtis growled. 
"it's this vegetarian diet that i can't stick. fancy living on bean 
and potatoes, and only milk and aerate water to wash them down. it 
was bad enough in san francisco, when we hadn't the means even to 
smell meat cooking--but with the money literally burning a hole in 
one's pocket, it's ten times worse! whatever the unknown has in store 
for us it can't be a worse hell than what i've got now. what say you, 
matt?" 

"the same! precisely the same!" kelson said. "only it's love--not 
potato and bean that worry me. in the old days when i was 
penniless, i did get some consolation from knowing it was all 
hopeless--but now--now, when, as ed says, 'the money's literally 
burning a hole in one's pocket,' and everything might go 
swimmingly--not to be allow even to buy a bracelet--is more than 
human nature can endure. i certainly can't conceive a hell to beat 
it." 

"don't be too sure," hamar said, "and for goodness' sake don't let the 
unknown give you an opportunity of comparing." 

the night succeed this conversation, hamar, curtis and kelson 
introduce their new property into the programme of their 
entertainment in cockspur street, and london got another big thrill. 
hamar exhibit such startle proof of his power of invisibility, 
that not only was the whole audience convinced, but from amongst 
certain prominent member of the council of the psychic research 
society, who were attend with the express purpose of unmask 
hamar, two had epileptic fit on the spot, and several, before they 
could get home, became raving lunatics. 

at the commencement of the second part of the programme--the audience 
was still too flabbergast to fully grasp what was happening. they 
saw on the stage a huge tank of water--with which they were told mr. 
curtis would experiment. 

"what i am about to do," mr. curtis--who now walk on to the 
stage--informed his audience, "is quite simple. all you want is faith. 
those of you who are christian scientist should be able to do it as 
easily as i. say 'i will! i will walk on the water!' and your 
faith--your colossal faith--faith in your ability to do it will 
actually enable you to do it." 

curtis then repeated--in tone that could not be heard by the 
audience--the atlantean cabalistic words--"karma--nardka--rapto-- 
nooman--k--arma--oola--piskooskte," and glide graceful on to the 
surface of the water. every now and then he sank slow down to the 
bottom, where he stroll about, or sat, or lay down. 

the audience was simply fascinated. nothing they had hitherto seen 
tickle their fancy half as much. as an american, who was present, put 
it--"to live under the water like a fish is immense--so hygienic and 
economical." 

though the time apportion to this part of the entertainment was 
half an hour, it was extend to over an hour, and even then the 
audience was not satisfied. they would have gone on watch 
curtis--eating--drinking--jumping--skipping--singing and chase gold 
fish--under the water all night, and when he was at length permit to 
come out of the tank--exhausted and sulky--they gave him even hearty 
applause than they had given hamar. 

but the cup of their enjoyment was not yet full. the greatest treat of 
all was in store for them. 

for the third and last part of the entertainment, a cage, contain a 
large bengal tiger, was wheel on to the stage. 

"you look precious white," curtis remarked, just as kelson was about 
to go on. 

"i guess you'd look the same," kelson retorted, "if you had to hobnob 
with a tiger. the unknown always give me the nasty jobs." 

"and in this case," curtis said with a low, mock laugh, "it also 
load you with consolations. the house is full of ladies who adore 
you, and if you are eaten, just think of the sympathy well up in 
their beautiful eyes! if that isn't sufficient compensation for you, 
i--" but the remainder of this encourage speech was lost in a loud 
roar. the bengal tiger shook its bars--the audience screamed, and 
curtis flew. 

with a desperate attempt to look calm, kelson, clutch the red laryx 
stone in his left hand, walk on to the stage, whilst the tiger, 
rear on its hind leg try to reach him with its paws. 

there were loud cries of "oh! oh!" from the audience, and kelson's 
heart beat quicker, when a girl with wavy, fair hair and big, starry 
eyes, scream out "don't go near it! don't go near it!" 

as soon as there was comparative quiet kelson spoke. 

"as you can see, ladies and gentlemen," he said, "this animal is 
genuine savage! it is not like the tiger one see in menageries, 
drug and deprived of their natural weapons--teeth and claws. it 
come direct from india, where its reputation as a man-eater is 
widespread. i am not, however, intimidated--its growl merely amuse 
me." 

quake all over, he approach the cage, and staring fixedly into the 
tiger's face, made the prescribed passes. in an instant, the whole 
attitude of the great cat changed. drop on to its fore-legs, it 
rub its head against the bar and purred. a low buzz of 
astonishment burst from the audience, and kelson, now assured that the 
spell had worked, wave his disengaged hand, in the most gallant 
fashion, at the audience, and strut into the cage. he shook paw 
with the tiger, pat it on the back, sat down by its side, and, 
whilst pretend to be on the most familiar terms with it, took every 
precaution to avoid coming in too close contact with its teeth and 
claws. 

the audience was charmed--the men cheered, the ladies wave 
handkerchiefs, and the only disappointed person present were a few 
belligerent and bloodthirsty boys, and a suffragette, who severally, 
and for diverse reasons, would have relish the performance of a 
savage tiger, but had little sympathy with the performance of a tame 
one. 

the next surprise that mr. kelson had for his audience, was the 
announcement that he could interpret the language of animals. at his 
invitation, a dozen member of the audience came on to the platform 
and stood near the cage. look steady at the tiger he then 
pronounced the mystic words "meta--ra--ka--va--avakana," holding up 
his right hand, with the thumb turn down and stretch right across 
the palm, and the little finger extend to the utmost. in an instant 
the great secret--the secret that darwin had studied so strenuous 
for years--was reveal to him. the language of animal was olfactory. 
the tiger spoke to him through the sense of smell--through his nose 
instead of his ears. it regulate and modify the odour it gave off 
from its body, and which work its way out through the pore of its 
skin, just as human being regulate and modify the intonation of 
their voices. indeed, so delicate are the olfactory organ of animal 
that the faintest of these language smell make an impression on 
them, which impression is at once interpret by the brain. if an 
animal wish to leave a message behind it, it merely impregnate some 
article--a leaf or a root, or a clump of grass--or merely the ether 
with a brain smell, and any other animal, happening to pass by the 
spot, within a certain time (in favourable weather), will at once be 
attract by the smell, and be able to interpret it. that is the 
reason one so often see an animal sudden stop at a spot and sniff 
it--it is reading some message left there by some other animal. all 
this, and more, kelson explain to his audience, who were exceedingly 
interested, many of them get up to ask him questions. he also 
report to them the tiger's conversation, which consist chiefly of 
complaint against the management with regard to its food. 

"to be everlastingly fed on scraps of horse-flesh," it said, "when 
there were dozen of plump young women sitting in the stalls, under 
its very nose, was tantalize to a degree. would mr. kelson kindly 
speak to whoever was responsible for such cruelty and negligence?" 

a bear and a crocodile have been tame in the same manner, and their 
remark interpret to the audience, the entertainment concluded. 

the next day the papers were full of it. 

the _planet_, under the startle announcements-- 

"recovery of the lost senses. 
more extraordinary feat in cockspur street. 
leon hamar become invisible at will," 

--narrated all that had occurred. 

the _monitor_--if anything more sensational--declared-- 

"the language of animal discover at last! 
the problem of breathing under water--solved! 
dematerialize at will established!" 

and even the _courier_--the steady, ever cautious old _courier_, 
england's premier paper, create a precedent by the use of a quite 
conspicuous large type; vide the following-- 

"the age of miracle revived! 
actual case of subdue and converse with wild animals. 
recovery of the property of invisibility; of walking on water, 
and of breathing under water." 

as before, there were innumerable case of imitation, many of them, 
unhappily, result in the death of the imitator. at dover, for 
instance, a congregationalist minister convinced that he had the 
requisite amount of faith, announce from the pulpit, that he intended 
walking on the water, in the harbour, after service. thousand flock 
to see him, but despite the fact that he said "i will! i will!" with 
the greatest emphasis, the unkind wave would not support him. indeed, 
since they swallow him, it might almost be said that the rev. s---- 
support the waves. 

for two whole days there was regular stampede of experiment to 
hyde park and regent's park, and the bank of their respective waters 
resound with the words, "i will walk! i will walk!" succeed by 
splash and cries for help. 

nor was the water feat the only one that induce imitators. crowd 
flock to the zoology gardens, and the various house were 
literally packed with people try to get into conversation with the 
animals; these attempt being also marked by a large proportion of 
fatal results. one old gentleman--a fellow of the royal 
society--carried away in his enthusiasm to talk with a tiger, after 
making what he thought to be the correct signs, slip his nose 
through the bar of the tiger's cage, and had it prompt bitten 
off--whilst a girl, in her endeavour to sniff the crocodiles, and so 
get in conversation with them, fell in their midst, and was torn to 
pieces before help arrived. 

however, these fatality only serve as an advertisement to the firm, 
and hundred of people, for whom there was not even standing room, 
were turn away from the house nightly. 

but later on there were hitches. curtis, whose dislike to vegetarian 
diet steady increased, when dine one evening at his club, could no 
long withstand the sight of roast beef. the smell of it tickle his 
palate unmercifully. 

"take this infernal mess away!" he said, push a plate of nut steak 
from him in disgust, "and let me have a full course--entrée, soup, 
fish, meat, everything you've got--chartreuse and a liqueur, and bring 
it quick--i'm famished." 

he ate and ate, and drank and drank, until it was as much as he could 
do to rise from the table. and then, in excellent spirits, he repair 
to cockspur street. 

how he got on to the stage he could never tell. everything was in a 
haze around him, until there was a dull crash in his ears, and he 
sudden found himself drowning. no one, at first, notice his 
helpless condition, but attribute his antics to part of the 
programme; and he most certainly would have been drowned, had it not 
been for lilian rosenberg, who, being quite by chance, in front of the 
house, perceive he was drunk, the moment he came on the stage. she 
flew to the wings, and, just in the nick of time, got two of the 
super to haul him out of the tank. of course, it was announced--with 
a pretty apology--by mr. hamar, that mr. curtis had been taken ill. 
kelson immediately came on with his animals, and the audience departed 
without the slightest suspicion as to the truth. 

hamar was furious. 

"you idiot!" he said to curtis, "that all come of your making a beast 
of yourself--you would sacrifice matt and me, for your insatiable 
craving for meat and alcohol. can't you see it was a trick of the 
unknown to make us break the compact? had you been drowned, the 
partnership, would, of course, have been dissolved--and it would have 
been your fault! you must obey your injunctions! damn it, you must!" 
and hamar spoke so fierce that curtis was for once in a way cowed, 
and solemn promise that he would not repeat the offence. 

kelson was the next culprit; and his misdoing were indirect 
associate with the foregoing incident. lilian rosenberg's action in 
saving curtis's life, thrill him to the core, and call into play 
all his ardent passion. he had seen her sitting in the front of the 
house, and had come upon the scene just as she was urge the super 
to go to curtis's assistance; and he then thought she had never look 
so lovely. 

"come out with me to-morrow afternoon," he whispered. "hamar's go 
out of town!" and before she could stop him he had kiss her. 

kelson hardly expect lilian rosenberg would accept his invitation, 
but on arrive at the place he had named, he was delight beyond 
measure to find her there. 

nor could anyone have been nice to him. no girl, he told himself, who 
did not in some degree at least, reciprocate his sentiments, could 
have allow him to stare into her eyes as she did, or squeeze her 
hands, as he did. he took her to the ladies' drawing-room of his club, 
where there were plenty of quiet, secluded nooks, and there, whilst 
she pour out tea for him, he once more related to her all his early 
deed and ailments--real and imaginary--and all his ideal and 
aspirations. 

lilian rosenberg was most sympathetic. 

"you should have been a poet," she said. "there is something about you 
that is quite byronic." 

and kelson, who had never even heard of byron, was immensely 
flattered. 

"will you come to the jeweller's with me," he said, "and choose 
whatever you like best. those finger of yours are made for 
rings--rings of all sorts!" and he gave them a gentle pressure. 

she let him escort her to bond street, and follow him gaily into 
raymond's; but when it came to accept a ring from him, she 
laughing refused, and chose, instead, the most expensive diamond 
bracelets and pendant in the shop. some of these she wore--the 
rest--unknown to him of course--she sold; send the proceeds, 
anonymously, to shiel davenport--who was starving. 

when kelson went on the stage, that evening, his thought were so far 
away--planning for his honeymoon--that he enter the cage of a newly 
import lion without have made the necessary signs, and would most 
certainly have been mangle out of recognition, had not one of the 
supers, perceive how matter lay, rush to his assistance, and kept 
the lion at bay with a pole, till further help could be procured. it 
had been a narrow squeak, and to kelson the bare idea of continue 
his performance was appalling. his nerves were, as he himself put it, 
anyhow, and he prefer retiring for the rest of the evening. 

but hamar would not hear of it. 

"this is the second bungle we have had," he said, "and the reputation 
of the firm is seriously at stake. you must go on again and retrieve 
it." 

and kelson, tremble all over, was oblige to reappear. 

after it was all over, and he had bowed himself out into the wings, 
hamar led him aside. 

"don't look so damn pleased with yourself," he said, "i don't half 
like the look of things. this is the third time the unknown has try 
to trap us--the fourth time it may be successful! take care!" 




chapter xx 

the stage of haunting 


much to the relief of the trio, the end of stage three was at length 
reached--and, thanks to hamar, reach without further mishap. to keep 
curtis and kelson up to the mark, hamar had work indefatigably. he 
had never relax his effort in the strict watch he kept over them, 
and he had unceasing impress upon them, the vital importance of 
obeying, to the very letter, the instruction they had received from 
the unknown. 

the part he had thus taken upon himself, the difficulty he had to 
encounter in this uncease vigilance, had produce a new hamar--a 
hamar that was a personality; a personality so utterly unlike the 
old hamar--the meek and servile clerk--as to make one wonder if 
there could possibly be two hamars--outwardly and physically the 
same--inwardly and psychological diametrically opposed. a year ago, 
curtis and kelson would have ridicule the idea of being afraid of 
hamar--such an idea would have struck them as simply absurd; but they 
were afraid of him now, they dread his anger more than anything, 
more even than the prospect of infringe their compact with the 
unknown. 

"we have made pot of money," curtis remark one day. "why can't we 
give up work and enjoy it?" 

"because i say no!" hamar hissed. "no! we can't give up--not, at 
least, until the last stage has been safe gone through. to give up 
now would be to break the compact!" 

"well, why not?" curtis mumbled. 

"why not!" hamar cried. "heavens, man, can't you understand! can you 
form no conception of what failure to keep the compact means? has the 
memory of that night--of that tree and all the foul things it 
suggested, pass completely out of your mind? it hasn't out of 
mine--it is as clear now as it was then. and often--mark this, both of 
you--often when i am alone in the night, i see queer luminous 
shapes--shapes of repulsive vegetable growths--of polyps--and of 
disgust tongue that come towards me through the gloom and circle 
slow round the bed, whilst the whole room vibrate with soft, 
mock laughter! you know how mirror shine in the moonlight. well, 
the other night, when i look at mine, i saw in it the reflection, 
not of a face, but of two light evil eyes that look at me 
and--smiled! smile with a smile that said more plainly than words, 'i 
am waiting!' and that is what the shapes, and the very atmosphere of 
the place at night always seem to say--'we are waiting! you are 
enjoy the joke now--we shall enjoy it later on!' if we knew exactly 
what was in store for us it wouldn't be so bad, but it is the 
vague of it, the vague of the horrors that the unknown has 
hint at, that make it so appalling! we may die awful deaths--or we 
may not die at all--the shapes, indefinite and misty no longer, but 
materialized--wholly and entire materialized--may come for us and 
take us away with them! and it is to prevent this, that i am urge 
you, compel you, to stick to the compact, and give the unknown no 
loophole! think of the tremendous rewards, if we succeed in passing 
through the last stage! as i have said before, curtis need do nothing 
else but eat, whilst you, matt, can become a mormon and marry all the 
pretty girl in london!" 

this speech had the desire effect, and nothing more--for the time at 
least--was said about retiring. 

"do you think leon is quite--er--like--er--like us?" kelson said, when 
hamar left them, after administer his admonition. "at times he 
hardly looks human. his face is such a funny colour, such a lurid 
yellow, and his eyes, so piercing! he give me the jumps! i can't bear 
to think of him at night!" 

"rubbish," curtis growled. "you imagine it. there's nothing of the 
spook about leon! he's of this world and nothing but this world." 

it was odd, however, that from that time he, too, began to have the 
same feeling--the feeling that hamar was perpetual watch 
them--watching them awake and watch them asleep! curtis awoke one 
night to see, standing on his hearth, a shadowy figure with a lurid 
yellow face and two gleam dark eyes, which were fixed on him. he 
call out, and it vanished! 

"of course it's the nut steak!" and thus he try to assure himself. 
but he was badly scared all the same. 

another night, he saw some one, he took to be hamar, peep at him 
from behind the window curtains. he threw a slipper at the figure, and 
the slipper went right through it. if hamar's phantom had been the 
only thing he saw, he would not have minded much; but both he and 
kelson soon began to see and hear other things. curtis frequent saw 
half-materialized forms, form of men with cone-shaped head and 
peculiarly form limbs, steal up the staircase in front of him, 
and, turning into his bedroom, vanish there. he heard them moving 
about, long after he had got into bed. sometimes they would glide up 
to the bed and bend over him, and though he could never see their 
eyes, he could feel they were fixed mocking on him. once he saw the 
door of his wardrobe slow open, and a white something with a 
dreadful face--half human and half animal--steal sly out and 
disappear in the wall opposite. and once when he put out his hand to 
feel for the matches, they were gently thrust into his palm, whilst 
the wall of the room shook with laughter. 

kelson was equally tormented, though the phenomena took rather a 
different form. alone in his bedroom at night, the shape of the room 
would frequent change; either the wall and ceiling would recede, 
and recede, until they assume the proportions of some vast chamber, 
full of gloom and strange shadows; or they would slowly, very slowly, 
close in upon him, as if it were their intention to crush him to 
death. a feeling of suffocate would come over him, and he would 
gasp, choke, beat the air with his arms, be at the verge of lose 
consciousness, when there would be a loud, mock laugh--and the 
wall and ceiling would be in their proper place again. at other 
times he would see strange figures on the wall--numbers of circles, 
that would keep on revolving in the most bewilder fashion. then, 
suddenly, they would leave the wall and slow approach him, 
increase in circumference; and the same thing would happen, as 
happen with the wall and ceiling; he would undergo the whole 
sensation of asphyxiation, and be on the brink of swooning, when there 
would be a loud peal of evil, satirical laughter, and the circle 
would instantly disappear. 

sometimes the bedclothes would assume extraordinary shapes; sometimes 
the articles on his dressing-table; sometimes his clothes; and once, 
when he was about to put on his bedroom slippers, he found them 
already occupied--occupied by icy cold feet. another time, when he put 
out his hand to take hold of a tumbler, he put it on the back of 
another hand--smooth, cold and pulpy! 

hardly a night pass without some sort of manifestation happening to 
one or other of the trio, and even curtis--fat and stolid 
curtis--began to lose flesh and look harassed. 

on the eve of the initiation into stage four, the three, separate 
for the night, retired to their respective quarters in a far from 
pleasant state of expectation. 

hamar was undressing, when there came a loud ring at the telephone, 
outside his door. 

"holloa!" he call out, "who are you?" 

"are you mr. hamar?" a voice asked, breathlessly. 

hamar reply in the affirmative, and the voice continued-- 

"i'm mrs. anderson-waite, of 30 queen's mansions, queen's gate. i have 
been holding a séance here, with some of my friends, and most 
extraordinary things have happened, and are still happening. there are 
violent knock on the wall and ceiling, and the table has become 
positively dangerous. it has repeatedly sprung into the air, and 
savage assault several of the sitters. it has thrown one lady on 
to the floor, and despite our effort to prevent it, has rample on 
her so vicious that she is badly hurt, and the doctor who has just 
arrive think very seriously of it. we want to stop, but some 
strange power seem to be force us to go on. the table has rap 
out your name and address, and say it has something important to 
communicate with you, and that unless you come here at once, it won't 
answer for the consequences." 

"all right!" hamar said. "i'll come. i'll be with you in less than 
half an hour." 

when hamar arrive at queen's mansions, he found a terrify party of 
ladies await him in the entrance to the flat. 

"thank goodness, you've come!" they exclaimed, all together. "we've 
been have an awful time. the table has driven us out of the 
drawing-room--it is obsess by a devil." 

"let me have a look at it," hamar said, "and i'll soon tell you." 

the leader of the party, mrs. anderson-waite, very cautious open 
the drawing-room door, and hamar peer in. in the centre of the room 
was a large, round, ebony table, that commence to rock, in the most 
sinister fashion, the moment hamar look at it. 

"it evidently wants to speak with me," hamar said; "you had better 
leave me here with it for a few minutes." 

"do take care," mrs. anderson-waite said, as she shut the door. "it 
may want to murder you. if it does, ring this bell, and we will all 
come to your assistance." 

hamar gave her an assure smile, but he was by no means as much at 
ease as he pretended to be. he stood staring at the table, too 
fascinate to take his eyes off it, and too afraid to move. 

at length, however, pull himself together, and convinced the table 
was the medium, through which the unknown wish to give him fresh 
instructions, he stealthy approach it. he address it, and it 
rap out to him that he must at once obtain pen and ink and take 
down what it wish to say. 

obtain the requisite material from mrs. anderson-waite, he sat 
down and was prepare to write on his knee, when the table told him 
to rub its surface brisk with his left hand, to trace on it the 
three atlantean symbols, _i.e._ a club foot, a hand with the finger 
clench and the long pointed thumb standing upright, and a bat--and 
then--to place his paper on it, and transcribe what it had to say. 

hamar obeyed, and after sitting for exactly three minutes with his 
pencil between his fingers, he felt a cold, pulpy hand laid over his, 
impel him to write with lightning-like rapidity. the script read 
as follows:-- 

"to hamar, curtis and kelson--to the three of you in common--is given 
the knowledge of inflict all manner of torment and diseases, of 
impart all kind of injurious properties, and of cause plagues. 

"in the first place, you must understand that the essence of life, 
comprise the psychical, psychological and physical, permeate every 
part of the living corporeal body--and that any limb, or fragment of 
skin or flesh, cut off from the living corporeal body, retain the 
essence of life, comprise the psychic and physical in its full 
vigour and entirety. consequently, if a person have graft on to them 
a piece of skin or flesh, or be inoculate with the blood or vein of 
a tiger--then that person not merely become liable to all the 
physical infirmity of the tiger, but may--if the counteract 
influence are not sufficient strong--partake of all the tiger's 
psychological characteristics. 

"thus, if you give a person, in whom there is a latent tendency to 
drink, a drop of a drunkard's blood--in a glass of wine, or sweet, or 
pill, no matter what--that person will at once take to drink. 
thus--mark you--people can be metamorphose into libertines, suicides, 
idiot and murderers. this metamorphosis can also be produce by means 
of a magnet call the 'magnes microcosmi,' which is prepared from 
substance that have had a long association with the human body, and 
are penetrate by its vitality. such substance are the hair and 
blood. take either one of them, and dry it in a shady and moderately 
warm place, until it has lost its humidity and odour. by this process 
it will have lost, too, all its mumia--that is to say, its essence of 
life--and is hungry to regain it. it is now a magne microcosmi, or a 
magnet for attract disease and properties, and if it be placed in 
close contact with a criminal or lunatic, it will be fill with his 
essence of life, and may then be use as a means of infect other 
people with his pernicious qualities. bury it under the doorstep of 
the person you wish infected, or hide it in his house, or mix it well 
with earth, and plant a shrub in the earth, and the vitality the 
magnet took from the criminal or lunatic will pass into the plant; and 
if the plant, or even flower of the plant, be given to any one, that 
person--unless she or he be a person absolutely free from the germ of 
vice--will be attract to it, and greatly affected by it. 

"or again, the earth over the grave of a lunatic or criminal will 
contain his essence of life, _i.e._ his vitality, which impregnate 
everything around it, and if that earth be placed somewhere in the 
immediate presence of a person, in whom there are latent tendency to 
vice--then that person will be affected by it. 

"and through these method of use the essence of life, that is 
impregnate with the disease you wish to inflict--you may infect 
people with all kind of incurable ailments. 

"but a quicker, and equally sure method of smite people with 
disease, such as cancer, fever, epilepsy, apoplexy, etc.; of smite 
them blind, deaf, dumb, lame, etc.; or bring upon them all kind of 
accidents, is to make an image of the person you wish to torment, and, 
setting it in front of you, preferably, at times when the moon is new, 
or in conjunction with venus, mars or saturn, concentrate with all 
your will on whatever injury you wish to inflict. if, for example, you 
desire the person to become blind, stick a pin, or thorn, or nail in 
the eyes of the image; if deaf, in its ears; if maimed, cut a limb off 
the image; if to have a certain disease, will very earnest that he 
or she shall have that disease. you may thus, too, torment the object 
of your aversion with plague of insect and vermin. 

"if you desire to bewitch your neighbour's milk, wine, or any food he 
or she has, you may do it by place the mumia, _i.e._ the vehicle 
contain the essence of life of some criminal or lunatic, in the 
immediate vicinity of the food, etc.; or in the case of milk, by 
give it to the cow to eat; or you may accomplish your design simply 
by means of concentration and an image. 

"always, however, whatever method you employ, prelude them with this 
prayer: 'i conjure thee, great unknown power that is antagonist to 
man, that was at the beginning, that is now, that always will be; by 
the wind and rain, and thunder and lightning; by the swirl rivers; 
by the moon; by the sinister influence of the moon with venus, mars 
and saturn; help me obtain the perfect issue of all my desires, which 
i seek to perform solely for the further of what is detriment to 
humanity. amen.' and conclude them with the sign of the foot, the 
hand and the bat. if you desire to know anything further it will be 
unfold to you in your dreams." 

the hand that had been laid on hamar's was now removed. the writing 
ceased. the table rose several inch from the floor, and struck the 
latter three times in quick, violent succession. then it remain 
quiet, and hamar knew, by a subtle change in the atmosphere, that all 
occult manifestations--for that night at least--were at an end. the 
ladies were, of course, die to know what had happened; and like most 
ladies, who dabble in spiritualism, were ready to believe anything 
they were told. hamar, who had no intention whatever of telling them 
what had actually occurred, satisfy them admirably. 

he went home delighted--far too delight to sleep--for he had in his 
possession now the greatest of all weapons--the weapon to torment. and 
with it what could he not do! what could he not get! he could 
get--gladys! 




chapter xxi 

the sell of spell 


the period of stage four promise to be one of such a lucrative 
nature, that the trio set to work to profit by it at once. they bribe 
medical men to procure for them the mumia of people suffering from 
every kind of disease; of criminal lunatics; of idiot and epileptics; 
they obtained, by bribery also, the blood and hair of the most 
abandoned men and women--rakes, thieves, murderers. they bottle and 
labelled, and arrange and catalogued, the mumia, in a laboratory 
design for the purpose; and, when all their preparation were 
complete, advertised-- 

spell for sale 

the modern sorcery company ltd. 
offer for sale every variety of spells--love 
charms, sleep charms, etc. 

in order to carry out the principal conditions of the compact, namely, 
to do harm, they made pseudo-love charm as follows:-- 

they procure the hair of a girl whom they knew to be an incorrigible, 
and, at the same time, heartless flirt; and, in the manner describe 
(and related in the last chapter) made a magne microcosmi of it. when 
ready for use, _i.e._ after it had been in immediate contact with the 
girl's flesh, so as to get it fully charged, they had portion of it 
set in rings, locket and pendants. and the purchaser of any one of 
these trinket had only to persuade the object of his (or her) 
affection to wear it, and his (or her) love would at once be 
reciprocated. 

had the magne microcosmi been charged with real, deep-rooted love, 
the effect on the wear would have been highly satisfactory, but 
charged as it was with the effervescent and fleeting fancy of a flirt, 
the effect on whoever wore it could not be more disastrous. the 
sentiment of the hopeful purchaser would be reciprocate for a time, 
which would probably lead to marriage--after which the affection his 
adore had professed would sudden decrease, and before the honeymoon 
was over, would have vanish altogether. 

during the week following the announcement of the sale of these 
spells, over a thousand were sold, the applicant being mostly shop 
girls, typists, clerk and servants; in the second week the sales rose 
to three thousand, and every succeed week show a still greater 
increase. 

in charge the magne microcosmi, the motive of the purchaser had 
always to be taken into account. if the love charm were want by a 
woman--a housekeeper may be, who desire some rich old man to fall in 
love with her, in order that she might come into his property; or by a 
woman--a companion probably--who, have worm herself into the 
confidence of some eccentric old lady, was anxious that that lady 
should leave her all her money--hamar took care that the magne 
microcosmi should be charged with a lasting infatuation; and the sale 
of this love spell--the spell that was sought solely that the 
purchaser might inherit property to which he (or she) had no 
claim--far exceed the sale of any other spell. indeed, it was 
extraordinary how many people--people one would never have 
suspected--desired spell that would do other people harm. 

lady de greene, the well-known humanitarian, who was most 
indefatigable in get up petition to the home secretary, whenever 
the perpetrate of any particularly heinous and inexcusable murder was 
about to be hanged, and who was universally acknowledge "incapable of 
harm a fly," called, surreptitiously, on hamar. 

"i understand," she said, "everything you do here is in strict 
confidence!" 

"certainly, madam, certainly!" hamar said. "we make it a point of 
honour to divulge--nothing!" 

"that being so," lady de greene observed, "i want you to tell me of a 
spell that will hasten some very obnoxious person's death." 

"if you will give me a rough idea of their personal appearance," hamar 
said, "i will make a wax image of them, and undertake they will 
trouble you no longer." 

but lady de greene shook her head. she had no desire to commit 
herself. 

"can't you do it in any other way," she said, "can't you let me give 
them an unlucky charm--the sort of thing that might bring about a taxi 
disaster?" 

hamar thought for a moment and then--smiled. 

"yes!" he said, "i think i can accommodate you." 

leave her for a few minutes, he went to the laboratory, and from a 
tin box marked homicidal lunatic, he took a plain, gold ring. with 
this he return to lady de greene, murmur on the way the prayer he 
had learned from the table. 

"here you are," he said hand the ring to lady de greene, "give it 
to the person you have mention to me--and the result you desire will 
speedy come to pass." 

three days later, london was immeasurable shocked. it read in the 
papers that the highly accomplished lady de greene, beloved and 
respect by all, for the strenuous exertion on behalf of 
humanitarianism, had been barbarous murder by her husband (from 
whom--unknown to the public--she had been living apart for years), who 
had suddenly, and, for no apparent reason, become insane. hamar, who 
was immensely tickled, alone knew the reason why. 

this was no isolated case. score of society women came to the trio 
with the same request. "a spell, or charm, or something, that will 
bring about a fatal accident--not a lingering illness"--and the person 
for whom the accident was desired, was usually the husband. and the 
trio often indulge in grim jokes. 

without a doubt, lady minkhurst got her heart's desire when her 
husband abrupt cut his throat, but alas, amongst those decimated, 
when the charm fell into the hand of one of the footmen, was her 
ladyship's lover. 

again, mrs. jacques, the beauty, who, at one time, wrote for half the 
fashion papers in england, certainly secure the demise of colonel 
dick jacques, who tumble downstairs and broke his neck, but as in his 
fall the colonel alight on one of the maids, who was not insured, 
and so seriously injure her that she was pronounced a hopeless 
cripple, mrs. jacques--with whom money was an object--had, of course, 
to maintain her for the rest of her life. 

likewise, sir charles brimpton, in jump out of the top window of 
his house, besides pulverize himself, pulverized, too, lady 
brimpton's pet pekingese "waller," without whom, she declared, life 
wasn't worth living; and lord snipping, in setting fire to himself, 
set fire to lady snipping's boudoir (which he had been secret 
visiting), and thereby destroy treasure which she tearful 
declared were quite priceless, and could never be replaced. 

crowd of young married women were anxious to get rid of their rich 
old relatives, who clung on to life with a tenacity that was "most 
wearying." 

"can you give me a spell that will make my grandmother go off 
suddenly?" a girl with beautiful, sad eyes said plaintive to kelson. 
"don't think me very wicked, but we are not at all well off--and she 
has live such a long time--such a very long time." 

"you don't want her to be ill first, i suppose," kelson inquired. 

"oh, no!" the girl replied, "she lives with us and we could never 
endure the worry and trouble of nursing her. it must be something very 
sudden." 

"this will do it," kelson said, give her a locket contain the 
mumia or essence of life of a mad dog; "fasten it round the old lady's 
neck, and you will be astonish how soon it acts." 

"and what is your fee?" the girl asked, her eyes brim over with 
joyous anticipation. 

"for you--nothing," kelson said gallantly. "only tell no one. may i 
kiss your hand." 

the firm's sale of spell for get rid of husband have risen one 
day to five hundred--and the sale of their spell for putt old 
people out of the way to fifteen hundred--even hamar, who was no 
believer in the perfection of human nature, was astonished. 

"my word!" he remarked. "isn't this a revelation? who would have 
thought how many people have murder in their hearts? at least half 
society would, i believe, become homicide if only there were no 
chance of their being found out and punished. anyhow, if we go on at 
this rate there will be no old people left." 

and it did indeed seem as if such would be the case. for the moment 
the idea got abroad that old people could be thrust out of existence 
with absolute safety and ease, there was a perfect mania amongst men, 
women, and even children, to get rid of them, and the death of people 
over sixty record in the papers multiply every day. the following 
is an extract from the _planet_ of july 28-- 

bolt.--on july 27, at no. ---- elgin avenue, s.w., emily jane, 
love and venerate mother of mary bolt, m.d., in her 69th year. 
drown in her bath. and all the angel wept! 

cushman.--on july 27, at no. ---- sheep street, northampton, sarah 
elizabeth, adore mother of josiah cushman, plymouth brother, in 
her 88th year. run over by a taxi. joy in heaven! 

starling.--on july 27, at no. ---- snargate street, dover, susan, 
highly esteem and greatly beloved mother of alfred starling, 
wesleyan minister, in her 71st year. lost in the harbour. asleep in 
jesus. 

tretickler.--on july 27, at no. ---- the terrace, st. ives, 
cornwall, elizabeth, adore grandmother of tobia tretickler, 
congregationalist, in her 91st year. fell over the malatoff. "oh, 
paradise! oh, paradise!" 

broot.--on july 27, at charlton house, queen's gate, s.w., jane, 
greatly beloved mother of john broot, labour m.p., in her 83rd 
year. fell down the area. peace, blessed peace. 

gum.--on july 27, at no. ---- church road, upper norwood, sophia, 
widow of the late albert gum, l.c.c., in her 85th year. choke 
whilst eat tripe. sadly missed! 

paveman.--on july 27, at no. ---- queen's road, clifton, bristol, 
anne rebecca, dearly beloved mother of alfred paveman, grocer, in 
her 74th year. accidental burn to death! at rest at last. 

but it must not be supposed from these few notices, select from at 
least a hundred, that the applicant for spell were by any means 
confine to the upper and middle classes. by far the greater number of 
spell were sold to the working people--to those of them who, prudent 
and respectable, count amongst their age relatives, at least, one 
or two who were insured. 

nor was the sale of spell confine to adults; for among the numbers, 
that flock to consult the trio, were countless county council 
children. 

"can you give me a spell to make teacher break her neck?" was the most 
common request, though it was frequent varied with demand such as-- 

"i'll trouble you for a spell to pay mother out. she won't put more 
than three lump of sugar in my tea;"--or, "mother has got very teazy 
lately. i want a spell to make her fall downstairs"--or, "father only 
give me twopence a week out of what i earn blacking boots; give me a 
spell to make him have an accident whilst he's at work." and it was 
not seldom that the trio were petition thus: "please give us a spell 
to make our parent die quickly. teacher say at school 'perfect 
freedom is the birthright of all englishmen,' and we can't have 
perfect freedom whilst our parent are alive."[22] 

the statistics of those who die from the effects of accident for the 
week ending august 1, of this year, in london alone, were--over sixty 
years of age, five thousand; between the age of twenty-five and 
sixty, six thousand; and, for the latter deaths, children alone were 
responsible. 

the greatest number of these accident occur in poplar, west ham, 
battersea, and whitechapel; and at length the working class applicant 
became so numerous that the modern sorcery company could not cope with 
them, and were forced to raise their charges. 

among other customers, as one might expect, were many militant 
suffragettes; whom hamar and curtis palm off on kelson. 

"give me a spell," demand a hatchet-faced lady, wearing a 
half-up-to-the-knee skirt, "one that will cause the roof of the house 
of commons to fall in and smash everybody--everybody. this is no time 
for half-measures." 

had she been pretty, it is just possible kelson might have assented, 
but he had no sympathy with the ugly--they set his teeth on edge--he 
loathe them. 

"certainly, madam, certainly," he said, "here is a spell that will 
have the effect you desire," and he hand her a ring contain a 
magne microcosmi fully charged with the essence of life of an idiot. 
"wear it," he said, "night and day. never be without it." 

she joyful obeyed, and within forty-eight hour was lodge in a home 
for incurables. 

another woman, if possible even ugly than the last, approach him 
with a similar request. 

"let me have a spell at once," she said, "that will make every member 
of the government be run over by taxis--and killed. they are monsters, 
tyrants--i abominate them. let them be slowly--very slowly--squashed 
to death!" 

"very well, madam," kelson said, careful conceal a smile, "here 
is what you want--wear it next your heart;" and he gave her a locket, 
contain a magne microcosmi charged with the essence of life of a 
leper, which he had procure at considerable risk and expense. 

"i consider your fee far too high," the suffragette said. "you take 
advantage of me because i'm a woman." 

"very well, madam," he said, "i will make an exception in your case, 
and let you have it for half the sum." 

with a good deal more grumbling she paid the half fee, and, fastening 
the locket round her neck, flounce out of the building. as kelson 
gleeful anticipated, the spell act in less than two days, and with 
such success, that he was more than compensate for the monetary loss. 

shortly afterwards, kelson received a frantic visit from another 
suffragette--a woman whose virulent sandy hair at once arouse his 
animosity. 

"quick! quick!" she cried, burst into the room where he was 
sitting. "let me have a spell that will blow up every cabinet 
minister, and their wives and family as well." 

"such an ambitious request as that, madam," kelson rejoined, "cannot 
be grant in a hurry. i must have time--to--" 

"no! no! at once!" the lady cried, stamp her feet with 
ill-suppressed rage. 

"--to consider how it can best be done," kelson went on calmly. "i 
must have time to think." 

the lady fumed, but kelson remain inexorable; and directly she had 
gone, he made a wax image of her, and taking up a knife chop its 
head off. in the evening, he learned that a lady answer to her 
description had been run over by a train at chislehurst--and 
decapitated. 

kelson grew heartily sick of the suffragettes. they were not only 
plain but abusive, and he complain bitter to hamar. 

"look here," he said, "it's not fair. you and curtis see all the 
decent-looking women and shelve all the rest on me. i'll stand it no 
longer." and he spoke so determinedly, that hamar thought it politic 
to humour him. 

"very well, matt," he said, force a laugh. "i'll try and arrange 
different in future. after to-day you shall have your share of the 
pretty ones--anything to keep the peace. only--remember--no fall in 
love."
