chapter xxii 

the persecution of the martin 


hamar's one great idea on reach stage four was to utilize the 
torment as a means of get gladys. though he saw crowd of pretty 
girl every day, none appeal to him as she did--and the very 
difficulty of get her enhance her value and stimulate his 
passions. 

"i will give her one more chance," he said to himself, "and then if 
she won't have me i'll plague her to death." 

he went to the imperial, and passing himself off as her father to the 
new official at the stage-door entrance, was shown into the ante-room 
(which led to her dressing-room). it took a good deal to scare hamar, 
but he admitted afterwards that he did feel a trifle apprehensive 
whilst he await her advent; and his anticipation were fully 
realized. 

"why, father!" she began, as the door of her dressing-room swung open 
and she appear on the threshold, clad in a shimmer white dress, 
that intensify her fair style of beauty, "what bring you--" the 
smile on her face sudden die away. 

"you!" she cried, "how dare you! go! go at once! and if you dare come 
here again or attempt to molest me in any way, i'll prosecute you!" 

hamar, dumbfound at such an exhibition of wrath, slunk out of the 
room without utter a syllable. 

"the vixen," he mutter as soon as he found himself in the street. "a 
thousand cat in one! treat me like mud. jerusalem! i'll pay her 
out. and i'll lose no time about it either. she'll look different at 
me next time we meet." 

he hurried back to cockspur street and go into the laboratory, 
threw himself into a chair and--thought. 

that same evening at nine-thirty, in the interval between her first 
and second "going on," glady hasten to her dressing-room, and was 
prepare to partake of the light refreshments she had ordered, 
when--to her horror--she perceive crawl towards her, across the 
floor, a huge cockroach--a hideous black thing with spidery leg and 
long antennae that it waved, to and fro, in the air, as it advanced. 
it was at least double the size of any glady had hitherto seen, and 
her feelings can best be appreciate by those who fear such 
things--her blood ran cold, her flesh crawled, she sat glue to her 
chair, terrify to move, lest it should run after her. she screamed, 
and her dresser, startle out of her senses, came flying into the 
room. 

"what is it, madam? what is it?" she cried. 

glady pointed at the floor. 

"kill it!" she shrieked. "stamp on it! oh, quick, quick, it is coming 
towards me." 

but the moment the dresser caught sight of the cockroach, she sprang 
on a chair and wound her skirt round her. 

"oh, madam," she panted, "i daren't! i daren't go near it. i'm 
frightened out of my life, at beetles. and there's another of 
them"--and she pointed to the wainscoting--"and another! why, the 
room's full of them!" 

and so it was. everywhere glady look she saw beetle crawl 
towards her--dozens upon dozens, hundred upon hundreds--and all of 
the same monstrous size and ultra-horrible appearance. 

"look!" she screamed. "they are climb on to my clothes. one's got 
into my shoes, and another will be in them, in a second. there's 
another--crawling up my cloak--and another on my skirt. oh! oh!" and 
her cries, and those of the dresser, speedy brought a troop of 
actor and actress to the door. the instant, however, the cause of 
the alarm was ascertained, there were loud yells, and a wild stampede 
down the passages. the stage manager was called, but one glance at the 
floor was enough for him--he fled. and in the end three of the super 
had to be fetched. hot water, brooms, ashes, and quicklime were used, 
and although thousand of the cockroach were killed, thousand more 
came, and so hopeless did the task of get rid of them become, that 
the room eventually had to be vacated, and the crack under the door 
secure sealed. 

before glady left the theatre, she was call on the telephone. 

"who are you?" she asked. 

"hamar," came the reply, in insinuate tones. "how do you like the 
beetles? you'll never see the end of them till--" 

but glady rang off. 

on her return home something scuttle across the hall floor in front 
of her. she sprang back with a scream. it was a gigantic cockroach. 
the hall was full of them. she summon the servants, and they set to 
work to kill them. but they might as well have try to stop niagara, 
for as fast as they squash one battalion, another took its place. 
they came out of crack in the floor, from behind the wainscoting, 
from every conceivable place in the kitchens, and in a dense black 
ribbon some six inch broad, ascend the staircase. glady try to 
barricade her room against them, but it was of no avail. they came 
from under the boards of the floor and pour down the chimney. they 
swarm over the furniture, in the cupboards, chest of drawers, the 
washstand (where they kept continual fall into the water), in her 
clothes (her dressing-gown was cover with them), over the bed, and 
the climax was reach when they approach the chair she stood on. 
too fascinate with horror to move, she watch them crawl up to 
her. she was thus found by her father. he had come to her assistance 
in the very nick of time, and after lift her from the chair and 
taking her to a place, as yet safe from molestation, return to her 
room, where, with savage blows, smashing, equally, beetle and 
furniture, he remain till daybreak. 

with the first streak of dawn the beetle decamped, and the fray 
ended. the work of devastate had been colossal. corpse were strewn 
everywhere--and it took the combine household hours, before all 
evidence of the slaughter were obliterated. as for gladys, she had 
not slept all night and was a wreck. 

"i can never go through another night of it," she said to miss 
templeton. "do you think we shall ever get rid of the horrible 
things?" 

"we can but try, dear!" miss templeton said consolingly, and she 
accompany glady up to town, where they inquire of doctors, and 
chemists, and all sort of possible and impossible people; and 
return to kew laden with chemicals, and patent beetle destroyers. 
but though they try remedy by the score, none were of use, and the 
beetle repeated their performance of the preceding night. 

glady did not go to bed: surround with light candles, she sat on 
the top of a wardrobe till daybreak. the following morning the house 
was fumigate with sulphur; and people were told off to kill the 
cockroaches, as they made their escape out of doors. by this means an 
enormous number were killed; but at night they were just as bad as 
before. 

an engineer friend then suggest a freezing-machine. the temperature 
of the house was reduce to ten degree below zero; the pipes froze 
(and burst next day), the milk froze, the housemaid's toe and the 
cook's little finger of the left hand froze, everything froze; and 
presumable the beetle froze, for there was not one to be seen. 

however, it was quite impossible to resort again to this extreme 
measure. john martin had the most agonizing attack of lumbago. glady 
had neuralgia, and miss templeton--a slight touch of pleurisy. 

when glady reach the imperial that evening, she found that the 
staff had been battle with cockroach all day, and that they had at 
last succeed in get rid of them with a fumigate mixture of 
camphor, cocculus, sulphur, bezonia and assafoetida--suggested to them 
by a hindoo student. 

for the next week not a beetle was to be seen at the theatre nor at 
the cottage; and glady was beginning to hope that hamar had cease 
plague her (in despair of ever winning her), when the persecution 
sudden broke out again. 

she had been in bed about half an hour, and was fall into a gentle 
and much need sleep, when a tremendous rap at the wall, close to her 
head, awoke her with a start, and set her heart pulsate violently. 
thinking it must be some one on the landing, she got up and lit a 
candle. there was no one there. the moment she got into bed again, the 
rap was repeated, and it continued, at intervals, all night. this 
went on for a week, during which time glady was never once able to 
sleep. 

a brief respite ensued; but it was abrupt terminate one morning, 
when glady awoke feeling as if some big insect were attempt to 
penetrate her body. utter a shriek of terror, she whip the 
clothes from her, and sprang out of bed. miss templeton, who slept in 
the next room, came rush in, and they both saw an enormous insect, 
half beetle and half scorpion, dart under the pillow. john martin was 
fetched, but although he search everywhere, not a trace of the 
insect could be found. 

that night, directly glady got in bed and blew out the light, she 
heard a ticking sound on the sheets, and a huge insect with long hairy 
leg ran up her sleeve. her shriek brought the whole household to the 
room, but the insect was nowhere to be seen. 

she was thus plague for nearly a fortnight. one insect only--never a 
number, but only one, of prodigious size and terrify form--appeared 
to her in the least suspect places, _i.e._, on the dressing-table or 
chimney-piece, in her shoes, or pockets; crawl over her in the dark; 
and could never be caught. 

these perpetual frights, and consequent sleeplessness, wore glady 
out. she grew so ill that she had to give up acting, and go into a 
home to try the rest cure. 

hamar then communicate with her, through a third person, and offer 
to leave off torment her, if she would agree to be engaged to him. 

"i never will!" she said. 

"then i will never leave off persecute you," was his retort. 

but he was wary. he had no wish to kill her or to damage her looks--so 
he let her get well and remain thus for a brief space. when she was 
once again in full vigour, acting at the imperial, he recommence his 
unwelcome attentions. 

at first he confine his new plague to the servant at the cottage. 
the cook was one day turning out a drawer in the kitchen dresser, when 
she was horrify out of her senses to find squat there, a large, 
black toad, which stare most malevolent at her, and then sprang in 
her face. she shriek to the housemaid to help her kill it, but 
before a weapon could be got, the creature had bounce through an open 
window, and disappeared. 

after this incident the servant knew no peace. their bedclothes were 
thrown off them at night, their dress torn and bespattered with ink, 
their brush and comb thrown out of the window, and the water they 
pour out to wash in was sometimes quite black, sometimes full of a 
bright green sediment, and sometimes boiling, when it invariable 
cracked both the jug and basin. 

unable to stand these annoyance the servant left in a body. their 
successor fare the same, and worse. besides have to endure the 
above-named horrors, pebble were thrown through the windows, their 
chair were pull away as they were about to sit down (the cook, who 
was one of those upon whom this trick was played, thereby seriously 
injure her spine), and all sort of obstacle were placed on the 
stairs, so that those who ran down unwari trip over them and hurt 
themselves (two successive housemaid broke their legs, whilst another 
sprain her wrist). 

the meat, too, was a constant worry--it went so bad that enormous 
maggot crawl out of it by the thousand and cover the table and 
floor; and the milk, of which a large quantity was taken daily, 
"turned" in a very curious manner. after being deposited, in its usual 
place, in the pantry, it began to darken; first of all it became light 
blue, then deepen into an almost inky blackness, exhibit curious 
zigzag lines; and, lastly, the whole mass began to putrefy and to emit 
a stench so overpowering that every one in the house retched, and the 
whole place had to be disinfected. this occur day after day. 
nothing would stop it. the dairyman who supply the milk did all he 
could to counteract it. he had his dairy constant cleansed, he saw 
that the cattle had a change of food, he bought an entire new stock 
of dairy utensils, and no milk was ever sent to the cottage that he 
had not had careful analyzed. 

the trouble continue for three weeks, at the end of which period 
john martin received a telephone call from hamar. 

"hullo!" the latter said, "i guess you've had about enough of it by 
this time. wouldn't you like some sweet-smelling milk for a change, or 
do you prefer to go on till you all get typhoid? the remedy, you know, 
lie in your own hands. you've only to tell that daughter of yours to 
accept me, and i'll undertake all your trouble shall cease." 

"i'll see you hang first," john martin answered. 

"very well, then, you old mule," hamar shouted, "look out for 
yourself--and miss gladys." 




chapter xxiii 

love 


to bring about plague of insect hamar had resort to a very simple 
method. he had first of all made a wax image represent a 
cockroach--scorpion--centipede, or whatever other species came into 
his mind. then, place the image he had made in front of him, and 
repeating the prayer he had learned from the unknown, through the 
medium of mrs. anderson-waite's table, he had concentrated body, soul, 
and spirit on plague glady with the insect, which the image 
represented. when his concentration reach the highest degree, 
insect in their actual physical body were transport from the 
tropics;[23] but when he was unable to concentrate to the utmost, only 
the ethereal projection of the insect were obtainable; hence the 
hybrid--partly scorpion and partly beetle, that appear and 
disappear in gladys's bed and bedroom. 

to produce the rap on the wall of gladys's room, he had made a 
wax representation of a wall, and whilst concentrate to the very 
utmost, had struck it with his knuckles. 

the plague of the servant hamar had also accomplished by means of 
image and concentration. 

but in order to bewitch milk, he had been oblige to resort to other 
means. he had convert the mumia of an idiot into a magne 
microcosmi; and bribe the man who deliver the milk, he gave him 
instruction to soak the magne microcosmi, for a few minutes, in 
every portion that he left at the cottage.[24] 

at length hamar have fail to gain his object by plague glady 
and the servants, set about torment john martin. he made a wax 
image of the latter, and after pronounce the necessary prayer, stuck 
the image full of pins, crying out as he did so "john martin, i hate 
you. john martin, i curse you. john martin, a plague on you." and each 
time hamar stuck a pin in the image he had made of john martin, the 
real john martin felt an acute pain in the region of his body 
corresponding to that in which the pin was stuck. 

the doctor, who was call in, could make nothing of the malady, but, 
following the etiquette of the profession, cloak his ignorance with 
a look of profound wisdom, and the pronouncement that he would tell 
them, in a day or two, what was the matter. in the meanwhile, he found 
it necessary and politic to prescribe a non-committal mixture of chalk 
and rhubarb, which, although disguise under the usual fanciful 
pharmacopoeia appellation, did not, however, allay the pain. sharp, 
agonizing pricks, now on the neck now in the chest, now in the most 
sensitive part of the knee-cap, now under the toe-nail, now--most 
painful of all--under the finger-nail--continued to torment john 
martin, who, though as a rule fairly stoical, could not stand these 
attack with any degree of composure. he screamed, and swore, and 
cursed, until the whole household was terrified--and gladys, pretty 
nearly out of her mind. 

during a lull--an interval, wherein john martin enjoy a brief 
respite, the telephone bell rang. 

"hulloa," call a voice, "i'm hamar. haven't you had about enough of 
it? remember, you've only to say the word and i'll stop." 

"tell him i'll do nothing of the sort," john martin said, "that he'll 
never get the better of me this way." 

miss templeton gave the message, and hamar reply "wait! wait and 
see!" 

he then thrust wool, pins, horsenails, straw, needle and moss into 
the mouth of the image, and john martin had such frightful pains in 
his stomach that he went into convulsions; and, after an emetic had 
been given him, vomit up all the above-named articles, save the pin 
and needle which work their way out through his flesh, cause him 
the most exquisite tortures. 

gladys, have given up go to the theatre in order to be with her 
father during these attacks, now declared that she could no long 
bear to see him in such excruciating pain, whilst it was in her power 
to prevent it. 

"tell him," she said, "tell hamar you'll accept his conditions. don't 
think of me! i would rather do anything than see you suffer like 
this." 

"i can hold out a bit longer," he groaned, "at any rate i needn't give 
in yet." 

every now and then there came a respite--perhaps for several hours, 
perhaps for several days--then the torture recommenced. and always 
john martin steel himself to bear them. at last came the climax. 

hamar, infuriate that his efforts, so far, had prove fruitless, 
resolved, since time was pressing, to play his trump card and either 
win, or lose all. he rang up glady on the telephone. 

"my patience is exhausted," he said. "i'll give you one more chance, 
and one--only. agree to be engaged to me at once--or i'll smite your 
father with the most virulent form of cancer, and leave him to die." 

there was no question now in gladys's mind as to what she should do. 
of all things in the world, she dread cancer most, and after the 
many evidence hamar had given her of his skill in black magic, she 
did not doubt for one instant that he could, immediately he chose, 
carry out his threat. 

"i have decided," she said faintly, "to--to--give in." 

"you accept me, then?" hamar said. 

"y-yes!" 

"when may i see you?" 

"when you like." 

"then i'll come at once," hamar replied. "_au revoir._" 

but hamar, when he arrive at the cottage, did not realize any of the 
gleeful anticipation he had indulge in _en route_. glady was 
ill--so miss templeton informed him--at the same time beg him, if 
he really had any regard for miss martin, not to ask to see her for 
the next few days; and to this request hamar, seeing no alternative, 
was oblige to assent. 

shortly after he had gone, shiel davenport called, and found glady 
alone in the garden. 

"i've been told that your father is ill," he said, "and should like to 
hear better news of him. how is he?" 

"i think he's all right now," glady replied, "but he has suffer 
frightfully. indeed, we've all had a terrible time," and she told him 
what had happened. 

"then you've not been acting at the imperial lately?" shiel asked. 

"not for the past week," glady replied. "i couldn't leave father." 

"how has mr. bromley burnham got on without you?" shiel ask 
bitterly. 

"i don't understand you," glady said quietly. "i have an understudy, 
and from what i am told she has given every satisfaction. i have some 
news which i fear won't be altogether welcome to you." 

shiel turn a shade paler. "what is it?" he faltered. 

"i'm engaged to be married." 

for a few moments there was silence, and then shiel exclaim 
mechanical "engaged to be married! to whom?" 

"to leon hamar! i couldn't help it." and she explain the position. 

"but he'll never keep you to it," shiel said. "he couldn't be such a 
brute." 

"i'm afraid he will," glady replied. "he's shown pretty clearly that 
he's capable of anything. i've given him my promise--i must keep it." 

"then it's good-bye to all interest in life--for me," shiel said, with 
a gulp. "i've thought of no one but you since we first met. for 
you--in the hope of someday winning you, i've struggle on; i've 
reconcile myself to a bare existence. now i've lost you, i've lost 
everything. i hate life. i shall--" 

"you'll do nothing of the sort," glady interrupted, "unless you want 
me to regret ever have met you. i wonder that you say 'i've nothing 
to live for'--when we can still be friends; and when you can, at 
least, win my respect, by putt your shoulder to the wheel, and 
exert yourself to the utmost to get on." 

"and you--what about you?" 

"never mind me--i can well look after myself." 

"you'll live in hell," shiel cried, her eyes goad him to madness. 
"even though you may not care for me, i do not choose to stand quiet 
by, whilst you spend your life in purgatory. hamar has won you through 
some diabolical trickery, and if i can't thwart him in any other 
way--i'll kill him. he shan't marry you." 

"he will," glady sighed. "no one can stop him. he is omnipotent." 

apparently, gladys's statement was more or less true; and ninety-nine 
men out of a hundred, in the same circumstances as shiel, would have 
now recognize the hopeless of the situation. but shiel was 
abnormal. as he walk home from the cottage that evening he kept on 
repeating to himself "gladys is my goal. i want only gladys. i'll have 
only gladys." and have once made up his mind to get gladys, it 
seem to him, as if out of every obstacle, that lay between him and 
gladys, he could and would merely make a stepping-stone. "since," he 
argue to himself, "all's fair in love and war, i'll win glady 
through another woman." 

and he straightway telephone to lilian rosenberg to have tea with 
him. 

the latter had already made an engagement for the afternoon; but, all 
the same, she accept shiel's invitation. 

"will you do me a favour?" he asked. 

"if it is anything that lie in my power," she said. "what is it?" 

"i want you to find out how hamar works his spells. i ask you 
before?" 

"i know you did and i've not forgotten," lilian said, "but i have to 
be very careful. i've play the part of eavesdrop once or twice, 
and heard enough to confirm me in my suspicion that hamar is in touch 
with evil, occult powers. i've heard him pray aloud to them on more 
than one occasion, and i've also a shrewd idea he performs, at least, 
some of his spell by means of wax images. but why do you want to 
know?" 

"only curiosity. i am intense interested in the occult." 

"you don't want to start a rival show, do you?" lilian ask 
jestingly. 

"with a maximum capital of two pounds--and a minimum of knowledge!" 
shiel laughed. "hardly. i wish i could. i would offer you the post of 
manageress." 

"partner!" 

"well, partner, if you like. would you take it?" 

"perhaps!" she said, look at him with a sudden shyness. "what a 
pity you are not rich. can't you get a post that would bring you in 
about £200 a year for a start? i believe you really want something to 
stimulate you, to make you work in grim earnest--then you would 
succeed. there's grit in you--i love grit--but at present it's latent, 
it wants bring out." 

"you are very kind," shiel said, "but i'm afraid i'm a hopeless case, 
and, being such, have no business to be in your company. will you come 
to the theatre with me?" 

"the theatre! when you've no business to be in my company, and when it 
is as much as you can do to pay the rent of a back attic!" 

"oh, never mind that. i've had ticket given me. i've been do odd 
bit of journalism lately, and a dramatic critic i know has given me 
two stalls at the imperial!" 

"the imperial!" lilian rosenberg ejaculated. "that's where glady 
martin is acting, surely! i can't bear her!" 

"she's not the only person in the cast," shiel observe drily, "and 
the play's a good one! do come!" 

with a little more persuasion shiel gain her consent; and both he 
and she enjoy the play, or more correct speaking, the occasion, 
immensely. so long as glady was on the stage shiel's eyes never once 
left her; whilst throughout the performance lilian rosenberg saw only 
shiel, thought only of shiel. the interest she had taken in him, the 
interest she had so confident assert was only interest, had grown 
apace--had grown out of all recognition. it need only a fillip now 
to convert that interest into something warmer; and the fillip was not 
long in coming. 

shiel was seeing lilian home to her lodgings in margaret terrace, a 
turning off oakley street, when a man knock a woman down right in 
front of them. he was just the ordinary type of street ruffian--the 
whitewash english labourer--and the woman, have without doubt been 
serve by him in the same manner fifty times before, was probably well 
use to such treatment. but it was more than shiel, who had spent so 
much of his life where they treat women differently, could stand, and 
before lilian rosenberg had time to remonstrate, he had rush up to 
the prostrate woman, and was holding the man at bay. a scuffle now 
began, in which the woman, whom shiel had help to regain her feet, 
joined. both man and woman now attack shiel, who, place himself 
with his back against the railings, defend himself as best he could. 

the hour was late, there were no police about, and it seem only too 
probable that the fracas would end in a tragedy. the labourer was a 
burly fellow, short than shiel, but far broad and heavier, and any 
one could see at a glance that shiel stood no chance against him. 
lilian rosenberg, at her wits' end to know what to do, ran into oakley 
street, and as there was no one in sight, she made for the nearest 
light house and rang the bell furiously. a man came to the door, 
whom, unheede his expostulations, she caught by the arm and drag 
into the street. 

they arrive on the scene of action, just as the ruffian, break 
through shiel's guard, struck him a terrific blow on the forehead, 
which sent him reel against the railings. the newcomer (upon whom, 
both man and woman, seeing shiel incapacitated, instantly turned) 
would probably have share the same fate, had not the occupant of 
several of the neighbouring houses--amongst whom were some half-dozen 
athletic young men--roused by the noise, come out into the street, and 
the ruffian and his companion, seeing the odds were against them, 
decamped. 

shiel had not fully regain consciousness, when lilian rosenberg, 
regardless of propriety, led him into her sitting-room, bath his 
forehead, dose him with brandy, and making up a bed for him on the 
sofa, bade him rest there, till the morning. 

when he took his departure, he had quite recovered, and lilian 
rosenberg had, at last, realize that she love him.

chapter xxiv 

the subpoena 


a few days after the incident in margaret terrace, shiel had an 
inspiration. he was lunch with an old schoolfellow whom, quite by 
chance, he had met in lincoln's inn, have previous lost sight of 
him for many years, and the conversation, which had at first been 
confine to the old days, had gradual drift to what was ever 
uppermost in shiel's mind--namely, the modern sorcery company, _i.e._ 
hamar, kelson and curtis. 

"did you know," his friend remarked, "that the old statute, introduce 
in henry the fifth's reign against sorcery, has never been repealed?" 

"you don't mean to say so," shiel cry excitedly--a vague idea 
dawn on him. "tell me all about it." 

"well, that's rather a long order. for one thing, it impose all kind 
of penalty from capital punishment to fines. for another, it was in 
force up to the beginning of george the third's reign, when the last 
case of a person being burn for witchery in england occurred, and 
since then it has fallen into disuse." 

"could it be revived?" shiel asked, a sudden wild hope surge through 
him. 

"for all i know to the contrary, it could," his friend--who, by the 
way, was a barrister--replied. "of course no one could be burn or 
hang under it, but they might be fine or imprisoned." 

"then i wish to goodness you would file a case against the modern 
sorcery company! i'd move heaven and earth to get the scoundrel sent 
to prison!" and he told his friend how matter stood between glady 
and hamar. 

the barrister--whose name was sevenning--h.v. sevenning, of t.c.d. and 
cheltenham college renown--was keen interested. it was not only that 
his sense of chivalry was stirred, but he saw sport. consequently, the 
foregoing conversation result in a prosecution which, taking place 
some four week later, was report in the london herald as follows-- 

extraordinary charge heard at the old bailey. 

revival of an ancient statute. 

yesterday, at the old bailey, before his honour judge rosher, leon 
hamar, edward curtis and matthew kelson, of the modern sorcery 
company ltd., were indict under the 23rd of henry the fifth, c. 
15, which make it a capital offence to practise and administer 
spells. the case for the prosecution promise to be a lengthy one. 
an enormous number of witnesses, who are most anxious to make 
statements, will be called; and it is anticipate that much of 
their evidence will be of a most extraordinary nature. 

the accused are cite with have work spell to the 
injury--which injury, in many instances, has been fatal--of a vast 
number of people, representative of every rank in life. 

hilda, countess of ramsgate, who appear in heavy mourning, was 
the first witness called. in her evidence she stated, that it was 
owe to an advertisement she had seen in the _ladies' meadow_, 
that she had consult the modern sorcery company ltd., with the 
object of buy a spell to prevent her pekingese pet, brutus, 
catching cold on his liver. she had hope to see mr. kelson, as 
she had heard that he was more sympathetic, where ladies were 
concerned, than either mr. hamar or mr. curtis, but as mr. kelson 
was engaged, she had consult mr. edward curtis instead. the 
latter had given her a spell which he had assured her would have 
the desire effect, but directly she got home, her adore brutu 
develop melancholia, and die raving mad, after have bitten 
her child, who, by the way, had died, too. 

for the defence, gerald kirby, k.c., declared that the spell his 
client had given the countess was perfectly harmless; that it 
could not possibly have produce either melancholia or madness. 
"can any dependence," he said, "be placed on a woman, who 
obviously think more of her dog's death than that of her child!" 

the court was adjourn till to-morrow. 

in the following day's paper, the evidence for the prosecution was 
continued. lady marjorie tatler, who, in the weekly and illustrate 
journals, for no other reason than her reputed beauty, was reintroduce 
over and over again to the long-suffering public, was the first to 
step into the witness-box. 

she declared that edward curtis, instead of give her a spell to 
make florillda win the derby, had given her a diabolical something 
that had brought out spot all over her face, and that she had to 
undergo a most expensive treatment before they could be got rid 
of. 

in cross-examination, lady marjorie tatler admitted that she had 
ask edward curtis for a spell that would cause all the horse 
running in that particular race, save florillda, to be taken ill. 

for the defence, gerald kirby, k.c., explain that his client was 
so disgust at the immorality of lady marjorie's request, that he 
had purposely given her a spell that would have no effect upon a 
horse, and could not possibly bring out spot on her ladyship's 
face. "the spell edward curtis gave her," gerald kirby said, "was 
a mixture of hempsee and sago, flavour with violet powder, and 
my client instruct her ladyship to wear it next her heart." 
(loud laughter.) 

lady coralie mars, the next witness, who declared she had sought a 
spell to make the man, she was forced into marrying, fall into a 
trance, just before the marriage ceremony was to take place; and 
that, instead of bring this about, the spell edward curtis had 
sold her had cause her to have st. vitus's dance,--was adroit 
trap into admit that she had really want her fiancé 
smitten with paralysis. "a wish," gerald kirby announced, with a 
dramatic flourish of his hands, "that so arouse my client's 
indignation that, instead of give her the spell she wanted, he 
gave her one that would make her affiance husband more than ever 
hungry for the marriage hour to arrive. as for st. vitus's dance, 
would any woman, with an emotional and hysterical-nature, such as 
obviously was that of lady coralie mars, ever be free from such a 
complaint?" 

the hon. augusta mapple, who state that she had visit the 
modern sorcery company, for the purpose of obtain a spell to 
bring about a defeat of the government, by afflict the bulk of 
their supporter with such bilious attack as would necessitate 
their absence from the house, and that, instead of give her such 
a spell, edward curtis had given her one which had cause every 
member of her household to fall downstairs--admitted, under 
cross-examination, that she had ask for a spell that would make 
every supporter of the government in the house be sudden seize 
with tetanus. "a diabolical request, your lordship," gerald kirby 
said, "and one to which my client could not possibly accede. 
consequently, as a punishment for such cruelty, he sold her a 
spell that would result in her have a sharp attack of toothache. 
it could not possibly have produce any of the mishap she 
attribute to it." 

it is unnecessary to quote further. by far the greater number of these 
witnesses, on being cross-examined by mr. kirby, who defend with an 
ability that has rarely, if ever, been excelled, were made to confess 
that they had want the spell for a far more subtle and dangerous 
purpose than they had previous stated; admission which, of course, 
were highly prejudicial to the case for the prosecution. 

shiel lost hope. he had look forward to the trial with an excitement 
that almost border on frenzy. it was never out of his mind. he 
thought of it at meals, he thought of it at his work, he thought of it 
out of doors, and, when he went to bed, he dream of it. 

"i'll save you! i'll save you yet!" he wrote to gladys. "the trial can 
only result in one thing--the break up and imprison of the 
trio." 

but when he read the papers each day, and saw how, in almost every 
instance, evidence which ought to have been damning to the accused, 
had been twist into their favour, his heart sank. 

there was only one chance now--lilian rosenberg. she, of all the staff 
employ in the hall in cockspur street, was best acquaint with the 
_modus operandi_ of messrs. hamar, curtis and kelson. 

"we must get hold of that girl at all costs," h.v. seven remark 
to shiel. "you say you feel sure she likes you. work upon her feelings 
to show the firm up." 

"i don't much like the idea of it," shiel said, "but i suppose the end 
justify the means." 

"of course it does!" seven retorted. "it's your only chance of 
saving miss martin." 

acting on this suggestion, shiel approach lilian rosenberg on the 
subject. 

"what about the spells?" he ask her. "have you found out yet how 
hamar works them?" 

"i have only heard him mutter in his room again," she said, her 
cheek paling. "and--you will only laugh at me--i have seen queer 
shadow hover in his doorway and steal down the passages, 
shadow that have terrify me. i never knew what real fear was before 
i came to cockspur street, and for the past few week i have been 
almost too afraid to open my room door, for fear i should see 
something standing outside." 

"you have no doubt, i suppose, in your own mind, that the trio 
practise sorcery?" 

"i certainly think they are help in all they do by evil spirits." 

"do you approve of such proceedings?" 

"i don't think them right. i don't think we have any right to pry into 
the unknown. some day, undoubtedly, it will be given us to know, but 
until that day comes, we had far better leave it alone." 

"if you think like that," shiel said, "how can you reconcile yourself 
to working for these people?" 

"how can i help myself?" lilian rosenberg answered. "beggars can't be 
choosers. i am not responsible for what they do." 

"but supposing you knew they were about to commit a very heinous 
crime, wouldn't you feel it your duty to try and circumvent them?" 

"that depends," lilian rosenberg said. "if i could stop them without 
running any risk of lose my post, then i would probably try to stop 
them, but if stopping them meant being 'sacked,' i most certainly 
shouldn't. it isn't so easy to get post nowadays--especially good 
pay post like this. what do you take me for, a fool!" 

"then you don't believe in self-sacrifice, even for a friend?" shiel 
said slowly. 

"that depend on the degree of friendship," lilian replied. "if it 
were for some one i like very much, then--perhaps!" 

"is there any one you like very much! i, somehow, couldn't fancy you 
being very fond of any one." 

"couldn't you?" lilian said, with a faint laugh. "you don't think me 
capable of any deep affection. you forget, perhaps, that a woman 
doesn't always wear her heart on her sleeve." 

"i confess i don't understand women," shiel said, "and i had best come 
to the point at once. i happen to know that the trio--or at least one 
of the trio--is contemplate do something ultra-abominable--a 
cruel and shameful wrong, which i particularly wish to prevent. but i 
may not be able to do anything without your help! will you help me?" 

"how _can_ i?" lilian asked. 

"why, by finding out something which might be damning evidence against 
them, or by state your opinion in court. there is only one way of 
stay the trio from do this dastard thing, and that is by 
get this case, which is now being tried, to go against them." 

"well, and supposing, by some chance, the defendant should win! what 
would become of me?" 

"ah! that is where your self-sacrifice would come in! it would be a 
noble action." 

"how do this wrong, you say they are about to perpetrate, touch on 
you personally?" 

"it touch on some one with whom i am personally acquainted." 

"some one you like?" 

"yes!" 

"a relation?" 

"that i can't say." 

"then i can't help you. i am naturally inquisitive; curiosity is, as 
you know, a woman's privilege. you must tell me all." 

"it's for a friend, then!" 

"a man?" 

"no," shiel replied, "for a girl!" 

there was an emphatic silence, and then lilian rosenberg spoke. 

"have i ever heard you mention her?" 

"occasionally," shiel replied. 

there was silence again. then lilian rosenberg said slowly-- 

"you surely don't mean glady martin! i can think of no one else." 

"i do mean her!" shiel replied, drop his eyes. "she is to be 
coerce into marry hamar." 

"the silly fool!" lilian rosenberg said. "i would like to see any one 
try to coerce me. and it is to serve _her_ you want me to sacrifice 
myself." and she turn away in disgust. 

after this interview, lilian studious avoid shiel; and despairing, 
at length, of ever winning her over, shiel report his failure to 
h.v. sevenning. 

"we must subpoena her," said sevenning. 

"you'll never get her to speak that way," shiel said. "if once she has 
made up her mind not to do a thing, nothing will ever compel her." 

"i have heard that said of people before," h.v. seven reply 
dryly, "but it's wonderful what the witness-box can do; it loosen the 
most mulish tongue in a marvellous manner." 

"it wouldn't hers," shiel maintained. 

h.v. sevenning, however, thought he knew best--what lawyer doesn't? 
moreover, it was all part of the game--the great game of becoming 
notorious at all costs. he serve the subpoena. 

like most modern girls, lilian rosenberg was wholly selfish; and for 
this fault only her parent were to blame. she had been brought up 
with the one idea of pleasing herself, of saying and do exactly 
what she thought fit; and no one had ever thwart her. now, however, 
the unforeseen had happened. she was smitten with the grand passion, 
and confront for the first time in her life with the startle 
proposition of "self-sacrifice." she love shiel. she wouldn't marry 
him for the very simple reason he had no money--but that only add 
poignancy to the situation. she love him all the more. she knew shiel 
love glady martin. whether he could ever marry glady was another 
matter--but he love her all the same. and the proposition, that had 
been so abrupt thrust upon lilian rosenberg, was that she should 
sacrifice herself, not only to save glady martin from marry hamar, 
but to pave the way for shiel, supposing glady could reconcile 
herself to penury, to marry her himself. in other words she had been 
call upon to give up what was, at the moment, dearest to her in the 
world, and to court all the inconvenience and worry of being thrown 
out of employment--for if she gave evidence that would in any way tend 
to damage the firm of hamar, curtis & kelson, she would undoubted 
lose her post and, in all probability, never get another--at least not 
another as good--for the sake of a woman whom she did not know, but, 
nevertheless, hated. 

yet there was in her, as there is in almost every girl, however up to 
date, a chord that respond to the heroic. a short time back she 
would have scoff at the very thought of self-sacrifice; but now, she 
actually caught herself considering it. she kept on considering it, 
too, until the trial was well advanced, and had practically made up 
her mind to denounce the trio and go to the wall herself, when the 
subpoena was served. 




chapter xxv 

curtis in a new rÔle 


in an instant, lilian rosenberg had decided the course she would 
adopt. 

"what a disgust thing to do," she indignant exclaimed. "i 
wouldn't have believe it of shiel. the idea of force me to give 
evidence--of force me to save the situation for the sake of the 
woman he think he loves! i shan't do it!" 

and she prove as good as her word. apart from her importance as a 
witness, considerable interest attach to her on account of her 
appearance--she was infinite more attractive than any of the women 
who had hitherto appear in the witness-box--though many of them were 
so-called society beauties. 

"you were wrong," was the look which shiel read in h.v. sevenning's 
eyes, as lilian rosenberg took the oath. "she is on our side." 

but simple as shiel was in many ways, he knew women better than the 
lawyer, and the exceedingly sweet expression lilian rosenberg had 
assumed, and which he knew to be quite foreign to her, fill him with 
misgivings. nor was he mistaken. the evidence she gave was entire in 
favour of the trio. 

the case for the prosecution was concluded. for the defence, gerald 
kirby, k.c., resort to satire. he characterize the whole 
proceedings as the most absurd heard in any court for the past two 
centuries, and wondered, only, that it had been possible to procure a 
counsel for such a ridiculous prosecution. 

"even though," he remarked, "spirits such as have been specify by 
the prosecution do exist--which is extremely dubious--there has never 
yet been produce any reliable corroborative evidence respecting them, 
and the prosecution has wholly fail to prove, that it is through the 
medium of these spirits, that the modern sorcery company have work 
their spells. the marvellous feat that we have all seen perform in 
cockspur street have been accomplished--as the defendant have all 
along stated--through will--sheer will power and nothing else; and i 
intend produce evidence to show that the secret of the wonderful 
efficacy of all the charm and spell sold by the sorcery company, 
lie in will power also. whenever they have been consult with regard 
to the purchase of a spell, the firm have invariable pointed out 
this fact to the purchasers, careful explain at the same time 
that the rings, locket and other articles sold to them were merely to 
assist them in concentration. it is ridiculous to suppose that such 
trivial articles could have produced, of themselves, such calamity 
as the witness for the prosecution attribute to them. but, of 
course you did not believe the statement of such witnesses. how could 
you? how could you expect anything but falsehood from women who, upon 
cross-examination, had own that their object in obtain the spell 
was a far more dangerous object than they had at first led you to 
suppose. they sought spell that would do evil, and that evil was not 
accomplished. now, i ask you, if the firm work their spell through 
the instrumentality of evil spirits--for it is assured only evil 
spirits that are associate with sorcery--would not the spell they 
sold naturally have brought about the sinister result for which they 
were required? undoubted they would! and they fail to produce the 
desire effect, simply because their efficacy depended, not on spirit 
agency, but on human will power; which power one could only too 
plainly see the society ladies--who had witness for the 
prosecution--did not possess. 

"it may be asked, why the defendants, if they do not accomplish their 
spell through black magic, style themselves 'the sorcery company'--and 
so mislead the public? obviously they do so purely for advertisement. 
'the sorcery company' is an attractive title, a 'catchy' title, and 
for this reason, which is surely a legitimate one, since it is 
strict in accordance with the prevailing custom of advertisement--the 
firm of hamar, curtis and kelson adopt it. they did not expect--they 
were not so extraordinarily foolish as to expect--any one would take 
them literally. they thought--as you and i think--that sorcery cannot 
be taken seriously--that it is confine to fairy tales--and that, as a 
fairy tale, it is potent only in the nursery." 

this was the gist of counsel's speech for the defence. a number of 
witness then gave evidence for the defendants; and when the 
prosecute counsel rose, it was only too evident that he was pleading 
for a lost cause. the court with ill-concealed derision barely 
accord him a hearing. 

two hour later the _meteor_, always the first in the field when 
sensation crop up, head the first column of their front page with-- 

collapse of the sorcery case 
crush speech by gerald kirby, k.c. 
acquittal of the defendant 

"the judge"--so the _meteor_ reported--"expressed himself in absolute

was it too late to recover it?

give her waist an emphatic squeeze. "everybody will be all right

lilian could account for a great deal now. the uncanny feeling she had

"in diabetes, plant the mumia of the patient with a bignonia, and as

"in appendicitis, cover the stomach of the sick person with a piece of
